HEADLINE: Growing Peril on South African Roads: Wave of Carjackings

BYLINE: By ISABEL WILKERSON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: JOHANNESBURG, Sept. 22

 BODY:
During the bleakest days of apartheid, when schoolchildren were shot for picketing and funerals were used as a forum for protest, township militants often forced motorists into funeral processions all in the name of the struggle.
Now the struggle has apparently been won, but the practice of diverting motorists continues, this time in a context that has nothing to do with politics. 
 At suburban intersections and on township roads, robbers as young as 12, pointing AK-47's or 9-millimeter pistols, force motorists from their cars and shoot them if so inclined. The motorist is sometimes taken hostage and later dumped along a roadside, left to watch his what may represent his life savings roll off in a cloud of red dust to a chop shop for dismantling or out of the country for resale.
Everyone seems to know someone whose car has been hijacked if he himself has already not become a victim. Taxi drivers have been hijacked after dropping off their last passenger, truck drivers have been hijacked for their cargo of rice or cigarettes, and some people's B.M.W.'s have been hijacked, recovered and then hijacked again.
The crime wave has resulted in part from the years of apartheid, when guns streamed into the country for the resistance and people grew accustomed to violence.
"There is a contempt for the law because of a culture of violence," said Col. David Bruce, spokesman for the South African Police Service in the Johannesburg area. "Your massacres and your political violence have dissipated. Now, I believe that violent crime is the biggest problem in South Africa."
The violence threatens to overwhelm the new Government as it tries to attend to the basic tasks of providing jobs and shelter. As reality has set in that it will take years for the Government to bridge the disparities created by apartheid between rich and poor, black and white, crime has soared.
This year, carjackings have more than doubled in South Africa, rising in the Johannesburg area alone from 1,879 in the first six months of 1993 to 4,656 in the first six months of 1994. About 40 people have died in carjackings in Johannesburg this year and many others have been wounded.
A motorist is 10 times more likely to be carjacked in South Africa than in the United States, where the rate is one carjacking for every 6,200 cars, according to figures from the Federal Bureau of Investigation. In South Africa, one out of every 600 cars is hijacked, according to police and insurance company figures.
Every week, from the wealthy white suburbs north of here to the poorer black neighborhoods of Soweto, 200 vehicles are taken from their drivers at gunpoint.
It happened to Mapula Plessy, a real estate agent, when she stopped for a traffic light in Soweto during rush hour one afternoon. Two young men came up to her B.M.W. with a 9-millimeter pistol.
"The keys, sisi," she recalled one of them saying as he used the township term for 'sister.
The motorists behind her, she said, nervously sped around her and never looked back.
"I was left with nothing," she said. "I felt paralyzed. People were standing there and did nothing."
It happened twice to Thabo Setaka, a truck driver. Once he was parked by the side of the road to finish some paperwork on the 450 bales of rice he was hauling. Two robbers came up to him with automatic weapons, took the truck and the rice and left him by the highway.
The second time he and his crew thought they were being pulled over by plainclothes police officers doing cargo checks. The men were robbers instead and ordered them into another car to be dumped far from the carjacking site.
Mr. Setaka looked closely at their faces, praying that they were strangers. "I thought, "Please, my God, I don't want to know them,' " he said. "If they know you, they will kill you. If you can recognize them, you are supposed to die."
He was spared. But when the police came in pursuit of the robbers, a co-worker was killed in the crossfire.
What is in all the vehicles is quick money in a country where nearly half the people are unemployed and where the economy is trying to shake a recession. With the involvement of body shops and gangs, auto theft has become such an organized business here that some cars and parts are stolen to order, police and insurance officials say.
Because of decades of isolation and protectionism that added heavy tariffs on imports to South Africa, car prices are wildly inflated; some cost more than the average house. In the great majority of cases, no one is arrested and the car is never found. That is partly because blacks, the primary victims of carjackings, are wary of going to the police out of fear of reprisal from the carjacker or suspicion of the police after decades of abuse under apartheid.
The threat of a carjacking figures into people's everyday routines now. Some people no longer stop at traffic lights, especially at night. They just pause and move on. Some are careful not to drive with a full tank of gas, hoping that if the robbers find that the tank is near empty, maybe they will let them pass.
Many insurance companies refuse coverage unless a car has an alarm system and some reject the priciest B.M.W.'s altogether. Insurance rates have risen 60 percent for some cars in the last year because of the increase in claims. Because of the cost, 70 percent of cars here have no insurance at all, said Damon Deavin, executive manager of the Aegis Insurance Group here.
Fear has prompted motorists to seek ever more advanced means of protecting their cars, but robbers immediately find ways to decode or sidestep them. Nearly every new car includes a remote control alarm system and a device that prevents a would-be robber and sometimes even the hapless motorist from starting the engine unless the security system is overridden.
"To make sure the driver doesn't use the cutoff device, they take the driver hostage," Mr. Deavin said.
Some people like Timothy Jiyana have changed their driving habits now, riding only in certain kinds of cars, the more downscale and beat up the better.
The other day a friend wanted Mr. Jiyana to help haul some equipment in a pickup truck like the one that was seized from him at gunpoint early one morning in Soweto. Mr. Jiyana said he preferred to ride in his old hatchback, which he figured nobody would want.
"I told him, "I will not ride with you,' " Mr. Jiyana said. " 'I will follow you. So if they take your buggy, I can give you a lift in my car.' "

GRAPHIC: Photo: Members of the Johannesburg flying squad, a special unit of the South African Police Service, arresting a suspect after a car hijacking. (Henner Frankenfeld for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: President's Words: Fight Between Hope and Fear

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from the speech given yesterday by President Clinton to the United Nations General Assembly, as transcribed by The Associated Press:
Today I am proposing a first step toward the eventual elimination of a less visible but still deadly threat -- the world's 85 million anti-personnel land mines -- one for every 50 people on the face of the earth. 
 I ask all nations to join with us and conclude an agreement to reduce the number and availability of those mines. Ridding the world of those often hidden weapons will help to save the lives of tens of thousands of men and women and innocent children in the years to come.. . .
 
On Haiti
 Never before has the United Nations been in a better position to achieve the democratic goals of our founders. The end of the Cold War has freed us from decades of paralyzing divisions, and we all know that multilateral cooperation is not only necessary to address the new threats we face, but possible to succeed.
The efforts we have undertaken together in Haiti are a prime example. Under the sponsorship of the United Nations, American troops, now being joined by the personnel of an ever-growing international coalition of over two dozen nations, are giving the people of Haiti their chance at freedom.
Creative diplomacy, the influence of economic power, the credible threat of military force -- all have contributed to this moment of opportunity.
Essential civil order will be restored. Human rights violations will be curbed. The first refugees are returning within hours on this day.
The military leaders will step down, the democratic government will be restored. President Aristide will return. The multinational mission will turn its responsibilities over to the United Nations mission, which will remain in Haiti throughout 1995, until a new President is elected.
During this time, a multinational development effort will make available more than $1 billion to begin helping the Haitians to rebuild their country.
In the spirit of reconciliation and reconstruction, President Aristide yesterday called for the immediate easing of sanctions so that the work of rebuilding can begin immediately.
Accordingly, I intend to act expeditiously within the Security Council Resolutions 917 and 940 to enable us to restore health care, water and electrical services, construction materials for humanitarian efforts, and communications, agricultural and educational materials.
Today, I am also announcing that the United States will suspend all unilateral sanctions against Haiti except those that affect the military leaders and their immediate supporters.
This will include regularly scheduled air flights when the airport becomes available, financial transactions and travel restrictions. I urge all other nations to do the same.
In Haiti, the United States has demonstrated that it would lead a multinational force when our interests are plain, when the cause is right, when the mission is achievable and the nations of the world stand with us.
But Haiti's people will have to muster the strength and the patience to travel the road of freedom. They have to do this for themselves.
Every new democratic nation is fragile, but we will see the day when the people of Haiti fulfill their aspirations for liberty and when they are once again making genuine economic progress.
 
On Bosnia
 United Nations actions in Bosnia, as those in Haiti, demonstrate that progress can be made when a coalition backs up diplomacy with military power.
For the first time ever, NATO has taken, since we met last year, military actions beyond the territory of its members. The threat of NATO air power helped to establish the exclusion zone around Sarajevo and to end the Bosnian Serb spring offensive against Gorazde.
And NATO's February ultimatum boosted our mediation efforts, which helped to end the war between the Bosnian Government and the Bosnian Croats and forged a federation between those two communities.
The situation in Bosnia, to that extent, has improved, but in recent weeks, the situation around Sarajevo has deteriorated substantially and Sarajevo once again faces the prospect of strangulation.
A new resolve by the United Nations to enforce its resolutions is now necessary to save Sarajevo, and NATO stands ready to act.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasury Issues' Prices Are Mixed

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities were mixed yesterday in a quiet session as the market awaited the outcome of a meeting that will determine the Federal Reserve's monetary strategy for the next few months.
For weeks, dealers and investors have been split over the timing of another increase in interest rates by the Fed, which has already raised rates five times this year. Some expect a move to increase short-term rates to take place during a meeting of the Fed's policy-making committee that starts today, while others lean toward an action after the November meeting, which would allow the Fed time to assess the most recent economic data to see if the last few increases have worked their way through the economy. 
 The price of the 30-year bond slipped 1/32 , to 9620/32 , for a yield of 7.79 percent, unchanged from Friday's close. Shorter-maturity bill rates were mixed. At yesterday's auction of new three- and six-month bills, rates surged to their highest level in three years.
James Hale, a fixed-income analyst with MMS International, a market research and analysis firm, said the Fed would move to raise rates soon despite the past track record. "Time and time again in the past they have shown that they err on the side of caution," he said.
The market sees the need for another tightening to prevent an acceleration in inflation, he said.
"The data we've seen over the last two months has provided two reasons for a rate hike," Mr. Hale said. "First there are the signs of inflation pressures building, not just in commodities but also at the producer price levels. And they are certainly moving in the direction of the consumer level. It's only a matter of time."
The second reason, he said, is that economic growth is nearing a point were wage pressures will develop.
But there are those like Laura D'Andrea Tyson, chairwoman of the Council of Economic Advisers, who called the market's fear of inflation unwarranted. Before a meeting of the National Association of Business Economists yesterday, she said "today the economy is doing very well."
"In the next two to three years, the economy will continued to have a moderate expansion with modest inflation," she said. "Fears of a large uptick in inflation are unwarranted."
The five rate increases by the Fed have raised short-term rates 125 basis points. A basis is one-hundredth of 1 percent. By historical standards, an increase of 125 basis points is not all that much, so another tightening would not endanger the economy. "First they took the foot off the gas now, and now we see the need for them to start tapping the brake," Mr. Hale said.
Some traders note that if the Fed does not change policy this week then the short end of the market will likely benefit as a safe haven.
Yesterday's report on existing- home sales in August had a negligible impact on the bond market. Existing-home sales fell 1.8 percent in August to 3.90 million, consistent with the declining trend seen in conjunction with the rise in interest rates.
The market is also bracing for the auction of new two- and five-year notes today and tomorrow.
The following are the results of yesterday's auction of three- and six-month bills:

HEADLINE: MISSION TO HAITI: POLICY;
Lifting Sanctions on Haiti Will Take Up to 2 Weeks

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
Administration officials said that the lifting of sanctions on Haiti, announced today by President Clinton, would not go into effect immediately. It would take several days to lift the ban on most financial transactions with Haiti, and at least a week or two before scheduled flights are resumed to Port-au-Prince, they said.
In an address today to the United Nations General Assembly, the President said that one part of the unilateral sanctions would not be lifted: the freeze on the assets of Haiti's military officer corps and their close financial supporters. The freeze on those 600 individuals would remain at least until the exiled President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide, is restored to power, State Department officials said. 
 Within the next few days, the Treasury Department is scheduled to issue new regulations that will officially remove the ban on most financial transactions as well as the freeze on assets owned by Haitians, except for the targeted list of 600.
The Administration plans to ease the ceiling that limits to $50 a month the amount that American residents can send to their relatives and friends in Haiti. The Administration will also ease its restrictions on granting travel visas to Haitians.
President Clinton announced the tough unilateral sanctions on June 10 as part of an effort to press Haiti's military leaders to cede power.
Before flights are resumed, the American military must first declare the Port-au-Prince airport safe. The United States will also confer with France, Canada and the Netherlands, all of which also suspended flights to Haiti.
Tim Smith, a spokesman for American Airlines, the American carrier that traditionally had scheduled flights to Haiti, said his airline would also wait for a green light from the Federal Aviation Administration before it resumed its flights.
"We have concerns involving stability and safety in Haiti for the sake of our employees, our equipment and our passengers," Mr. Smith said. He said that in the face of a volatile situation like the American military occupation, his airline wants information about the physical condition of the airport, the security situation in the area and the availability of personnel to provide navigation assistance, weather information and other services.
Mr. Smith said it would be at least several more weeks before American Airlines resumed its flights to and from Haiti. The airline used to have daily flights of a 266-seat, A-300 Airbus from New York and Miami to Port-au-Prince.
Under the United Nations trade embargo, the international sanctions are to remain in force until the military leaders surrender power and President Aristide returns.
Nonetheless, President Clinton said today that his Administration would, with Father Aristide's approval, begin shipping more oil, food, medical supplies, and telecommunications equipment into Haiti. The Administration would use an exemption in the United Nations embargo resolution that allows the United States and the multinational military force in Haiti to provide what is necessary to create a safe and stable environment to pave the way for President Aristide's return.
Thus, the United States will import more oil not just for the American troops, but to power Haitian hospitals and power stations that have had only enough fuel to run three hours a day.
According to a White House statement, this will "increase security and improve the general daily life of Haitians in metropolitan areas."
To improve the lot of Haitians, the United States will also bring in more educational materials, agricultural supplies and construction and transportation materials to help relief efforts. As soon as the airports reopen, the United States will also grant permission for charter flights.

HEADLINE: Spanish Economy Picking Up, but Many People Still Suffer

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MADRID

 BODY:
After suffering the ravages of the deep European recession, the Spanish economy is perking up and should grow at a reasonable rate over the next several years. Exports are surging. Corporate profits are improving. Inflation is under control.
But none of that means much to Soledad Arevalo Sastre. She recently lost her job as a history and geography teacher, has little hope of finding a new one and is living on unemployment insurance. She does not expect the economic recovery to change the outlook for her or for most of the 3.7 million other Spaniards who are out of work, a staggering 24.3 percent of the work force, nearly four times the current rate of 6.1 percent in the United States. 
 "I have absolutely no idea what will happen to them or to me and my life," said Miss Arevalo, a 35-year-old resident of Madrid. "The world is changing too fast."
Across Western Europe the recovery is gathering steam, bringing an end to one of the bleakest economic periods in the region since World War II. But for the European Union's 20 million unemployed people -- 10.8 percent of the work force -- the rebound is only confirming what many of them had feared and what economists had been predicting: that their plight will not end with the recession.
Instead, the jobless are beginning to understand what analysts mean by "structural" unemployment, the kind that will not be swept away by a cyclical economic improvement. Like Miss Arevalo, they are at risk of being caught permanently between an array of problems that are much discussed but difficult to solve.
There is an ever-intensifying worldwide industrial competition that has been forcing companies to pare their work forces. There is the high cost of employing workers in Europe, where businesses foot much of the bill for generous social welfare programs. And there are Europe's rigid labor laws, which inhibit business expansion by making it expensive and difficult for employers to hire and dismiss workers.
"Economic growth will play a part in reducing unemployment," said a recent report from the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development, a research group based in Paris that is financed by the largest industrial democracies. "But beyond the cyclical component of unemployment is a structural element that persists even into recovery. This is harder to reduce and is even more troubling."
Nowhere is the problem more troubling than in Spain, and nowhere are the difficulties of solving it more clear. Although the figure appears to be inflated to an unknown extent by fraudulent claims from people who are working in undocumented jobs, Spain's jobless rate is by far Western Europe's highest. The country's Socialist-led Government has tried to address the problem with an ambitious package of new labor laws that went into effect earlier this year. Among other changes, the laws make it easier for employers to hire temporary workers and to pay them, in some cases, less than the minimum wage.
Economists said the new laws appeared to be spurring the creation of jobs. But they said that some of the jobs might be coming at the expense of permanent, better-paid employment for other workers. In any case, they said, Spain has so far shown little ability to create or attract new jobs in industries with bright futures like technology.
"Although the labor market reforms are helping the Spanish economy to create more jobs with low growth, unemployment rates are going to stay high for some time," said Sonsoles Gallego Herrero, an economist with Analistas Financerios Internacionales, a consulting firm in Madrid. "Things will improve next year, but not substantially."
A recent forecast by NatWest Markets, an investment firm based in London, projected that Spain's unemployment rate would decline only marginally, to 23.9 percent next year and 23.3 percent in 1996.
While the jobless rates in the rest of Europe are not so high, the projected improvement over the next few years is equally slight. According to NatWest, France's unemployment rate will decline to 11.9 percent in 1996 from its average level for this year of 12.5 percent.
Over the same period, Italy's rate will drop to 10.7 percent from 10.9 percent. In western Germany, joblessness is projected to decline from 8.3 percent this year to 7.6 percent in 1996.
"The situation in this country is very bad," said Maria Carmen Conde, a 27-year-old Madrid resident who has been out of work for a year despite having two years of college and experience in a variety of secretarial and public relations jobs.
What jobs there are, she said, are going to people 25 and under who qualify for special programs under the new labor laws. She said she has little hope of being able to support herself and her 6-year-old daughter independently, a realization that she said was devastating.
"I feel very, very disillusioned and deceived," she said.
Her 27-year-old boyfriend, Pablo Ferrari, who recently lost his job as a restaurant manager, is so pessimistic that he is thinking of moving to New York City. There, the unemployment rate of 8.5 percent, while higher than the national average of 6.1 percent, would be much lower than Spain's.
Europe has remained calm at least in part, economists said, because unemployment benefits are relatively generous -- they start at $470 a month in Spain for a single person and go up to more than double that for a worker with two or more children -- and generally continue, in some form, for as long as workers remain jobless.
What progress there has been in battling unemployment has come largely as a result of deregulating the labor force. The need for such steps -- once seen as unacceptably Thatcherite on the Continent -- has gained widespread acceptance, even among Socialists such as Prime Minister Felipe Gonzalez of Spain, who pushed a package of changes through Parliament last year despite strong opposition from his party's left wing and from the country's trade unions.
Under the new system, companies can hire workers up the age of 25 into an "apprentice" program. The jobs can last for up to three years. Employers pay no social security taxes on them, a savings of hundreds of dollars a month a job, and can pay workers as little as $312 a month -- 70 percent of the minimum wage.
The laws have also been changed to make it easier for employers to hire part-time workers, a practice that the old laws made difficult. Moreover, the new system seeks to make it less risky for employers to hire permanent, full-time workers by reducing the payments companies must make to any workers they lay off, from a maximum of five years' salary to a maximum of one year's salary.
Employers want more.
"In my opinion and that of the employers' association, the reforms are necessary but not complete," said Felipe Manzano Sanz, a labor relations specialist with the Spanish Confederation of Employers' Organizations.
He said that among other changes, the country should scale back its collective bargaining system, in which centralized union contracts are imposed across entire industries, leaving little room for the needs of individual employers.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
$400-an-Ounce Price of Gold Is Expected to Prevail Soon

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
Inflation worries and a rebounding global economy have lifted demand for gold, and the experts are confident that the new price level of $400 an ounce will soon prevail.
On New York's Commodity Exchange, gold for delivery in December yesterday breached the magic $400 level for a second consecutive trading session, retreating to close at $397.80 an ounce. 
 On Friday, the December contract also hit $400 an ounce before closing at slightly less than the mark. It was the first time gold had broken through that barrier since August 1993.
Analysts yesterday remained optimistic that gold, after gaining more than $10 since the beginning of September, would stay close to $400 in the near term and would eventually work its way higher, partly because of fears of inflation after the release of strong United States economic data recently, leading nervous investors to shift money out of stocks and bonds and into gold and cash.
"The market has built a nice head of steam," said Andre DiMino, trading strategist for Apostle Capital Management.
Morgan Stanley doubled the share of gold in its global model investment portfolio to 4 percent yesterday, citing expectations that stock prices would languish or decline.
Running counter to inflationary expectations, however, the Commodity Research Bureau index gave up 0.49 point, closing at 232.24, pushed down by declines in coffee, oil and other contracts.
Orange juice prices soared to four-month highs as the drought searing Brazil's coffee plantations began to hit its orange groves as well.
The Agriculture Department, in a report from its officer in Sao Paulo, Brazil, lowered its estimate of Brazil's 1994 orange crop slightly to 14.3 million metric tons. Local authorities complained that the dryness had created problems for processors.
On the New York Cotton Exchange, the November frozen orange juice contract rose 4.4 cents, to 99.35 cents a pound, the highest since June 3. At one point, the contract reached the key $1-a-pound level.
Coffee prices retreated. December coffee fell 3.05 cents, to 226.60 cents a pound, on the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange in New York. Light rain in Brazil's coffee regions over the weekend prompted selling.
On the New York Mercantile Exchange, November crude oil fell 16 cents, to $17.67 a barrel, after failing to rally past $17.99.

HEADLINE: An Abundance of Passion At Public Smoking Debate

BYLINE: By JONATHAN P. HICKS 

 BODY:
Some spoke passionately about the danger of secondhand smoke and pleaded for measures to restrict smoking. Others talked with equal fervor about smokers' rights and the economic threat to their businesses from any move to further curtail smoking in restaurants and public places.
The City Council held a full day of often emotional hearings yesterday on a bill that would effectively ban smoking in virtually all public places in New York City. And a wide array of New Yorkers -- and even people from other cities -- descended on City Hall in droves to testify before the Council's Health Committee, from the powerful tobacco industry, restaurant owners and surgeons to ministers, public officials and a beauty queen. 
 Before the hearing began, people stood in a line that wrapped around City Hall, waiting to enter the building to testify.
Many used their time before the committee to engage in tirades in general terms about their opposition or support of antismoking measures. But some offered specific recommendations on how to improve the bill, which was submitted in March by Speaker Peter F. Vallone and co-sponsored by 31 more of the Council's 51 members.
The bill, in its current form, would ban smoking in all restaurants, retail stores, business establishments, concert halls and auditoriums, stadiums and convention centers. The measure has been fiercely denounced by the tobacco industry and some restaurant owners and hotel officials. Opponents have complained that the bill would inflict great hardship on businesses, which could lose the patronage of smokers.
The hearing came one day after the Philip Morris Companies, the world's largest cigarette maker, said it would consider moving its headquarters out of New York City if the legislation passed. And in yesterday's hearing, several Council members angrily denounced Philip Morris for what they characterized as the ultimate in hardfisted lobbying techniques.
"I don't know if that's how you lobby in the tobacco country of North Carolina," said Anthony D. Weiner, a Councilman from Brooklyn, speaking to an official of the Tobacco Institute, the industry trade association. "But New Yorkers don't like to be threatened. What does not add to the debate is a company saying that 'if we don't get our way, we're going to take our ball and go home.' Well, you know, my view is, go home now."
Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani told reporters at an unrelated ceremony at City Hall yesterday that he was sympathetic to the complaints from restaurant owners of the economic risk of legislation limiting smoking. But he said he generally supports smoking restrictions and that the savings in health costs would ultimately offset any reduction in business.
About Philip Morris considering leaving the city, Mr. Giuliani said, "That's not the best way to carry out a public policy debate." He added, "If they leave, they leave. If they don't leave, they don't leave. We should be looking at the long-term impact."
By yesterday afternoon, Philip Morris had released a statement saying that the company "never threatened to leave New York City." The statement added, "We said it was an option we would consider if the proposed legislation passed in its current form." The company did not testify at the hearing but sent officials to monitor the proceedings.
The Council's ornate chamber, where the hearing was held, was filled to capacity with more than 350 people. The crowd seemed to be evenly divided between supporters and opponents of the bill with much of the testimony greeted with either wild applause or thunderous boos.
Council officials initially suggested that the Health Committee might vote on the bill yesterday. But the sheer volume of people who came to testify -- more than 150 witnesses -- meant that it will be some time before a vote will occur. Richard A. Riley, a spokesman for Mr. Vallone, said the volume of witnesses suggested that the committee would likely conduct another hearing and that the committee would spend time considering the various suggestions. He said that a vote by the Council might not occur until November and that Mr. Vallone would like to see a bill enacted by the full Council by the end of the year.
While most of those who supported the bill proposed little in terms of revisions, some opponents of the legislation suggested a few changes. For example, Fred G. Sampson, president of the New York State Restaurant Association, suggested amending the bill so that restaurants could be allowed to devote just 25 percent of the seats for smoking areas. Under the current smoking regulations, adopted in 1988, restaurants can devote 50 percent of their space for smokers.
Joseph E. Spinnato, president of the Hotel Association of New York City, suggested that the bill require seating areas be arranged so that nonsmokers do not have to walk through smoking sections, and that it establish a minimum number of times air filters must be changed. He also suggested increasing the fines for violations of maintenance on ventilation equipment.
Among those who testified in favor of the smoking restrictions were cancer patients, doctors and several public officials, including State Attorney General G. Oliver Koppell and Comptroller Alan G. Hevesi. Patrick Reynolds, the grandson of the founder of the R. J. Reynolds Tobacco Company and an ardent antismoking crusader, said the measure would help save lives.
Another supporter of the measure was Kelly Gray Johnson, Miss Greater New York City. She said that as an aspiring singer who has worked in restaurants that serve smokers, "I can attest to the thousands of dollars in medical bills I have paid because of respiratory infections from being around smokers."
While saying that the Council was genuinely interested in hearing from all parties, Mr. Vallone made it clear in his opening statement that he was deeply committed to a bill that would severely restrict smoking in public places.
"I know that this bill will be unpopular with some people," Mr. Vallone said. "I also know that smokers feel that they have a constitutional right to smoke. But I strongly believe that they do not have a right to make others breathe that smoke."

HEADLINE: Restoration of Long Island Sound Is Pledged

BYLINE: By JOHN T. McQUISTON 

 BODY:
The top Federal environmental official came to Long Island Sound yesterday with pledges that its cleanup is a priority -- as long as Congress comes through with money to do the job.
"Long Island Sound is a national treasure and one of the nation's most important waterways," said Carol M. Browner, administrator of the United States Environmental Protection Agency, who joined Gov. Mario M. Cuomo of New York and Eunice S. Groark, the Lieutenant Governor of Connecticut, in signing a joint agreement to restore and protect the Sound, estimated to cost $7 billion over 20 years. 
 The agreement, which contains no new Federal or state money, calls for the cleanup of the Sound through existing programs, or through new legislation.
At the signing, on the campus of the State University Maritime College on the Throgs Neck peninsula in the Bronx, overlooking the confluence of the East River and the Long Island Sound, cautions were sounded both about the price of cleaning up the sound and the price of doing nothing.
"It's going to be very, very expensive, and very hard to do," said the Governor, who is up for re-election this fall, cautioning that changes in the political lineup could make money even harder to get.
But Senator Joseph I. Lieberman, a Connecticut Democrat who is also running for re-election this fall, noted the Sound's benefit to the region's economy -- in excess of $5 billion annually.
The agreement, based on nearly a decade of studies, identifies six problems that Federal and state environmentalists say merit special attention, including low levels of oxygen, toxic and pathogen contamination and floating debris.
Mrs. Browner said that low oxygen level, or hypoxia, was the most critical threat to the Sound, which is approximately 110 miles long and about 21 miles across at its widest point.
Hypoxia occurs when marine plants grow profusely, fed by nitrogen. When the plants die and decompose, they consume oxygen, harming other plants and shellfish while repelling fin fish. In the Sound, the nitrogen comes from sewage plants ringing the shore in Connecticut, the Bronx and Westchester County and on Long Island.
The agreement calls for New York and Connecticut to take steps by the end of 1995 to reduce nitrogen levels by 18 percent in the next six years, at an estimated cost of $120 million.
Mrs. Browner said that because the Administration was seeking changes in the Clean Water Act, it was unclear what money would be available for states to spend on sewage treatment beyond projects already underway.
She also noted that Congress had approved $1.3 billion for the Safe Drinking Water Act, but had not yet determined how to allocate the funds.

HEADLINE: Right to Life Nominee Looks Beyond Albany;
Diverting Votes as a National Message

BYLINE: By FRANCIS X. CLINES, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: DEER PARK, L.I., Sept. 22

 BODY:
For all the ballot-proven fervor of the Right to Life Party in state elections, its dedication this gubernatorial year is extra heated and targeted to teach a larger national lesson to Republicans intent on retreating strategically from the politics of the abortion issue.
The party's dapper, affable gubernatorial candidate, Robert T. Walsh, is incensed not just by the fact of legalized abortion but also by the party's former favorite son, Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato. Right to Life leaders say the Senator epitomizes this retreat in his masterminding the nomination of State Senator George E. Pataki as the Republican gubernatorial candidate. 
 "Al who?" said Mr. Walsh, 49, a bank management and securities specialist, as he strategized in his suburban home here with party loyalists who labored hard for Mr. D'Amato's re-election two years ago.
The Right to Life Party's goal is to achieve, if not victory, then the 3 or 4 percent of the vote that political handicappers think is attainable in this unsettled year. That might be enough, if the race is tight, to block victory by Senator Pataki, a politician who denies the Right To Life Party's charge that the further into statewide politics he has ventured, the more he has hedged his earlier abortion criticism.
"Mario's doing all he can to elevate this guy Walsh and his name identity, get him into the debates, hoping he'll siphon off the A.B.C. voters -- anyone but Cuomo," said Maureen Connelly, a political consultant, noting the needs of Gov. Mario M. Cuomo, the 12-year incumbent.
Ms. Connelly, who has no candidate in the race, stressed this strategy might only work if Mr. Cuomo can narrow what she estimates is Mr. Pataki's 10-point lead.
Senator Pataki, who previously voted against Medicaid financing of abortion, has said that he would permit such financing as Governor and that his position has consistently been that "abortion is a woman's decision, period."
He is being lambasted as "multiple choice Pataki" by Mr. Walsh, a first-time candidate running with the 16-year-old party's usual shoestring budget of several thousand dollars plus highly dedicated volunteer help.
Mr. Walsh, the managing director of the American Institute of Banking, one of the industry's main educational arms, grew up poor as one of 12 children in a four-room flat on the East Side of Manhattan. "I know what it is like to be hungry," he said, "and to feel the prejudice of those in society who do not understand poverty."
He offers himself as a candidate who appreciates moral as well as material need. "We were blessed with a hard life," he said of an Irish-Catholic upbringing in which his mother died at age 46 and his father was laid off chronically from a shipping company job.
"But they had the courage to bring forth life," said Mr. Walsh, offering his explanation of why he is in the party.
In the Cuomo camp, Raymond B. Harding, the Liberal Party leader who is one of the Governor's chief strategists, looks on Mr. Walsh as critically needed to force abortion to the fore as a potentially negative issue for Senator Pataki.
"Pataki must have read the same poll finding that D'Amato used to select him -- a Roman Catholic who is pro-choice," said Mr. Harding, gauging voters' gradual acceptance of legalized abortion. "Walsh more than anyone else will be able to credibly point at the flip-flops Pataki has taken on the choice issue over the years."
Harding said he hoped to see a three-way debate of the candidates. But Mr. Pataki insists voters would be best served in one-on-one debates with Governor Cuomo that exclude Mr. Walsh.
Zenia Mucha, the Republican campaign spokeswoman, contended, "Mario Cuomo is trying to hide behind a third-party candidate because he lacks the courage to debate George Pataki one on one." She said that Mr. Pataki in accepting Medicaid financing for abortion had proposed adding an "educational component" about birth control, and that Senator D'Amato, while opposed as ever to abortion, considered Mr. Pataki the ideal candidate in a race far broader than a single issue.
For his own needs, Mr. Walsh takes care to condemn the recent wave of attacks on abortion clinics, calling for less aggressive tactics by abortion opponents and for discussions by both sides aimed at solutions that lessen the need for abortion. "The alarming incidence of violence raises the importance of peaceful communication," he said.
Mr. Walsh, a lean, 6-foot-2-inch executive in banker's tailoring of chalk-striped, double-breasted blue, complains he has been ignored, even blackballed by the news media in three months of campaigning, even though the 33,000-member party could theoretically prove decisive in the gubernatorial outcome. With no funds for television, he hopes to garner attention and money for mailings if he can surface in a televised debate.
"Then, hey, he could pick up the 90,000 to 120,000 votes that might eventually make the difference," said one Cuomo adviser, thinking wishfully of tarnishing the pro-choice stance of Mr. Pataki.
The campaign is very much a Walsh family effort. Six of the candidate's siblings are involved, led by his younger sister, Diana, who is the campaign manager. "Bank On Bob! Thirty years of financial experience!" she says, dreaming of television spot ads that the party cannot afford.
Mr. Walsh, who is married with three children and five grandchildren, is trying to win votes as a "multi-issue candidate in a single-issue party." He speaks of the "slaughter of 31 million fellow Americans" as well as a raft of education, economic and anti-crime policies. These include his opposition to the death penalty and his view that citizens entitled to own guns ought to first pass a licensing test.
In the context of national politics, Cuomo strategists would like to identify Mr. Pataki with the Republican's fumbling history in 1992 of some stridently moralizing convention rhetoric followed by policy revisions in defeat. Mr. Walsh hopes New York voters will reject Mr. Pataki and signal hope for former Vice President Dan Quayle's attempt to revive the family values debate and his chances for the Presidency.
"There is a war on for the soul of the Republican party," said Lena Harknett, state chairwoman of the Right To Life party, summarizing her loyalists' nationally oriented motivation. "Two years ago, we delivered 224,000 votes to Al D'Amato," twice his slender margin of victory. "Without us he couldn't have done it," she claimed.
For all that furor, "D'Amato is not the candidate this year," one Cuomo sympathizer conceded. "The Right To Life line could eventually be crucial," this politician said. "But right now it's not, not the way Pataki's been moving ahead."
But Mr. Walsh, with less than $10,000 in his campaign kitty, was smiling this week, as he brandished his first invitations to two televised gubernatorial debates. "You'll see," he insisted. "Before this is over, I'll rock the political establishment."

At a rally on Long Island last week, Robert T. Walsh, left, the Right to Life candidate, spoke with with Gov. Mario M. Cuomo. (Jim Estrin/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Atom Plant Is Assessed At a Hearing

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: CROTON-ON-HUDSON, N.Y., Sept. 26

 BODY:
The Indian Point 3 nuclear reactor will not be worth reopening unless it can operate much more reliably than it has in the past and can cut operating expenses sharply, both opponents and supporters agreed in testimony today at a legislative hearing. And even supporters acknowledged that if the plant were closed, the economic consequences might be quite small.
The plant, which has faced stiff political opposition through most of its 18 years, has been shut for 19 months because of safety problems. It has been producing power in the last few years that is more expensive than the electricity from plants that use oil, coal or natural gas. But the New York Power Authority, the state-owned utility that runs the plant, hopes to reopen it next spring, after extensive retraining of workers and improvements in equipment. 
 The hearing was conducted today by Assemblyman Richard L. Brodsky, Democrat of Greenburgh, who is the chairman of the Assembly's Committee on Environmental Conservation.
An energy expert, Charles Komanoff, testified that the state could save $15 million to $20 million a year over the next five years by closing the plant. This would be true even if the reactor could operate 60 to 70 percent of the time, compared with a historical average of about 52 percent as of the end of last year, and even if Indian Point's operating expenses could be permanently cut by 15 percent, he said.
Even the plant's supporters painted a tough picture and the support they gave seemed lukewarm sometimes. The president of the Power Authority, S. David Freeman, in effect, proposed a probation period. "We should not rely on anyone's opinion and projections," he said. "We should give this reactor an opportunity to show its stuff."
But Mr. Komanoff said, "There are more instances of lousy plants remaining lousy than of lousy plants turning around and becoming good."
Francis J. Murray Jr., the New York State Energy Commissioner, testified in favor of the plant's operation. But if the plant were closed, by his calculation, the effect on utility bills would be decidedly modest: between $211 million and $451 million over the next 20 years, taking into account the value today of money that would be paid in decades to come. That would be one-tenth of 1 percent to two-tenths of 1 percent of the state's utility bills. Even including environmental effects, the price difference would be less than one-half of 1 percent, according to calculations by his office.
The Indian Point 3 reactor, and the adjacent Indian Point 2 plant, owned by Consolidated Edison, were the subject of the longest safety hearings in the history of the nuclear industry a decade ago, when the Nuclear Regulatory Commission evaluated emergency plans. Almost 300,000 people live within 10 miles of Indian Point, which is about five miles north of here in Buchanan, on the east bank of the Hudson River.
Con Edison officials also testified today in favor of Indian Point 3, a plant which it built and sold to the Power Authority in 1975. Con Ed operates the adjacent reactor, which is nearly a twin, and it might come under pressure to close that reactor if Indian Point 3 were closed. In addition, if Indian Point 3 were closed, Con Ed might have to supply the customers the reactor formerly served.
The plant's customers are local governments and governmental agencies, including the City of New York, the city's Housing Authority and the Metropolitan Transportation Authority.
If costs are higher than they should be, then governments pay extra, most of it raised from property taxes, Mr. Brodsky said. "We are looking at a property tax subsidy to operate a nuclear plant," he said. "That is unacceptable to any reasonable person."

HEADLINE: New York Looks to S. I. Site to Revive Port

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY 

 BODY:
The Howland Hook Marine Terminal on Staten Island was once a prosperous shipping hub, but since 1986 it has been virtually abandoned. Every so often, a maintenance crew cleans and powers up the cranes that used to hoist containers on and off freighters. The multilegged machines are put through a lonely pantomime of the chores that they once did, in the hope that someday New York City will again be a world-class port and they will be needed.
That day may now be coming. Howland Hook is scheduled to reopen by December, giving the city perhaps its last chance to win back some of the shipping business that fled en masse across New York Harbor to New Jersey in the 1960's. 
 "This means the rebirth of the waterfront industry in our city," said Carmine F. Ragucci, a longtime fixture on local wharves who is about to sign an agreement with the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey to operate Howland Hook. "This gives New York City the opportunity to shine again as a real partner in the dynamics of the port."
But not everyone is pleased that government agencies have spent tens of millions of dollars on the 187-acre terminal, which faces the Arthur Kill on the northwest corner of Staten Island. Though New York and New Jersey are partners in the administration of the harbor, through the Port Authority, their alliance has long been uneasy.
News of Howland Hook's resurrection has touched off accusations from the New Jersey side of favoritism and political gamesmanship in the management of the overall port, which handled $56.31 billion in cargo last year and generates more than 180,000 jobs in the region.
Some terminal operators in New Jersey contend that Howland Hook's reopening is a waste of money, intended to quiet complaints from city politicians about losing their dominance of the harbor.
"There is no need for another terminal at this time," said M. Brian Maher, president of Maher Terminals, which operates at both Port Newark and Port Elizabeth. "The only place the business can come from is the Jersey side. It doesn't take a rocket scientist to figure that out."
Either way, there is a lot at stake. If Howland Hook succeeds, the terminal could usher in a new era in the maritime history of a metropolis that was shaped by the sea, only to all but forsake it in recent years.
Mr. Ragucci said that when the terminal greets its first ship, it will be the largest private employer on Staten Island, with several hundred workers. Eventually, he said, it should have more than 500 people on its payroll and handle 250,000 to 400,000 cargo containers a year.
Depending on how much trade increases, that could be 20 to 30 percent of the containers shipped through New York Harbor, which is shared by New York City and New Jersey.
Last year, New York City accounted for only about 48,000, or 4 percent, of the nearly 1.2 million containers brought through the harbor. They were handled at the Red Hook Container Terminal in Brooklyn, which has long been hobbled by its poor transportation links. Until the 1960's, the piers of New York City were by far the busiest in the nation.
New York City bought Howland Hook in the early 1970's in a last-ditch response to a development that was having stark consequences for its maritime industry: the advent of containerized shipping. Instead of moving goods in odd-sized crates carried by longshoremen, companies in the 1960's found that it was more efficient and cheaper to load them into 20- and 40-foot containers that could be hauled by cranes on and off trucks or railroad cars.
This method requires swaths of land for storing the containers, as well as good transportation connections. The piers in Manhattan and Brooklyn, which had been receiving ships since the 1600's, were considered inadequate, and New York City officials were skeptical at first about the merits of containerized shipping, so the Port Authority and private companies built terminals in New Jersey. Shipping began to die in New York City.
Howland Hook enabled the city to keep a toehold until the tenant there, United States Lines, went bankrupt in 1986. At that time, the overall port was suffering from a reputation for high costs and poor infrastructure, so no company wanted to use the terminal, and it sat mainly unused.
Port officials now say that with a recent upturn in trade in New York Harbor, prompted by rising exports and improvements in service, the terminals in New Jersey are nearing capacity and there is demand for more space to insure that customers do not defect to rival ports like Baltimore.
"This means that there will be an alternative to New Jersey that will bring about enhanced competition and will also allow the regional port to grow," said Chris Ward, a senior vice president at the New York City Economic Development Corporation, which oversees the city's terminals. "It will allow many of the existing and smaller shipping lines increased service and better access."
Like the terminals in New Jersey, Howland Hook has plenty of land, and it is next to the Goethals Bridge, which allows trucks to drive from Staten Island to the New Jersey Turnpike. And in a step crucial to Howland Hook's success, New York City is completing negotiations to buy the Staten Island Railway, which runs from the terminal to the freight railways in New Jersey.
The purchase of the railroad, expected to cost about $10 million, is being financed by Federal money. The Port Authority, which began leasing the terminal from the city in the mid-1980's, is spending $25 million to repair it. Built from scratch, Howland Hook would cost more than $250 million.
"The strategic location makes it one of the most ideal locations in the port, and to not have it open is just mind-boggling," said Mr. Ragucci, who managed the terminal for United States Lines before it went bankrupt.
But Mr. Ragucci's enthusiasm is not shared across the harbor.
Phil Connors, president of Universal Maritime, which handles 210,000 containers a year at Port Newark, said he had expressed his concern about Howland Hook to the Port Authority, and would be watching it closely to determine whether it was receiving preferential treatment.
"There has been much influence put upon the Port Authority to look after Howland Hook, from the perspective of keeping the New York side alive," Mr. Connors said. "But in fact, the reality of life is that the New Jersey side is where the containerization market lies. There are major problems on the New York side, which is why so few lines call there."
Stanley Brezenoff, executive director of the Port Authority, acknowledged that officials in New York City had long lobbied for Howland Hook, but he said that the Port Authority would have sought a new tenant for the terminal regardless of pressure from the city.
"There is probably some concern among existing operators that this will serve to redistribute business," he said. "That is an understandable concern. But I really do think that the port is poised for growth. In addition, competition is good within the port. It keeps costs down and it makes us more competitive with other ports."
But even some officials on the New York side raise questions about Howland Hook. Representative Jerrold L. Nadler, who has long been an arch-foe of the Port Authority, said it was far more important that government examine building a new terminal in Brooklyn that would be fed by new freight railway lines.
Mr. Nadler, a Democrat whose district includes sections of Brooklyn, predicted that in the next century freighters might become so large that they might not be able to navigate the Kill van Kull, the waterway between Staten Island and Bayonne, N.J., that leads to Newark Bay, site of Port Newark and Port Elizabeth. Ships would have far less difficulty reaching a new terminal at Brooklyn, he said.
"Howland Hook is the one place in New York that enjoys the same geographic problems as New Jersey," he said. "The Kill van Kull is a narrow, treacherous body of water."
Mr. Nadler accused the Port Authority of refusing even to consider the idea of a new terminal in Brooklyn.
"The Port Authority has for the last 50 years been the enemy of New York," he said. "The Port Authority has given us a lot of glitzy projects that add very little to the economy, and has done very well for New Jersey."
But Port Authority officials contend that to create a new containerized shipping terminal in Brooklyn would require the government to obtain a huge parcel of land and spend hundreds of millions of dollars, perhaps more than a billion. Port Elizabeth, for example, is so large that if it were in Brooklyn it would extend from the waterfront to Prospect Park.
Other shipping experts say the squabbling between New York City and New Jersey is shortsighted and fails to recognize the increasing integration of the region's economy. Howland Hook, they say, will benefit both sides.
"The entire port district is a regional resource," said Jack Dean, a research associate at the Regional Plan Association, a nonprofit organization that studies land-use issues. "While it might be physically in New Jersey, it's still a regional resource. Many of the firms in the World Trade Center may operate in New York, but the cargo may actually hit the ground in New Jersey.
"To be very petty about which side of the river the Port Authority loves more isn't that productive."

HEADLINE: Pataki Riding Anti-Cuomo Wave, Poll Shows

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO 

 BODY:
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo faces a difficult battle for re-election, an independent poll released yesterday shows, as an increasing number of voters say they disapprove of his performance and are prepared to vote for his Republican rival, State Senator George E. Pataki, even though he remains relatively unknown.
The poll by the Marist Institute for Public Opinion in Poughkeepsie shows Mr. Pataki leading Mr. Cuomo by six percentage points, 44 percent to 38 percent -- a statistical dead heat, given the poll's margin of sampling error. The Right to Life Party candidate, Robert Walsh, received 3 percent. 
 But the poll shows that many voters do not have strong impressions of Mr. Pataki and that 15 percent remain undecided, meaning the race is likely to remain volatile. When fence-sitting voters were asked which way they were leaning, Mr. Pataki's lead grew slightly to eight points, 48 percent to 40 percent.
"It speaks to an unpopular incumbent and an unknown challenger and suggests that the Pataki vote is largely an anti-Cuomo vote rather than a pro-Pataki vote," said Lee M. Miringoff, director of the institute.
The poll of 911 registered voters was conducted from Sept. 19-21. It had a sampling error of plus or minus three percentage points among registered voters and plus or minus four percentage points among likely voters.
With six weeks left in the campaign, the poll offered several signs that Mr. Cuomo, a three-term Democrat, was in grave political danger. Asked to evaluate Mr. Cuomo's performance, 32 percent said it was excellent or good, a record low for Mr. Cuomo and four points lower than in a Marist poll conducted in March.
When asked about their economic well-being, 77 percent said they were worse off or about the same as a year ago, an increase of 11 points from 1990. And when asked whether the state is heading in the right direction, 62 percent said it was not, about the same as in March.
"Why is the electorate in a grumpy mood? Mr. Miringoff said. "You can see the answer in the fact that the number of people who think they are better off has fallen decidedly. There is a sense of economic turbulence, which gets laid at Cuomo's doorstep."
The poll also showed that fewer people, 63 percent compared to 71 percent in March, think the state is still in a recession. But the decline has not translated into new support for the Governor, Mr. Miringoff said, indicating that voters do not think his economic policies have helped.
The poll offered a few bright spots for the Governor. Among likely voters, Mr. Pataki's lead fell to three points, 43 percent to 40 percent. And the poll showed that 73 percent of Mr. Cuomo's supporters plan to vote for him because they have positive images of him. By comparison, most of Mr. Pataki's supporters are motivated mainly by anti-Cuomo sentiments.
Mr. Miringoff said those figures suggested that Mr. Cuomo's base support was firmer and more committed than Mr. Pataki's, an important factor in drawing voters to the polls.
Mr. Cuomo's aides said the poll demonstrated similarities between this year's race and the 1978 gubernatorial campaign, when Perry B. Duryea, the Republican challenger, lost his lead over Gov. Hugh L. Carey, and eventually the election, as voters learned more about him.
"When voters have to make a choice between the consistent, honest, tough leadership Mario Cuomo has given them and George Pataki, who has waffled on major issues of the day, they will choose Mario Cuomo," said Joel R. Benenson, Mr. Cuomo's campaign spokesman. "At this point, they still don't know George Pataki because he's been hiding."
But Mr. Pataki's camp was buoyant about the poll, saying it showed that voters tended to like Mr. Pataki as they got to know him. In March, when he was virtually unknown, Mr. Pataki received 28 percent in a Marist poll. And they said the poll suggested that the undecided vote tended to break in Mr. Pataki's favor.
"People have two reasons for voting for George Pataki: because they like him and his policies for change, and because they are hostile to Mario Cuomo's failed policies," said Zenia Mucha, Mr. Pataki's campaign spokeswoman.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Economy

HEADLINE: Broad Market Mixed, but Dow Gains 17.49

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
Stock prices closed mixed yesterday in advance of today's Federal Reserve policy-making meeting on interest rates. Almost one stock advanced for every one that declined on the New York Stock Exchange.
Economists are split over whether the Fed will act today to raise short-term interest rates a sixth time this year, a move that would hurt bonds and most stocks. Some economists, citing the higher-than-expected Producer Price Index for August, say the Fed will raise rates. Others say the Fed will wait for more data. 
 The Dow Jones industrial average rose 17.49 points, to 3,849.24, while the broader Standard & Poor's 500 rose 1.15 points, to 460.82. The Nasdaq composite index fell 1.83 points, to 755.63.
Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was 272.5 million shares, about 5 percent below this year's average of 287 million shares.
In a generally humdrum market, Mercantile Stores, a Midwestern department store chain, fell 16 3/8, to 38 3/4, in heavy trading after it announced that it had ended talks with a potential acquirer.
For much of this month, Mercantile stock had been trading as much as 50 percent above its average share price of 37 for the year, on the basis of merger rumors, which the company later confirmed.
Nextel Communications, the wireless telephone company, established an intraday 52-week low of 20 5/8, before closing down 1 3/4, to 20 7/8. It announced that it would increase the number of its shares to 3.5 million from 3 million to acquire American Mobile Systems.
Nextel was sued by some American Mobile holders over its previous bid, which the holders considered too low. Nextel's share price has been dropping since an aborted alliance with MCI Communications in August.
Philip Morris, a Dow component stock, saw its shares rise after it issued a statement denying that it had threatened to leave New York City if a tough anti-smoking ordinance were passed, saying that a possible move was an option to be considered it the legislation passed. Philip Morris shares rose 1 1/4, to 59 3/4, helping lift the Dow about 3 points.
Marriott International shares rose 1 5/8, to 29 5/8, when the lodging and food services company paid $5 million to Wellington Management, a Marriott bond holder, to settle Wellington's portion of fraud claims against Marriott.
Some bond holders in Marriott contended that Marriott should have told them of a reorganizationplan before they bought the bonds. The plan reduced the value of their bonds by nearly a third. The other bond holders are continuing their suit, which went to trial yesterday.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Election Results Hurt Mark; Dollar Gains Against Yen

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar rose against the mark yesterday after the results of a regional election in Germany raised concern that Chancellor Helmut Kohl's coalition Government could suffer defeat in federal elections next month.
The dollar also gained against the yen amid speculation that the United States and Japan were making progress in talks aimed at curbing Japan's swollen trade surplus. 
 Many traders sold marks after Germany's Free Democratic Party, one of Mr. Kohl's coalition partners, failed on Sunday to win the 5 percent of the vote needed to remain in the Bavarian state legislature. The results raised concern that the Free Democrats would lose seats in the Parliament in federal elections on Oct. 16, hurting Mr. Kohl's ability to form a new government.
"If Kohl doesn't get a majority in Parliament, people will dump Deutsche marks," said Anil Agarwal, currency specialist at Brown Brothers Harriman & Company. Mr. Kohl's opponents, the Social Democrats, are considered to be less committed to controlling government spending and inflation.
The dollar rose as high as 1.5573 marks yesterday before settling at 1.5527 marks in late New York trading, up from 1.5486 marks on Friday.
The dollar rose against the yen after Ambassador Walter F. Mondale told reporters in Tokyo that the Clinton Administration had made some progress in trade talks with Japan, but "we're not there yet."
With progress at the bargaining table, the Clinton Administration is considered less likely to resume calls for a strong yen, a tactic it pursued last year. A strong yen could help balance trade by making Japanese goods more expensive.
The dollar rose to 98.75 yen yesterday from 97.85 yen on Friday.
The Clinton Administration has set a Friday deadline for progress in the trade talks, after which the United States may impose trade sanctions on Japan.
The dollar strengthened against most other major currencies yesterday, rising to 5.3060 French francs from 5.2922 francs on Friday and rising to 1,563.50 Italian lire from 1,559.50 lire. The dollar was steady at 1.2870 Swiss francs. The Canadian dollar fell to 74.11 United States cents from 74.44 cents. The British pound fell to $1.5715 from $1.5772.

HEADLINE: Japan Plans Last-Ditch Visit To Avert U.S. Trade Curbs

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Sept. 26

 BODY:
With a deadline looming on Friday in trade talks with the United States, Japan's trade minister will leave for Washington on Tuesday in a last-ditch effort to avert sanctions against his country.
Ryutaro Hashimoto, the Minister of International Trade and Industry, is expected to meet on Wednesday with Mickey Kantor, the United States trade representative, and probably with Commerce Secretary Ronald H. Brown, officials here said today. Mr. Hashimoto and Mr. Kantor spoke by telephone this morning. 
 Mr. Hashimoto's ministry is in charge of the talks on automobiles and glass, two of the four sectors being negotiated under the so-called framework agreement signed by the two nations in July 1993.
Officials in both nations say that agreements in those two sectors, especially automobiles, are unlikely by Friday because the two sides are so far apart. It seems likely, therefore, that Mr. Hashimoto's visit is aimed not at reaching an agreement but perhaps at moving close enough to persuade the United States not to impose sanctions in those areas.
There are two separate sets of sanctions that will be decided by Friday. If there is no agreement on opening Japan's markets in government procurement of telecommunications and medical equipment, sanctions will almost certainly be applied under a section of United States law dealing with this matter. The Administration by Friday will also decide whether to cite Japan, and other countries, as unfair traders under the so-called Super 301 clause of the trade law, which would set in motion a process that could culminate in other sanctions.
At a news conference here today, Walter F. Mondale, the American Ambassador to Japan, predicted that there would be sanctions if there was no public procurement agreement by Friday. But he said it was "just too soon to know yet" if the Administration would decide on sanctions in other areas.
"On public procurement we have to get an agreement," Mr. Mondale said. He added later, "There will be a different tone to our relationship if we are able to break through on insurance and public procurement."
Of the four sectors under negotiation, Mr. Mondale said an agreement was closest on opening and deregulating Japan's insurance market. In government procurement, he said, "some progress has been made" but "we're not there yet."
Mr. Mondale said that "there has been some progress" on flat glass, which has not been as high a priority as the other three areas, but it is questionable whether it will be possible to reach an agreement by Friday. Talks on sales of foreign autos and auto parts in Japan are "not quite ripe" for a settlement, he said.
The sticking point in the government procurement talks is the issue of how to judge whether progress is being made. The United States essentially wants Japan to commit to an increase in procurement of foreign medical and telecommunications equipment.
Japan has argued that it cannot agree to any "targets," even one as vague as a commitment that foreign sales should go up without specifying by how much. If the public procurement system is to be made truly fair and open, it will be impossible for the Government to guarantee which companies from which countries will win the bids, officials here argue.
"We are not ready to reach a settlement by bending our principles," Mr. Hashimoto, the trade minister, said during a trip to Thailand last weekend, according to the Kyodo News Service.

HEADLINE: PERSONAL COMPUTERS;
Odd Keyboards May Help You Type; On the Other Hand, They May Not

BYLINE: By STEPHEN MANES 

 BODY:
"DO NOT REMOVE THIS TAG!" advises a sticker on the cord. A bigger sticker bears a bigger orange "WARNING" and a discussion of the perils of typing. These alarming admonitions appear on a mostly plastic product that the Microsoft Corporation bills as "natural."
Natural? The Microsoft Natural Keyboard looks surreal, as if it had been fished from the seas bloated, warped and whitened. It is the latest member of a growing collection of odd-looking "ergonomic" models pioneered in the general market by Apple Computer Inc. and sharing one tenet: the keyboards most of us use are unfit for human hands. 
 To quote Microsoft's legalese, "Continuous use of a keyboard may cause repetitive stress injuries or related injuries." But no modified keyboard has been proven any more "natural" than standard models. Microsoft "does not claim that its new Natural Keyboard will either cure or prevent" injuries. The Kinesis Corporation of Bellevue, Wash., notes that computer-related illness can have many causes, from bad chairs to inadequate break time, and reminds that its own keyboard "is not a medical treatment."
On the other hand (or, more accurately, on both), the problems are real. I have experienced pain and numbness so debilitating that they forced me to quit typing for a week. Exactly what caused the symptoms is impossible to say; typing too much definitely had something to do with it.
But "ergonomic" keyboards differ wildly from one another, suggesting that ergonomics, like economics, is a dismal science with a whole lot of guessing going on. Aside from a slightly modified QWERTY layout, about all these keyboards typically have in common is a separation of left hand from right to help keep users' wrists straight.
The design of the Kinesis Ergonomic Keyboard ($390) looks and is radical. It puts most of the keys in two concave bowls and realigns them vertically to minimize awkward reaches. It also moves keys like enter and backspace to the thumbs, divides the arrows between left hand and right and offers an optional foot pedal ($25) that can serve as a shift key or activate the laptop-style numeric keypad embedded among the letters. The keyboard is comfortable (though not adjustable), but mastering it is not a trivial task.
Microsoft's keyboard (less than $100) seems the Kinesis's antithesis. Unusually thick and wide with a rise toward the center, it puts the tops of many keys more than half again as far from the desktop as international standards dictate. The fixed angle between the left and right hands is meant to straighten them, but the navigational keys and keypad are arranged conventionally. The only adjustment is a two-position tilt intended to help keep the wrists horizontal.
In Windows, either of two new "Windows" keys works just like Ctrl-Esc, but they are much easier to hit by accident. A key with an icon of a drop-down menu is unexplained because it currently does nothing at all; future applications may find jobs for it. The best thing in the package may well be the software, including a surprisingly good mouse emulator for the numeric keypad and revamped Task Manager software, which should have been included with the operating system.
Split down the middle, the Select-Ease keyboard (about $150) from Lexmark International Inc., emphasizes flexibility. Its legs and adjustment knob afford an infinity of tilts and twists, including some the manual says should be "discouraged." Stunt typists may enjoy sloping the left and right sides at a 45-degree angle to form a sort of tent. Others are more likely to separate the left and right sides entirely or keep them together at a comfortable angle.
Lexmark's "Erase-Eaze" can cleverly turn either side of the split space bar into a handy backspace. The keyboard adds arrow keys for the left hand, but also repositions the right-handed navigational keys and makes the Alt and Ctrl keys annoyingly small. An adjustable numeric keypad, which will cost about $40 when available, will be a desk-hogging option.
The first couple of hours spent using any of these keyboards is irritating, but produces fewer errors than I would have guessed. Regularly switching back to a laptop or standard model would be a major nuisance.
And these designs may create new problems. For example, none includes a pointing device. Since Microsoft's unit is nearly three inches wider than standard, right-handed users will need to reach farther for their mice. Is that ergonomically correct?
All three manuals advise against using palm rests while typing, but most designs, including those from Lexmark and Kinesis, keep typists from dropping their wrists below keyboard level. Microsoft's palm rest drops off below the space bar. Can that be ergonomically sound?
Years ago, switching to a model that required a lighter touch seemed to help relieve my hand problems. But depressing the Microsoft and Lexmark keys requires about 25 percent more force than needed with the Kinesis or standard models. Ergonomically sensible? Who knows?
The keyboards sold with today's computers are often cheesy, but replacement models are widely available. Since keyboards are among the most personal of computer peripherals, make sure any one you buy, standard or unorthodox, comes with an unconditional money-back guarantee. And remember that no keyboard is a panacea, even if it is made from bean curd, looks like a pickle and is labeled "organic."
