HEADLINE: ON BASEBALL;
The Semantics Game May Be the Only Game in Town

BYLINE: By Murray Chass 

 BODY:
This was supposed to be the last week of the season. This was supposed to be the week that the Yankees prepared for their first post-season appearance since 1981, that the Cleveland Indians ended their post-1954 post-season drought. This week was supposed to be filled with games that would determine the makeup of the playoffs.
This weekend Cincinnati and Houston were supposed to be playing for the first championship of the National League's new Central Division. In other season-ending series, Atlanta was going to play Montreal for the National League East championship and Los Angeles was going to try to hold off San Francisco for the N.L. West title. The league's first wild-card playoff team was going to emerge from those final series. 
 In the American League, the Kansas City Royals, who would still be managed by Hal McRae, were hoping to be close enough to the Chicago White Sox for their final-weekend series to be meaningful in the Central Division. The Indians would play the Yankees four times this week, then play the Baltimore Orioles during the weekend, possibly for the wild-card spot.
And the Texas Rangers would be playing California and Minnesota in one last chance at taking at least a .500 record into the playoffs.
But none of those teams will play this week. There is, in fact, no this week; there is no last weekend either, just too many lost weekends.
The final week and weekend of the season, as it turns out, began Aug. 5. The strike started a week later. Forty-seven days later, the strike has not ended.
Because it has not ended, professional sports has a terrific opportunity to establish a first. That is, after all, what players and teams try to do, isn't it?
Come Saturday, for the first time, two major professional sports could be shut down at the same time. Think of it. On what is supposed to be the last weekend of the baseball season and the first weekend of the hockey season, there will be no baseball and probably no hockey.
National Hockey League players and owners could reach an agreement in the next few days and avert a shutdown in their sport, but if they don't, Commissioner Gary Bettman has said he and the owners will lock out the players.
No, that's not quite right. Bettman did not use the word "lockout" nor the verb form "lock out." He said they would "postpone" the start of the season. But a lockout by any other name is a lockout. If the N.H.L. planned to open the season even without a labor agreement but players decided not to play, the owners would call that a strike, not a vacation.
By avoiding the word "lockout" in noun or verb form, Bettman is playing a game of semantics. That's what sports people do these days. They don't play baseball or hockey or maybe even basketball; they play semantics.
Bettman shuns the term "salary cap," too, saying that's not what they want. But a forthright member of baseball management said yesterday, "He's seeking exactly what we want and he doesn't call it a cap."
Give baseball management people credit. They have, for the most part, not played the semantics game. When they said last year they would do nothing to interfere with this season, they pledged not to lock out the players. They used the actual words. When they made their economic proposal to the players in June, they called it a salary cap, although they acknowledge the term is burdened with a negative connotation.
They have trotted out some euphemisms -- cost certainty and cost control, for two. But they never have tried to suggest what they want is not a salary cap.
The National Basketball Association brought the term to the sports world. Its officials would have everyone believe that their salary cap has been responsible for the resurgent popularity of the league, but that's highly dubious unless salary cap is spelled the same as Magic, Michael and Larry.
The National Football League was No. 2 on the salary cap roster, implementing it this season after using legitimate free agency as the carrot to induce players to accept it. The N.H.L. is holding out a similar inducement to its players.
But the N.B.A. faces its own problems in trying to maintain its cap. The league and its players so far have argued their labor differences in court but eventually will have to get to the bargaining table. Once there, management may have difficulty defending its position in the face of widespread effort by the league's member teams to circumvent the cap.
How do you defend a system that uses the players to protect the owners from themselves and their compulsive spending when the very people it is meant to protect try to skirt it?
It's a question worth pondering as the world of professional sports converges at the cap intersection and creates gridlock that has shut down one game, threatens imminent shutdown of another and holds out the prospect of interrupting a third.

HEADLINE: 'Glass Ceiling' Is Pervasive, Secretary of Labor Contends

BYLINE: By CATHERINE S. MANEGOLD 

 BODY:
Labor Secretary Robert B. Reich said today that subtle but pervasive patterns of discrimination dominate the public, private and nonprofit sectors of society because of a "myopia" on the part of many white male managers who "unthinkingly discriminate" without having any idea they are doing so.
Speaking in New York at the fifth and final hearing of a Congressional commission studying discrimination at the executive level, Mr. Reich said artificial barriers to advancement are holding back many women and minorities from attaining positions commensurate with their skills, training and numbers, and preventing the economy from growing to its full potential. 
 "Walk through the boardrooms or executive suites of many companies, and it's still difficult to find a single woman or minority man," he said in the fifth and final hearing of the Glass Ceiling Commission. "Even though women are half the work force, they make up only between 3 and 6 percent of corporate executives and officers. Minorities account for only one percent."
Statistics presented today painted a monochromatic picture of life in the executive suites three decades after the passage of the Civil Rights Act and long after calls by leaders of the women's movement for a revolution in America's boardrooms.
They also showed the Federal Government's top echelons out of kilter with its vast work force. Minorities account for 28 percent of Federal jobs but fill only 8 percent of the top posts, said Mr. Reich. Women make up 44 percent of the work force, but 87 percent of the top posts are held by men.
"In Washington sometimes," Mr. Reich told a packed hall at the City Bar Association's headquarters on West 44th Street, "the only architectural feature as prominent as the Capitol Dome is the glass ceiling."
The Glass Ceiling Commission was created by Congress in 1991 to study impediments to the advancement of minorities and women to the top rungs of power. It is expected to file a report to President Clinton early next year. Today's hearing explored inequalities in places that included corporate boardrooms, exclusive law firms and the nation's best colleges and universities.
Some of the solutions, several speakers said, are already in place in Federal legislation and policies. But a lack of resources and minimal enforcement efforts have led to an environment in which corporate heads and other leaders have been able to ignore existing regulations.
"Enforcement of the existing laws lags," said Christine Carty, a lawyer and chairwoman of the Obstacles to Advancement Subcommittee of the New York Women's Agenda, a nonprofit coalition of women's groups. Describing a "corrosive cynicism" among employers, she criticized the lack of financing for enforcement agencies and called for investigations into unfair practices to send a message to employers around the country.
Other speakers called on the Government to publish the results of investigations and provide more general information to both employers and workers through a broad program of public disclosure.
"Good-faith efforts are not enough"' said Sheila W. Wellington, the president of Catalyst, a nonprofit research group that studies women in business. She said that at least half of the top 1,000 corporations in the country have not one woman on their boards. Overall, women hold 6.2 percent of those seats.
Several witnesses testified that problems for minority women, and Latin American women in particular, far outstrip those of all other categories. Across the board, said Jenny Rivera of the Puerto Rican Legal Defense Fund, Hispanic women tend to hold less stable jobs at lower positions for lower wages than their peers.
Conditions for many blacks are similarly bleak, said Barbara Armwine of the Lawyers Committee for Civil Rights Under Law. "We talk about the concrete ceiling or the concrete wall," she said. "But it is really old-fashioned racial discrimination."
That theme was heatedly replayed in the afternoon by Edward Jones of the Harvard Business School's Black Alumni Association. He said social barriers for blacks in the business world still keep them from the inner core of power. Blacks, he said, seldom win a full place in the top ranks of business.

HEADLINE: High Court to Review Kansas City School Desegregation Case

BYLINE: By LINDA GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
The Supreme Court announced this evening that it would review a long-running Kansas City school desegregation case as well as seven other new cases in its new term, which does not formally begin until next Monday.
The Justices, who met privately for much of the day to consider the more than 1,700 new appeals that have accumulated since June, when the Court's last term ended, gave no explanation for their unusual early evening announcement. 
 The Kansas City case as well as one of the other new cases, a constitutional challenge to a Federal program designed to steer Federal contracting dollars to minority-owned companies, is likely to revive debates that have been quiescent at the Court for the last few years over appropriate remedies for past racial discrimination.
The Kansas City school case is an appeal by the State of Missouri, which under a series of Federal court orders dating to the early 1980's has been required to shoulder most of the cost for improvements in the once-segregated school district. The court-ordered remedy has included elaborate magnet schools, lower class sizes and a tax increase for the district's taxpayers that the Supreme Court upheld in a 5-to-4 opinion in 1990.
The cost so far, according to the state, has been $1.3 billion, with the state share totaling $838 million. The state went back to Federal court three years ago to ask for a ruling that the district was now "unitary" and had erased the vestiges of segregation. But both the Federal District Court and the United States Court of Appeals for the Eighth Circuit, in St. Louis, refused. The court opinions suggested, without saying so explicitly, that the remedy would remain in place until student achievement, still below national norms as measured by standardized test scores, had improved.
The appellate court was deeply split in the case, and earlier this year it rejected, by a vote of 6 to 5, the state's request to reconsider the original decision, which had been issued by a three-judge panel of the court.
In its Supreme Court appeal, Missouri v. Jenkins, No. 93-1823, the state is arguing that the appellate court went far beyond the constitutional requirement of equal opportunity to impose an "outcome-based standard wholly foreign to traditional equal protection analysis."
For its part, the Kansas City school district opposed the state's appeal and has sought to keep the special remedial programs alive.
The constitutional guarantee of equal protection is also at issue in a second case the Court accepted today, a challenge by a Colorado construction company to a Federal law that takes race into account in awarding Federal contracts.
The Small Business Act requires government-wide goals to be set for purchasing from small businesses and from small "disadvantaged business enterprises." The law defines disadvantaged businesses as those owned by members of racial minorities as well as those owned by people who have been subjected to "ethnic prejudice or cultural bias."
American Indians, Asian-Americans, blacks and Hispanic people are presumed under the law to fall into the "disadvantaged" category, although this presumption is dropped in any individual case on a showing that the company's owners are not actually socially or economically disadvantaged.
The Department of Transportation awards prime contractors added compensation of up to 1.5 percent for subcontracting at least 10 percent of any contract to a disadvantaged business. This program was challenged by a non-minority contractor, Adarand Constructors Inc., which submitted a low bid on a federally financed highway project but lost a subcontract to a minority-owned company. Adarand filed suit on the ground that its constitutional right to equal protection had been violated. It lost in the Federal District Court in Denver and in the United States Court of Appeals for the 10th Circuit, also in Denver.
The company's appeal, which the Clinton Administration urged the Supreme Court not to hear, gives the Court its first opportunity in several years to review the constitutional standard that applies to Federal affirmative action programs. While the Court has invalidated a set-aside program imposed by a local government, it has upheld similar Federal programs. The new case is Adarand Constructors v. Pena, No. 93-1841.
The new cases, and the others accepted for review today, will be argued in January. The Court will add more cases to its docket when it formally convenes on Monday.

HEADLINE: H.M.O. Drug Plans Are No Consumer Bargain

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Overlooked in the headlong rush to embrace the health maintenance organization managed-care concept as a solution to the health care crisis is one crucial fact. The H.M.O. bottom line imperative creates an environment in which subscribers are often forced to pay exorbitant amounts for relatively inexpensive medication. The "prescription plan" is the vehicle for overcharging patients. 
 I became aware of this when several H.M.O. patients with thyroid hormone deficiency asked me to rewrite their prescriptions for the thyroid hormone Synthroid for 30 days rather than for one year.
Their health maintenance organization policy provided for prescription medication, requiring them to pay "only" $15 a prescription, but it stipulated that they could receive only 30 pills. Thus, they would have to spend $180 a year for medication that ordinarily costs $65.66 to $88.47 a year (according to figures this month at five Boston pharmacies).
By paying $91.53 to $114.34 over retail cost, subscribers are unwittingly overpaying the pharmacy and subsidizing the health maintenance organization. Another H.M.O. demands a lower out-of-pocket expenditure, totaling $108 a year, still higher than retail costs, but to get this "bargain" subscribers must restrict shopping to one pharmacy chain.
I appealed to the health maintenance organization director, noting that patients with hormone deficiencies have lifelong medication needs and should not be obliged to congregate every 30 days at the pharmacy groveling for their monthly ration of thyroid hormone, insulin or hydrocortisone. I was advised no exceptions were possible, for the health maintenance group had a "contract with CVS," the pharmacy chain.
This contract, it turns out, guarantees a constant monthly flow of traffic to CVS, with the expectation that subscribers, while they are waiting for the pharmacist to put 30 pills into a vial, will roam the store purchasing toothpaste, hairspray and shampoo, thereby further increasing the revenue of CVS at the expense of the subscriber.
Now it is true that some medications cost more than Synthroid, and it is possible that pharmacies use the excessive money paid for one patient's Synthroid to defray the cost of another patient's Prozac. If this is the case, none of my health maintenance organization patients are aware of their philanthropy.
Such "prescription plans," promoted as a benefit to increase enrollment in the health maintenance organization, impose a hidden economic burden on subscribers, who have paid a hefty up-front premium and who have the misfortune to require care.
 
RICHARD F. SPARK, M.D.
Director, Steroid Research Lab
Harvard Medical School
Boston, Sept. 21, 1994

HEADLINE: H.M.O. Drug Plans Are No Consumer Bargain;
An Expensive Swallow

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In view of the debate regarding what sacrifices should be made to achieve universal health care, I note Merck & Company's contribution: 
 When my cardiologist first prescribed Mevacor in 1992, concern about the cost of this expensive drug caused me to shop around since no generic was available. The American Association of Retired Persons' pharmacy discounted price was then $94 for 60 tablets -- about a $1.60 a swallow.
Since Hillary Rodham Clinton's offensive against high pharmaceutical costs took shape, Merck has raised the price three times, and A.A.R.P.'s discounted price, still considerably below that of most other providers, has risen to $104.
In the interim Merck has spent thousands upon thousands of advertising dollars to promote its product and to increase its share of the hyperlipidemia drug market. With patent protection for some years into the future, and with cholesterol-reducing medications being prescribed much more widely, such unneeded advertising would appear to be a direct contributor to rising costs.
Will Harry and Louise do another television commercial to publicize these increases? They might note that conventional economic theory says the increased volume of production should result in a price decrease. Or are they too busy beating the drum for the insurance industry?
 
IRVING RUCKENS
New York, Sept. 21, 1994

HEADLINE: THE HEALTH CARE DEBATE: CHRONOLOGY;
High Fever to No Pulse

BYLINE: By Monica Borkowski 

 BODY:

1991
 
 NOVEMBER: Harris Wofford, a Democrat running for the Senate from Pennsylvania, wins an upset victory with a campaign that centers on health care, demonstrating the issue's political power.
 
1992 

 FEBRUARY: In the New Hampshire primary campaign, Bill Clinton calls for employers to finance health insurance for their workers.
 FEB. 6: President George Bush unveils a proposal with tax credits or deductions that he says will make medical care affordable to every American. Congress never takes up the plan.
 SEPT. 24: Bill Clinton offers a revised version of his health care proposal. Under this plan, employers would have to buy insurance for their employees; the Government would guarantee coverage for the unemployed; a panel would establish national limits on health spending, and small businesses could buy insurance at a discount.
 
1993
 
 JAN. 25: President Clinton appoints his wife, Hillary, to head a panel to prepare legislation.
 MARCH 8: The United States Chamber of Commerce endorses "managed competition," an approach that emphasizes market forces as the way to control health care costs.
 SEPT. 8: The Health Insurance Association of America finances commercials portraying a suburban couple, "Harry and Louise," fretting over proposed health care restructuring.
 SEPT. 22: President Clinton outlines his proposal to a joint session of Congress. Under this plan, most Americans would obtain coverage through purchasing groups called health alliances; employers would pay at least 80 percent of the insurance premiums for their employees, and universal coverage would be achieved by 1999.
 OCT. 6: Representative Jim Cooper, Democrat of Tennessee, introduces a proposal without employer payments that would rely on market forces to keep health care spending under control. A similar bill is introduced on Oct. 21 in the Senate by John B. Breaux, Democrat of Louisiana.
 NOV. 20: The President's proposal is introduced as a bill in both houses of Congress.
 
1994
 
 JAN. 25: In his State of the Union Message, Mr. Clinton vows to veto any proposal that does not provide universal coverage.
 JAN. 31: The nation's governors unanimously call for Congress to pass a health bill this year, but they cannot agree on whether employers should be required to pay their workers' insurance costs.
 FEB. 2-4: The Business Roundtable, representing about 200 of the nation's largest companies, endorses Representative Cooper's plan. The United States Chamber of Commerce and the National Association of Manufacturers say they cannot support the Clinton plan.
 MARCH 15: The House Ways and Means subcommittee on health is the first panel in Congress to vote on health care. Four others take up the legislation.
 MAY 31: Representative Dan Rostenkowski, Democrat of Illinois, is indicted on 17 criminal charges and relinquishes his chairmanship of the Ways and Means Committee. The Administration loses a powerful ally on health.
 JUNE: Three committees adopt bills generally based on the President's proposals. On June 28, the chairman of the House Committee on Energy and Commerce acknowledges that the committee is hopelessly deadlocked over employer payments.
 JULY 2: The Senate Finance Committee adopts a bill that does not require employer payments and intends to attain 95 percent coverage by 2002.
 JULY 19: Mr. Clinton signals a willingness to be flexible on coverage levels.
 JULY 29: House Democratic leaders unveil a plan promising universal coverage by 1999. Their plan would require employers to pay 80 percent of the cost of insuring their workers, with subsidies for small, low-wage companies. It would extend the Medicare program for people who are unemployed or cannot get coverage through work.
 AUG. 2: Senator George J. Mitchell, Democrat of Maine, proposes legislation that he says will achieve coverage of 95 percent of all Americans by the year 2000 through voluntary measures and subsidies.
 AUG. 4: The President says the Mitchell bill meets his requirements.
 AUG. 9: The Senate begins debate on health legislation.
 AUG. 11: A battle over the Administration's crime bill keeps the House from taking up the leadership's health bill. A bipartisan group in the House introduces a bill to offer billions of dollars in subsidies to help the working poor buy health insurance without raising taxes or requiring employer payments.
 AUG. 17: House Democratic leaders say the Congressional Budget Office has not completed its analysis of health legislation and acknowledge they have no idea when the House can resume its work.
 AUG. 25: The Senate recesses until Sept. 12, and the House decides to do the same.
 AUG. 26: Congressional leaders and White House officials all but concede that there is no chance of passing universal health insurance legislation this year.
 SEPTEMBER: A bipartisan group of moderate Senators meets to devise a relatively modest bill. Mr. Mitchell negotiates with the group, but their efforts are inconclusive.
 SEPT. 26: Mr. Mitchell announces there will be no major health insurance legislation this year. Monica Borkowski

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Chronology

HEADLINE: S.E.C. Wants Mutual Fund Risk Disclosed

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
Concerned about the increased riskiness of certain mutual fund portfolios, the Securities and Exchange Commission recommended today that fund sponsors be required for the first time to provide shareholders with some quantification of that risk. The commission also suggested a tighter limit on portfolio investments that cannot easily be sold.
The proposals, which the commission could carry out on its own, are aimed specifically at investments known as derivatives, because their value is derived from an underlying security, but one result would be a new dimension of disclosure by the entire fund industry. 
 "The S.E.C. has indeed confirmed that speculative use of derivatives by some funds does raise significant investor protection issues," said a senior aide to the House securities subcommittee headed by Edward J. Markey, a Massachusetts Democrat.
Mr. Markey, along with Jack Fields of Texas, the panel's ranking Republican, asked the commission in June to study the use of derivatives by mutual funds after a series of derivative-related losses at funds investing in Treasury bonds and money market securities, generally regarded as among the most conservative of investments.
Derivatives are specially designed investments whose value is tied to, or derived from, the performance of an underlying asset or market, like a commodity or interest rates. While use of these investments may be intended to reduce risk, they often have the opposite effect, increasing the volatility and risk of portfolios.
In its response, the commission said that while funds' use of derivatives "appears to be limited" to relatively few funds and fund types, "they raise investor protection issues that merit serious consideration."
The 33-page document, which took the form of a memorandum to the S.E.C.'s chairman, Arthur Levitt Jr., from the agency's investment management division, said consideration should be given to requiring prospectuses to contain a numerical measure of portfolio risk.
Acknowledging the difficulty of this task, the agency listed three possible gauges. One is "duration," a measure of how much a change in interest rates affects a portfolio of fixed-income securities. Another is "standard deviation," or how volatile a fund's total return is during specified periods of time. The third is a fund's "beta," or its risk relative to that of the overall market.
Other possibilities, the committee aide said, might include graphs, tables, charts or scales as part of an effort to give investors information they can digest, instead of giving them still another layer of legalese.
A second recommendation would reverse a 1992 commission action that raised the limit, now 15 percent, on illiquid holdings by funds other than money-market funds. The measure was intended to make it easier for small businesses to raise capital. Previously, a 10 percent ceiling had applied. An illiquid holding is one that cannot be easily sold for roughly the same amount at which the fund has been carrying it on its books.
Certain mortgage derivatives have recently proved quite illiquid, as interest rates have climbed.
The commission also proposed reviewing the application of statutory restrictions on leverage to derivatives. Leverage magnifies gains or losses. And it proposed that the agency seek legislation to improve its ability to force funds to supply data needed to monitor derivatives adequately.
Paul S. Stevens, general counsel of the Investment Company Institute, the main industry trade group, said it supported the outlines of the S.E.C.'s recommendations but wanted more details.
Mr. Stevens expressed some enthusiasm for the proposal to lower the ceiling on illiquid holdings; few funds took advantage of their ability two years ago to move up to 15 percent of assets. But Mr. Stevens was dubious that the commission could adequately measure fund risks.
"The industry is somewhat skeptical -- and virtually everyone I've spoken to is skeptical -- that you can capture all risks in one statistical measure," he said.

HEADLINE: Rearranging Drugstore Shelves

BYLINE: By MILT FREUDENHEIM 

 BODY:
In a surge of activity not seen in years, at least seven big pharmaceutical companies are pressing for Federal permission to sell some of their most important drugs without a doctor's prescription.
With profit margins on prescription drugs already squeezed by the growing pressure to lower health care costs, the companies are scrambling to win Food and Drug Administration approval for over-the-counter versions of prescription drugs. This strategy is especially attractive for drugs whose patents have expired, making them vulnerable to brutal price slashing as generic copies come on the market. 
 By selling milder versions of these drugs, and advertising heavily, manufacturers create new products that often have bigger revenues than the original drugs ever did.
An F.D.A. advisory panel recently recommended that conditional approval be given to two new nonprescription heartburn medicines. Each is a milder version of a widely used ulcer drug: Tagamet from SmithKline Beechamand Pepcid from Merck & Company. Applications are also pending for over-the-counter versions of important drugs made by Johnson & Johnson, Upjohn and Wellcome Holdings.
Glaxo Holdings plans to file next month for a nonprescription version of its ulcer treatment, Zantac, the world's best-selling drug. And Marion Merrell Dow says it will formally request over-the-counter approval later this year for Nicorettes, an aid in stopping smoking.
"We are breaking new ground, and the activity level is very high," said Dr. Michael Weintraub, director of the F.D.A.'s Office of Over-the-Counter Drug Evaluation. "It's one of the phenomena of the 90's."
Although Federal officials do not provide numbers, industry experts say that at least 16 new over-the-counter medications are awaiting approval by the Food and Drug Administration, a process that can sometimes take years.
The new products would join a market for over-the-counter drugs that already totals $12.2 billion in the United States and $18 billion overseas. Drug makers spent $1.57 billion last year advertising these medications, Michael Perlmutter, a pharmaceutical consultant at Kline & Company, said.
In a triumph of marketing, Warner-Lambert multiplied the sales of the antihistamine Benadryl tenfold in nine years to an estimated $155 million this year, up from $14 million in the 12 months before nonprescription Benadryl was introduced in 1985. Warner-Lambert spent $31 million last year to advertise a dozen Benadryl variations .
The over-the-counter products often greatly expand a market. Johnson & Johnson reported that 18 million women were using over-the-counter treatments, like its Monistat 7, for vaginal yeast infections; in 1991, when only prescription versions were available, 12 million women used them.
And Tylenol, which went over the counter when Dwight D. Eisenhower was President, is now the nonprescription champion with $855 million in United States sales, helped by an ad budget of $144 million last year. It is packaged in no fewer than 24 varieties. "Anything with the name Tylenol on it, retailers will love," Mr. Perlmutter said.
Sometimes the nonprescription version of a drug is close to the original, but more often it is less potent and has a label stating that it is intended for treating a problem, like heartburn or headache, that an individual can identify without professional help.
The over-the-counter option is especially attractive to drug makers as health maintenance organizations and other cost-conscious buyers increasingly force down prescription drug prices by favoring low-cost generics or newer, more cost-effective drugs.
Health insurers and health maintenance organizations have little influence on over-the-counter drugs since such medication is usually not covered under their plans. Still, they and governments, seeking to cut spending on both medical products and services, welcome the prospect of having more drugs available without the need of visiting a doctor and getting a prescription.
For the drug makers, pretax operating profits on over-the-counter drugs average a robust 15 percent, according to Samuel D. Isaly of Mehta & Isaly Worldwide Healthcare Research. That is well below the 25 percent average on prescription drugs, which often lose up to 70 percent of their market share when their patent protection ends. It is also triple the typical 5 percent operating margin of a generic drug.
Reflecting their growing importance, nonprescription drugs figured prominently in a number of recent big pharmaceutical company deals.
SmithKline Beecham of Britain bought the Sterling Winthrop consumer lines from Eastman Kodak for $2.9 billion in August. SmithKline then sold the North American portion to Bayer A.G. of Germany for $1 billion on Sept. 12.
Consumer brands were important in the recent agreement by American Home Products to acquire American Cyanamid for $9.7 billion. And earlier this year, Warner-Lambert and Wellcome P.L.C. of Britain announced a joint marketing venture for over-the-counter products. They are seeking F.D.A. approval for over-the-counter sales of Zovirax, a Wellcome treatment for genital herpes that is one of the world's 10 best-selling drugs.
The competition among brands can be fierce and, adding heat, big pharmaceutical companies like Johnson & Johnson are fending off copycat "private label" products of mass merchandisers like Wal-Mart and drugstore chains like Revco. The private labels have seized 17 percent of the market.
The manufacturers face three choices when exclusive patent protection runs out on a big drug, said Stephen W. Schondelmeyer, a pharmaceutical economist at the University of Minnesota: patenting new prescription-dosage formulations; marketing or licensing lower-priced generic prescription versions, and "if the safety profile is reasonable," taking the drug over-the-counter.
Some companies try all three approaches. SmithKline Beecham, for example, is sponsoring a generic version of Tagamet, its ulcer treatment, and also seeking Federal approval for a milder over-the-counter version to treat heartburn.
Heading the F.D.A.'s over-the-counter waiting list are Tagamet, Pepcid and Nizoral, an antifungal shampoo made by the Janssen Pharmaceutica unit of Johnson & Johnson.
Nizoral was recently recommended for over-the-counter sale by a Food and Drug Administration advisory panel. The panel chairman, Randy Juhl, dean of the school of pharmacy of the University of Pittsburgh, said the group recommended conditional approval to Tagamet and Pepcid. Before the F.D.A. gives final approval, he said, the panel said the agency should have more information about the effectiveness of the Pepcid product, and potential problems if Tagamet was used with other drugs.
In another case, the Upjohn Company was told that it had "more work to do" on its application for Rogaine, a hair loss treatment, Dr. Juhl said. Unlike remedies for colds and headaches, he noted, it takes months of use to tell if Rogaine works and, if it does, customers will "use it forever," raising issues about long-term effects and the occasional case when baldness may be a symptom of a serious illness requiring a doctor's care.
An Upjohn spokesman said the company was working with the F.D.A. to try to meet such concerns. "Rogaine will ultimately be converted to over the counter," said Ronald Nordmann, a health care analyst with Deerfield Management, a money management firm, "but first, Upjohn will have to present better data."
In the next few months, the Food and Drug Administration plans to issue regulations requiring the makers of over-the-counter drugs to report cases of adverse reactions, already a requirement for prescription medicines, Dr. Juhl said.
"When kids start to abuse a type of cough syrup," he added, "we would like to pick up on those trends early on."

CORRECTION-DATE: September 29, 1994, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
A chart in Business Day on Tuesday with an article about a number of pharmaceutical companies seeking Federal approval to sell drugs over the counter misidentified the manufacturer of two prescription drugs. Both Pepcid and Dolobid are made by Merck & Company, not Johnson & Johnson. If over-the-counter versions of the drugs are approved by the Food and Drug Administration, they will be marketed in a joint venture with Johnson & Johnson.

HEADLINE: THE HEALTH CARE DEBATE: IN THEIR OWN WORDS;
Why Health Care Fizzled: Too Little Time and Too Much Politics

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
Everyone in the White House, in Congress and in the lobbyists' offices in Washington now agrees that major health care restructuring is dead for 1994. But people disagree on precisely what killed what might have been the boldest legislative initiative in decades.
Talking to those involved in the debate that consumed the first 18 months of the Clinton Administration is like taking depositions from witnesses in a complicated legal case: everything depends on where they stand. 
 So, too, the story of what happened to the health care bill is colored by policy goals, ideology and political ambition, from the President's plan to the alternatives from left, right and center in Congress.
What follows are comments by people who were intimately involved in the debate on health care, each offering his or her own view of events.
 
John J. Motley
VICE PRESIDENT, NATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT BUSINESS
The adamant opposition of small business to any requirement that employers be required to finance their workers' health insurance coverage was a major stumbling block for those in favor of universal coverage.
The left killed health care reform by overreaching, by not being willing to accept a consensus approach, by not trying to put together a proposal in the middle of the political spectrum.
The seeds of the demise of the President's proposal were sown in the very early days of the Administration, within the task force that drafted his proposal. The left in this debate, the real liberals, were able to seize control of the creation of the proposal. They seriously overreached and developed a proposal that was too large and too expensive, that gave too big a role to the Government and that simply couldn't pass.
In the end, no one in the business community, no one in the opposition, believed the Administration or the Democratic leadership of Congress when they said they were willing to compromise. We feared the endgame so much that many of us were afraid we would be snookered. No matter what passed in the House and the Senate, we feared that the bill would be rewritten in conference.
 
Representative Richard A. Gephardt
DEMOCRAT OF MISSOURI
 Mr. Gephardt, the House majority leader, was a key ally in the White House effort.
It became very clear very quickly that there was little or no support for meaningful reform among Republicans in the House. They have a very different vision of what health care reform should be. With very few exceptions, Republicans cannot look their constituents in the eye and say that they made a good-faith effort to give health security to every American.
Next year we will have to figure out if there is a more modest and incremental way to pursue the same goals. I don't know if there is or not.
 
Senator Edward M. Kennedy
DEMOCRAT OF MASSACHUSETTS
Senator Kennedy heads the Senate Labor Committee, one of the most liberal in the Senate, which passed a bill similar in many respects to President Clinton's proposal.
Time was our principal enemy, and time just ran out, with the Republican blind obstructionism and gridlock that has been so apparent in recent weeks.
I think there will be a strong effort at the opening of Congress. The issue will be very live and very current and very compelling. It's not going to disappear and it's not going to go away. This is an issue that is current and real and absolutely penetrating in terms of every family condition, and that just isn't going to go away.
 
John C. Rother
LEGISLATIVE DIRECTOR, AMERICAN
ASSOCIATION OF RETIRED PERSONS
Mr. Rother's group has long been committed to health care restructuring, with particular concern about extending prescription drug benefits and long-term care coverage to the elderly.
The death knell came on Aug. 11, the day the House defeated the Administration's anti-crime bill. It meant the leadership in Congress and the Administration had to completely divert themselves at a critical time, when time was the one thing we couldn't give up. That was the final obstacle.
The issue was made partisan both by Administration officials, in the way they proposed the bill, and by the Republican leaders of Congress, with their effort to bring down Clinton by defeating health care.
The various delays in bringing the bill forward gave the vested interests time to mobilize here in Washington and at the grass roots, to cast doubt and raise fears about the whole idea of health reform. Congress could not act until the Administration finished, and the institutional structure of Congress, with several committees claiming jurisdiction, contributed to the delay.
The President's proposal was very difficult to explain to people. It was difficult to generate real enthusiasm for it. People were not sure they understood it. We needed to explain better to the American people why it was in their interest to have this legislation passed.
At the start of this debate, in spring of 1993, there was clearly a zone of achievable compromise. The key disagreement was over how to pay for universal coverage. Some Republicans were willing to support an employer mandate, and it could have happened. But Presidential politics and Republican loyalty to Senator Dole made that impossible.
 
Bill Gradison
PRESIDENT, HEALTH INSURANCE
ASSOCIATION OF AMERICA
 The association, which made its case in the "Harry and Louise" commercials, opposed premium caps, mandatory insurance purchasing alliances and other provisions in the President's plan.
We failed to read the lessons of history on health reform. We're likely to get to universal coverage more quickly a step at a time rather than in one fell swoop. Regrettably, all or none often means none.
It takes time to build consensus for any major legislative proposal. It took six years to pass a crime bill. It took more than six years to pass Medicare and Medicaid. . . .
We are going to do a lot of introspection around here in the weeks ahead. I've sent signals to the White House that after they finish beating up on us, if that's what they choose to do, we'd like to examine ways to work more harmoniously together than we did in the last year or two, when they chose unwisely to demonize the health insurance industry.
To me, the turning point, the greatest disappointment, was when the White House dumped on Representative Jim Cooper and his bill. Instead of sitting down with him and looking for areas of agreement, they just leveled 16-inch guns on him. That showed how difficult it would be to achieve compromise.
 
Dr. P. John Seward
CHAIRMAN, AMERICAN MEDICAL ASSOCIATION
The group, which represents almost half of the nation's doctors, supports the goal of universal coverage but opposes government regulation of doctors' fees.
The main reason that health system reform didn't pass is simply the complexity of the nation's health care system, and the complexity of the solutions proposed. Rather than blaming interest groups, the Administration or Congress, we have to learn lessons from this exercise.
We never thought it would be a simple job. The issues are very complex. They do not lend themselves to simple answers.
The main question right now is whether comprehensive reform is viable, or whether it should be done incrementally. Comprehensive reform is still an appropriate goal, but incremental steps may be more doable. We will continue our commitment. The status quo is not appropriate.
 
Senator Bob Dole
REPUBLICAN OF KANSAS
Mr. Dole, the minority leader and potential Presidential candidate in 1996, initially seemed open to major health care restructuring but moved further and further to the right as the year went on.
I think the American people, after all the debate and discussion, understood it and wouldn't buy it. It's just too complex, too beauracratic, too expensive. That's the key. The more the American people understood it, the more trouble it was in. Because it was too costly and too bureaucratic and too much government intervention.
I blame the Clinton Administration for coming out with this big bureaucratic nightmare and then not really making any effort to try to work it out with anybody in Congress except the Democrats. I met a lot of people at the Kansas state fair who didn't have any lobbyists and weren't represented by lobbyists, and, if I recall correctly, it was something like 86 to 14 for Congress not to try to pass it this year. So I think there were a lot of working people, small people who go to fairs, who weren't buying the product.
 
Senator Paul Wellstone
DEMOCRAT OF MINNESOTA
Mr. Wellstone, one of the most liberal members of the Senate, supports a Canadian-style system in which the Government would provide health insurance coverage for all Americans.
What killed health care? I think the unholy mix of money, power and politics. Communication technology became the main weapon -- just an all-out ad campaign that spread fear throughout the country. It was very effective. And the second part is unprecedented giving of money from the health industry to senators and representatives at the very time we were considering this legislation. And that money was not given out of the goodness of anybody's heart. It was to buy time and buy access.
So in a sense with health care, democracy for the few triumphed over democracy for the many. In fact, I would argue that health care makes the best case for campaign finance reform that you could ever find.
The interesting thing to me is that the universal coverage basically doesn't strike at the perogatives of the health care industry. . . . But when you got to the issue of cost containment you had the Congressional Budget Office telling us over and over there is only one way to contain cost, either a) you do single payer or b) you've got to put some controls over what insurance companies could charge.
The health care industry, with some notable exceptions, used its full range of power to block this. Not all the Republicans, but a large part of the Republican Party was on an all-out mission to block any reform, right up to the point of threats of filibuster, of thousands of ammendments and threats of essentially bringing this entire process to a grinding halt.
Even though health care reform is an idea whose time has come and come and come and come this century, it will still come back because none of the problems that people face every day are going to go away. They are only going to grow. And the second part of it, this issue of cost containment, is fundamentally important. If we don't contain costs, we are going to be in huge trouble economically. There will be reform, but not now, I'm sad to say, and angry to say.
 
Representative Jim McDermott
DEMOCRAT OF WASHINGTON
A liberal Democrat, Mr. McDermott is a leader of the single-payer forces in the House and an influential member of the Ways and Means Committee, which produced a bill siimilar in some respects to the President's plan.
People's fear of change was mobilized by a massive campaign paid for by the vested interests. Insurance companies took premium dollars and went out and advertised against the best interests of their patients. The American people ought to be outraged by that, but apparently they've not made the connection.
You can add other issues; the White House certainly underestimated the difficulty, in the timing issues with respect especially to Congress. The Republicans had a political agenda of not wanting the President to succeed.
And this is a complicated issue. It's like a spider web. You can't touch anything without shaking the whole web, so when you start talking about Medicare you start talking about medical schools. Everybody's for reform -- as long as you're not talking about the piece I'm involved in. You've got a $950 billion pot and nobody wants to lose a nickel. . . .
My belief was the President was absolutely correct that universal coverage was the issue. And once they started talk about incremental reform, I knew this baby's over -- we're not going to get anything that's worth the powder to blow it up.
That was a sad day for me. Up to that point I thought we had a chance.
 
Bill Kristol
CHAIRMAN, PROJECT FOR A REPUBLICAN FUTURE
A former top aide to Vice President Dan Quayle and a leading conservative strategist, Mr. Kristol saw the health care debate as an opportunity to define the Republican Party as holding the line against government expansion.
Clinton's plan failed because of its very nature. He thought the middle class would accept an expansion of government in return for quote health security, and I think he radically underestimated the American people's mistrust of big government liberalism. He thought he was elected to carry forward, to enact the final piece of the New Deal, Great Society agenda, and it turns out the American public is more interested in reversing course now rather than continuing that course.
Clinton's allies will talk about all the tactical errors and mistakes in timing and details and legislative obstacles and interst group opposition, but I think they all miss the fundamental point.
I think the key moment was when the quality argument came to the fore. Americans might have tolerated a little more bureaucracy or a few more regulations for the sake of covering the uninsured. But when it became clear that the Clinton plan threatened the choice of doctors and the quality of medical care available to Middle America, or available to most Americans, they -- correctly in my judgment -- turned against it.
 
 Dr. Paul M. Ellwood
PRESIDENT, JACKSON HOLE GROUP
The group, a conclave in Jackson Hole, Wyo., composed of health care experts from academia, the health insurance industry and other policy circles, favors a health care strategy known as managed competition, which tries to mix free market economics with government regulation.
No one killed health care reform. It died in the White House with the first set of proposals, the Administration's original proposals. We were never able to recover from the first Clinton plan. It assumed too much about how consumers, large purchasers of health care, providers of care and the health care system in general would behave under a new set of ground rules.
It was assumption built on assumption that really wasn't supportable by actual experience.
Managed competition is going very well in the private sector. Unfortunately, we did not get managed competition out of the White House. We got an elaborate regulatory scheme out of the White House.
One other thing went wrong. Trashing the moderates was a terrible mistake. The Administration did a lot of that, as did some members of Congress, particularly those who supported a single-payer plan or the original Clinton plan. Instead of starting in the middle and working out, people way to the left and way to the right worked toward the middle, and by the time they got there we ran out of time.
 
Representative Jim Cooper
DEMOCRAT OF TENNESSEE
Mr. Cooper sponsored a pared down, bipartisan health insurance proposal, which did not promise universal coverage or require employers to pay for their workers' insurance. Like other measures, it was still stalled at the end of the session.
I think the Administration's key mistake was not being bipartisan. They only had one Republican in America supporting their approach. They should have reached out to moderates in both parties in order to build a broad national consensus for health reform.
Partisan approaches are doomed to fail on any major issue. What was the Jefferson quote? "Great initiatives cannot be built on slender majorities." You have to have a consensus. We were always proud that our bill had bipartisan support.
We could still have passed an excellent bill if Congress had returned from the recess with clear resolve. Too many people were discouraged by the partisanship and the limited time available.
 
Ira C. Magaziner
SENIOR ADVISER TO THE PRESIDENT FOR POLICY DEVELOPMENT
Mr. Magaziner and Hillary Rodham Clinton were principal architects of the White House health care proposal.
Oppponents of reform, the same interest groups that have historically opposed change, fought to defeat health care reform. While some paid lip service to the goals of reform, they spared no effort to protect the status quo. And they succeeded.
Although they failed to convince the American public that the fundamental goals of universal coverage and cost containment were wrong, they succeeded in scaring and misleading the public that the bills proposed by the President and various Congressional committees would threaten what they like about their own health care. They scared the public -- confusing them about whether they would be able to see their own doctor and whether the health care system would turn into a giant government bureaucracy.
It is interesting to note that despite the hundreds of millions of dollars spent to defeat health care reform, support for the President's goal of universal coverage remains strong. . . .
Why did these special interests triumph in the face of a united President and Democratic leadership? They succeeded through a combination of massive special interest money spent on advertising, delay, distortion and exploitation of the complexity of the Congressional process. . . .
Change is difficult in the current Washington environment. As is so often true, the forces of the status quo have the upper hand. Were mistakes made? Absolutely. Could the Administration have done a better job? Certainly. But the President, his Administration, many members of Congress and numerous groups and individuals fought as hard as they could to pass reform. . . .
But this battle is far from over. The efforts made and the lessons learned will contribute ultimately to reform of our health system. We will continue the fight to put the public interest ahead of the special interests and provide real health security to all Americans.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Ira C. Magaziner with Health and Human Services Secretary Donna E. Shalala, left, and Hillary Rodham Clinton in 1993. (Stephen Crowley/The New York Times); Representative Jim Cooper, Democrat of Tennessee, talked to Audria Mae Bowman last week in Kingsport, Tenn. (Alan S. Weiner for The New York Times)

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (94%);

HEADLINE: At the Summit, It's All Uphill

BYLINE: By Stephen Sestanovich; Stephen Sestanovich is vice president for Russian and Eurasian affairs at the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
Bill Clinton's meetings with Boris Yeltsin have been, more than anything else, a series of photo-op's for reform -- a chance for America to vote its confidence (however shaky) in Russia's progress toward democracy and free markets. The summit talks today and tomorrow are different. Their real content is, for a change, diplomacy.
The increasing political and economic stability over which President Yeltsin presides means that the two leaders can spend less time on ritual heavy breathing about the abstract principles that unite them and more on the concrete policy disagreements that divide them. 
 Disagreement has become almost the rule of Russian-American relations, and while it may seem a sign of diplomatic normality, it has its dangerous side as well. In a setting of pervasive disagreement, it is more likely that one or both governments will make decisions that damage larger common interests.
The list of Russian-American disagreements includes almost all of the most important issues of international politics. What was meant as a step toward reconciling Moscow's and Washington's policies on Bosnia -- the creation of a Contact Group of outside powers -- has instead pushed their positions further apart. Russian officials have expressed incomprehension at American proposals to lift the arms embargo against the Bosnian Muslims; U.S. officials say the Russians have been duped by President Slobodan Milosevic of Serbia.
Peacekeeping in the former Soviet Union reveals similar differences. Seen from Washington, Moscow is sabotaging peace between Armenia and Azerbaijan by blocking proposals, prepared under the auspices of the Conference on Security and Cooperation in Europe, for a multinational peacekeeping force. The Russians retort that the Western plan could never be carried out, that only Russian forces can do the hard work of policing a settlement.
Arms control disagreements lengthen the list. The U.S. wants Russia to forswear weapons exports to known bad actors; Russia wants to sell to Iran and Iraq. The U.S. wants to stick with the terms of the 1990 treaty on conventional forces in Europe; the Russians say that it was negotiated in another era, when both the Warsaw Pact and Soviet Union still existed, and is irrelevant to the problems they face today.
Of all the issues that divide Moscow and Washington, none is more important than NATO's future. Last fall, the U.S. fashioned the Partnership for Peace, in an effort to defer the issue of accepting new members into the Western alliance. Now, barely three months after Russia joined this new grouping, American officials are reopening the broader question of expanding NATO itself.
In response to German calls for such a step, Vice President Al Gore said three weeks ago that NATO will take up the issue this fall. And the State Department's new European policy chief, Richard Holbrooke, is reportedly lining up support in the Administration to accelerate NATO membership for Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic.
The Russians' view of this issue could not be plainer. A Yeltsin adviser said last week that "99.9 percent of the Russian elite is against it." They don't buy tortured explanations about how Russia need not be isolated just because it is left out.
With this list of disagreements growing, it is a tribute to both sides' interest in good relations that each is willing to excuse so many of the other's transgressions as the product of domestic politics. Russian and American officials have acquired a sympathetic and sophisticated understanding of each other's vulnerability to special-interest groups and mobilized lobbies.
While making clear its objections to major arms transfers to Iran, the U.S. sees Russia's policy as a commercial necessity rather than a hostile strategic choice. Similarly, Moscow's courtship of Mr. Milosevic is resented in Washington as a sign of timidity in taking on Russian nationalists, not as a single-minded effort to thwart U.S. policy.
The Russians also read President Clinton's policies as a function of his domestic imperatives -- the need to look tough, to record successes, to protect his Congressional majority in November and get re-elected in 1996. In this spirit, one recent article in Izvestia portrayed the summit meeting as a chance for the two leaders to compare "headaches."
Yet the most important Russian-American disagreements do not have a domestic origin. They involve each side's response to international rather than domestic difficulties and need to be addressed in these terms.
Moscow's policy in the "near abroad," for example, is above all the expression of a strategic desire to create stability in Russia's own neighborhood. This desire would almost certainly be played out in more ambitious ways were it not checked by domestic political considerations: Russia simply can't afford the burden of reabsorbing Ukraine or Belarus. Russian polls show clearly that the public does not want to pay for empire. For this and other reasons, their leaders are learning to live with uncertainty and disorder.
The U.S. policy that troubles Russia most -- bringing Eastern Europe into NATO -- is also a response to an authentic international problem: how to fill the vacuum left by the end of the cold war.
Unfortunately, neither Washington nor Moscow is handling the issue in a serious way. American explanations sometimes actually pretend that expanding NATO has no military implications of any kind. It is described instead as an innocent attempt to bolster the new democracies by showing that we consider them part of the West.
To Russian leaders, such talk is frivolous or dishonest. Discussion of this issue in the alliance does focus on the wrong questions. When NATO bureaucrats sit down to refine the membership criteria that will dictate which Eastern European countries are eligible to join the alliance, they don't ask who faces a real external threat, but who has established effective civilian control of the military.
As a result, NATO expansion is being debated without reference to what ought to be the only truly serious test: its likely impact on European security. U.S. officials lamely explain that they can't address the question of security because they don't know what Europe's future security problems will be. (Not knowing the illness, they are nevertheless certain of the cure!)
In this light, accelerating a decision on NATO expansion looks foolish. Yet, in trying to slow the process, Russia is responding with its own foolishness. Its proposals to make the Conference on Security and Cooperation in Europe the supreme institution of European security -- above NATO -- have too many echoes of Mikhail Gorbachev's "common European home." And what content they have discredits them further: Moscow will hardly get a fair hearing in the West by conducting a propaganda campaign to handcuff NATO, whether or not it expands.
Russia's ability to create a European security order of which it is a full member will depend not on flashy proposals but on how it treats its neighbors over the long term.
The most powerful argument Russian leaders can make is that new security alliances should start from the new realities of Russian policy. Only on this basis will they be able to defer an expansion of NATO for which there is no present need.
The growing success of President Yeltsin's reforms has liberated both sides from last year's worries -- that we were too closely identified with him, and he with us. The freedom to disagree is welcome. Even better would be signs that Moscow and Washington are grappling seriously with the problems that threaten their new partnership.

HEADLINE: MISSION TO HAITI;
Haiti's Priest-President Faces A Hostile Catholic Hierarchy

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Barely a day goes by without Haitians taking to the streets to cheer the impending return of their elected President. But from the Roman Catholic Church hierarchy that educated and ordained the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide, there has come no word or gesture of rejoicing or support -- only silence.
To the President's many supporters within the church, it is clear that the mere thought of Father Aristide back in power is nearly as alarming to Haiti's Roman Catholic bishops as it is to the Haitian military. 
 In the past, the hierarchy has tried to prevent Father Aristide from running for office, condemned his presidency before he was even sworn in, supported the military leaders who overthrew him and opposed American efforts to restore him to power.
"Most of the bishops have sold their souls to the devil" and now fear that retribution, either at the hands of their flock or from Father Aristide, is on the way, said the Rev. Gerard Jean-Juste, an enthusiastic Aristide supporter. "People don't forget, and that's why the bishops are scared."
Just last month, Haiti's 11 bishops issued a statement saying any military intervention by the United tates to restore Father Aristide was "scandalous and immoral" and "makes us tremble with indignation."
Several of them then joined Lieut. Gen. Raoul Cedras and other military leaders at a Mass intended for "prayer and reflection" on the United Nations sanctions that were intended to force the military to step down.
More recently, resentment of the hierarchy's unwillingness to speak out on behalf of Father Aristide has been running especially high because of the death of the Rev. Jean-Marie Vincent, a close friend and ally of Father Aristide who was shot to death late last month, apparently by Government gunmen. When Bishop Joseph Lafontant, the apostolic administrator of the Port-au-Prince diocese, showed up at Father Vincent's funeral, he quickly found his presence was not welcome.
"Why are you here?" one mourner hissed at Bishop Lafontant as he approached Father Vincent's body, according to a priest who witnessed the scene. "You are one of the killers."
For his own part, Father Aristide has often been no less suspicious and critical of his church superiors. In his autobiography, published in 1992, he called Francois-Wolff Ligonde, the Archbishop of Port-au-Prince, "a zealous servant of Macoutism," referring to the Tontons Macoute, who terrorized civilians in the Duvalier era. Father Aristide said others were equally guilty, exempting only Bishop Willy Romelus, his sole ally in the hierarchy.
In the 29 years of the Duvalier family dictatorship, Father Aristide wrote, "bishops and Macoutes walked arm in arm, defending the same causes, but using a different vocabulary to mask their collusion for the benefit of the oligarchy." And in an apparent reference to Haiti's current military-backed Government, he added that any time a bishop "supports generals who murder liberty, he commits a crime."
Three of Port-au-Prince's Roman Catholic bishops were asked for comment about Father Aristide and his relations with the church, and none responded. The bishops' conference has made no comment about events here since its message in August criticizing American policy in Haiti, which came after more than a year of silence.
Nevertheless, Father Aristide's supporters within the church suggest, there is enough of a record to demonstrate that the hierarchy is hostile to him for both ecclesiastical and political reasons.
From the moment in 1982 that he was ordained a member of the Salesians, who are dedicated to the education of youth and parish ministry, Father Aristide's zeal to defend and fight for the country's poor, the same people who would eventually elect him to office, convinced his superiors that he was both a radical and an undisciplined priest.
The Vatican, the only body in the world that has diplomatic relations with the military Government, has also expressed doubts about Father Aristide through the papal nuncio here. Tensions have run so high that early in 1991, after a failed coup attempt, a crowd of Haitians, thinking the Vatican had had a hand in the effort to prevent Father Aristide from taking office, chased an assistant to the nuncio and ripped his clothes off.
"He is a problem for them, but not for the Haitian people," said Necker Dessables, executive secretary of the Commission for Justice and Peace, the human rights wing of the Roman Catholic Church. "Questions of obedience and canon law are perhaps primary to the bishops, but secondary to believers."
Indeed, Father Aristide appears to enjoy cordial relations with most of his fellow priests and the church faithful. "It is not Aristide who has distanced himself from the church, but the bishops who have distanced themselves from the people," said the Rev. Frantz-Michel Grandoit, a Dominican priest and university professor here.
Father Aristide has particularly close ties with the progressive wing of the church, the "ti legliz," or "little church." During the last 15 years, more than 1,000 "ecclesiastical base communities" have sprung up in Haiti to preach the gospel of liberation theology, which Father Aristide also endorses, and to work for social change.
"Ours are communities of faith that fight for justice, human rights and life," said Veronique Nelson, a member of a coalition of base communities. "Father Aristide works for that too, so we are very happy to think of his return."
But that sort of politicized approach to religion is precisely what the bishops do not want and fear will grow when Father Aristide resumes the presidency. "Direct democracy gives people too much autonomy, and they fear that in the church," Father Grandoit said. "They worry about a contamination of the church."
In 1988, it was conflicts over these and related issues that led to Father Aristide's expulsion from the Salesians. The document expelling him complained that he had engaged in "incitement to hatred and violence and a glorification of class struggle" and accused him of "profanation of the liturgy" and "a lack of sincerity and of a religious and priestly consciousness.
"Father Jean-Bertrand Aristide has always preferred to distance himself from the concrete demands of community," the decree added, "becoming a protagonist of the destabilization of the community of the faithful." What is worse, the Salesians concluded, "he has done so in a constant, public and incisive manner so that the organs of the press and groups of varied origins present him as the 'leader of the popular church' in Haiti."
Then, two years later, after Father Aristide had won election with two-thirds of the vote but just before he was sworn in, Archbishop Ligonde expressed doubts about Father Aristide's ability to govern Haiti. "Is Socialist Bolshevism going to triumph?" he asked darkly in a homily delivered from the cathedral here.
"Fear is sending a chill down the spines of many fathers and mothers of families," said Bishop Ligonde, who had presided at the wedding of Jean-Claude Duvalier, who took over from his father as a dictator.
He also expressed fear that Haiti was heading toward "an authoritarian political regime" that would install "a political police" and resort to "a campaign of denunciation."
Despite the residue of hostility left by such incidents, some of Father Aristide's supporters say his relations with the hierarchy may not be so difficult when he returns to Haiti next month. Father Aristide has contributed to that feeling with radio speeches calling for reconciliation and condemning the desire for revenge.
"He is more mature, redeemed, a democratic man," Father Jean-Juste said of Father Aristide. "He has learned a lot in the United States. If he can give amnesty to General Cedras, then why not the bishops?"

GRAPHIC: Photo: Roman Catholic parishioners attended services at the National Cathedral on Sunday in the Haitian capital. (Librado Romero/The New York Times) (pg. A8)

HEADLINE: ACCORD MAY GIVE NYNEX CUSTOMERS A BREAK ON RATES

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD 

 BODY:
Nynex and the staff of the New York State Public Service Commission announced yesterday that they had struck a tentative deal for a new kind of regulation that would give customers a break on rates and remove almost all limits on how much the company could earn.
The accord, if approved soon by the Public Service Commission itself, would make New York the first state in the nation to let long-distance carriers compete easily with local phone companies in handling medium-distance calls. Michigan plans to allow such competition beginning in 1996. In theory, this means that customers could end up dealing with three different phone companies: one for local calls, one for medium-range calls and one for long-distance calls. 
 The agreement announced yesterday is part of an effort by New York State to deregulate the local telephone business. It would end traditional "cost-based" regulation, in which the commission sets a limit on profits and then sets rates that will produce those profits. Instead, prices for services will be regulated and the company will be free to make as much money as it can by selling more of those services more economically. About 28 states have taken steps toward such a system, called "incentive regulation."
If the plan is approved, Nynex would cut customers' bills by a total of about $1 billion between 1995 and 1999, about 3 percent of its revenues. In 1996, rates would be 6 percent lower on average than they would have been.
"We're looking at this as a real win-win," said Richard A. Jalkut, president and chief executive of Nynex New York, the company's New York subsidiary, which serves 7.3 million customers with 10 million lines, or about 90 percent of the telephone market in the state.
Many outsiders also praised the deal. "These companies are all inefficient, and Nynex is probably the most inefficient," said Raymond E. Liguori, a telephone industry analyst at Salomon Brothers. Under traditional regulation, he said, a phone company has little incentive to cut costs because the savings go back to customers through reduced rates. "Under price regulation, they'll get to keep some of it," he said.
Nynex had 40,000 employees at the beginning of this year and reiterated yesterday that it planned to reduce its payroll by 13,000 by 1996.
If the deal is approved, consumers would notice a difference beginning on Jan. 1, when the extra charge for touch-tone service at residential numbers, now 50 cents a month, would be dropped.
The direction that prices would take in the absence of an agreement is not clear; some analysts think they would decline anyway because of competition or the threat of competition. Nynex has already lost its monopoly status in New York, with several other companies offering local telephone service, mainly to office buildings in Manhattan.
And beginning next year customers could sign up with other phone companies for the medium-range calls -- like those between, say, New Rochelle, N.Y., and Manhattan -- just as they now sign up with a long distance company for calls between New Rochelle and New Orleans.
But AT&T, MCI and some others that might be expected to enter that market said quickly yesterday that they would oppose the deal.
Those companies already do business with local phone companies on long-distance calls -- if the customer in New Rochelle calls Washington, D.C., the long-distance company would accept the call from Nynex and deliver it to the local company in Washington and pay a handling charge to each company. But the long-distance carriers are complaining that the handling charge Nynex wants for the medium-distance calls, which would be handled by Nynex at both ends, is so high that they could not compete with Nynex itself.
"The proposed plan," said Marc Rosen, an AT&T vice president, in a statement, "would effectively lock in Nynex's entrenched monopoly and lock out competitors at least until the end of the century."
But another long-distance competitor that was a party to the negotiations, Sprint, supported the deal.
Most rate cases in New York are settled through agreements among the regulated utility, consumer groups and other interested parties, and the commission staff; the commission itself then accepts the deal, rejects it or tinkers with the package and sends it back to the parties to accept or amend. The last two deals between the staff and Nynex were approved by the commission and the one before that was rejected.
That is little guide to how the commission will act in this case because the issue is not really the rates but the means of regulation itself. Still, this agreement, which has been under negotiation among 40 parties for nearly two years, follows a direction sought by the commission, toward deregulation.
The plan, intended as a bridge toward a period of even greater competition, includes several provisions designed to protect low-income consumers. It also allows for the commission to modify the arrangement if the company achieves what the staff termed "exceptional" profits.
Wall Street evidently thought Nynex could make money under the proposed deal; the stock closed yesterday at $38, up $2, in heavy trading.
The deal may have implications for the company in Washington since Nynex has applied to the Justice Department for permission to enter the long-distance market nationally. The company, analysts said, could argue that if AT&T can compete in Nynex's backyard in handling calls between New Rochelle and Manhattan, then Nynex should be able to compete with AT&T between New Rochelle and New Orleans.
Separate from yesterday's agreement on Nynex's rates, the Public Service Commission is now hearing a case about establishing competition in telecommunications, cable television and information services, and experts said this accord would push the commission toward more competition in the second case.
