HEADLINE: So Much Yet to Do: Cuomo States His Case

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK 

 BODY:
By his own acknowledgment, Gov. Mario M. Cuomo has not accomplished nearly enough in his 12 years in office on a variety of issues from education to mental health. But he argues that voters should re-elect him this year because of what he has managed to achieve in the face of a divided Legislature and a feeble economy, and because his opponent would not try to complete the work he has left undone.
The Governor spent five hours and 15 minutes delivering that message -- and demonstrating his endurance -- in a marathon interview with waves of reporters at The New York Times on Monday. Even for Mr. Cuomo, who has never had trouble talking at length, it was an uncommon performance. 
 The Governor, one of the country's most prominent Democrats, who now faces a formidable challenge from George E. Pataki, a freshman State Senator, made it clear that he would neither change the direction of his administration nor propose sweeping new programs if given a fourth term.
Instead, he said he hoped that a better economy and greater pressure from him on the Legislature would bring to fruition proposals he has failed to deliver, including a state assumption of local Medicaid costs and elimination of mandatory prison sentences for second-time drug offenders, who have swelled the state's prison population.
"I'm running again because I don't think I've gotten enough good things done," Mr. Cuomo said.
Throughout the interview, he repeatedly suggested that he has yet to create a legacy that satisfies him, even after 12 years. "What do I want to be remembered for?" he said, repeating a reporter's question.
"Being a four-term governor," he said with a smile.
Mr. Cuomo proposed the unusually long session with reporters and editors to demonstrate his command of governmental issues, in part to contrast with what his aides are characterizing as the shallowness of the Pataki campaign. He said he also wanted to create a record about his record, and like a lawyer addressing a court stenographer, he repeatedly insisted on reading a litany of statistics from note cards supplied by his special assistant, Kim Gold, who sat at his left elbow, equipped with a portable computer. A transcript of the session ran to 44,000 words.
Mr. Cuomo, who was accompanied by 11 aides, delivered a masterly performance in the role of himself: playful and jousting, tentatively repentant for self-confessed venial sins, ever eager to attack the premise before addressing the question. "A paraphrase can be a dangerous instrument," he warned at one moment, quibbling with a questioner's restatement of his views on local government. After a series of deadlocked exchanges on a single point with another reporter, he demanded: "What's the matter? You got a block?"
Mr. Pataki, a Republican, is scheduled to attend a similar session at The Times, although the questioning is not scheduled to last as long.
As if he were running against his own government, Mr. Cuomo acknowledged throughout the session on Monday many of the frustrations that have contributed to his lowest approval ratings. Taxes, he said, were too high. State government was inefficient. Spending was higher than he wanted, though not high enough to begin chipping away at plagues like homelessness, drug addiction and AIDS.
And yet he argued that the record of the last 12 years should be sufficient reason to return him to Albany. Voters, he said, "should be astonished" at how he had cut personal income tax rates, spent billions on road and bridge construction and encouraged bus and subway car manufacturers to locate in New York.
"I think New Yorkers should concentrate on the agenda I completed and say, 'My God, how did you do it?' " Mr. Cuomo said. He added: "Some things take time. And if I got so much done over the last 12, why can't you assume I'm going to get a whole lot done over the next four?"
More often than not, Mr. Cuomo attributed his governmental disappointments to forces beyond his control.
Bringing equity and order to the public schools, he said, was largely the province of local school administrators and the Board of Regents. Effective pilot programs in areas like welfare and education could not be expanded because a national recession dried up the state's revenues. Relieving local governments of Medicaid costs had not been possible because the Senate Republicans refused to agree. Taxes were high because local governments had failed to control spending.
Although he is perhaps best known for his oratorical skills, Mr. Cuomo said he had not always communicated effectively as Governor. He said repeatedly, for instance, that he should have lobbied individual legislators more aggressively, rather than just dealing with the legislative leaders in each house. The only other mistakes he could cite were cutting the state's tourism budget, raising the hotel tax and not traveling enough on trade missions overseas.
Throughout his tenure in Albany, Mr. Cuomo has been criticized for not expending political capital -- or not using it wisely -- to manhandle a Legislature where the Republican Senate and the Democratic Assembly oppose each other on virtually every issue.
Yet Mr. Cuomo described the process of squeezing incremental change out of the Legislature as one that takes time, like a stream eroding a canyon wall.
"You can't surrender on their ability eventually to be rational," he said. He added, "If you're persistent and keep pushing, something happens."
And yet, on the issue of raising the welfare grant, which Mr. Cuomo has said is unreasonably low, the Governor maintained that he had not proposed an increase in recent years because he knew the Legislature would never approve one. "I thought I wouldn't get it," he said.
Mr. Cuomo said he had not lost confidence in the liberal vision of government as a force for good. But he also paid homage to a father of supply-side conservatism, Arthur B. Laffer, whose philosophy Mr. Cuomo ridiculed during the Reagan and Bush administrations. "To some extent it works," said Mr. Cuomo, who signed tax cuts in 1987 and 1994 and has said he will propose more tax cuts next year if he is re-elected.
As he has throughout his political life, Mr. Cuomo said he opposed the death penalty because it is an instruction in brutality, because innocents could be executed and because there is no proof it is a deterrent.
But he said that life without parole for murderers and repeat felons would deter. "Living the rest of your life in a 6 1/2-by-8 cell, for an hour or two a day outside, with no intercourse of any kind with human beings that's meaningful, with no role in life, with no future, with no dignity, is regarded as a fate worse than death," he said.
In discussing social policy, Mr. Cuomo pointed frequently to pilot programs that have yet to be replicated statewide. He spoke of Working Towards Independence, a workfare program that requires public sector employment of 33,500 able-bodied recipients of Home Relief, or about 2 percent of the state's total welfare population. He touted 21st Century Schools, a new initiative to relieve schools of state regulations, which may be implemented at up to 60 of the state's 5,000 schools over the next five years.
He said the state was not spending enough to solve its social problems, but was spending far more than other states. "Are you spending so much that they call you a neo-socialist state?" he said. "Yes. Are you spending more than the Republican-Conservatives want you to? Yes. Are you spending more than you will if they win? Absolutely."
Ultimately, Mr. Cuomo said, New Yorkers must make a choice between philosophies, and he sought to depict Mr. Pataki as the captive of what he called the radical right.
"What I see happening in this country, and maybe in this state if we're not careful, is that you may be led by people who are driven by political winds and who will take you wherever the winds blow," he said. "Right now they're blowing toward Pat Robertson in this state, gun people."
Mr. Cuomo added: "I'm very eager to keep the voice of this state civil, sound, balanced, intelligent, honest. That's all I'm thinking about."

GRAPHIC: Photo: "I'm running again because I don't think I've gotten enough good things done." GOVERNOR CUOMO (Ozier Muhammad/The New York Times) (pg. A1)
 Excerpts from a five-hour interview, pages B4-5.
 
Chart: "GOVERNOR'S RACE: Recent Polls"
 The latest polls on the New York State Governor's race show the contest between Gov. Mario M. Cuomo and his Republican challenger, George E. Pataki, to be a statistical dead heat.
 A poll conducted by the Quinnipiac College Polling Institute in Hamden, Conn., released yesterday, found a 4-point lead for Mr. Cuomo among "likely" voters. The poll, conducted with 501 registered voters who said they would definitely vote on Nov. 8, has a margin of sampling error of plus or minus 4 percentage points.
 The Marist Institute for Public Opinion in Poughkeepsie, found the reverse in their poll released Monday. Mr. Pataki led by 6 points in the poll of 911 registered voters. Among 560 "likely" voters, Mr. Pataki led Mr. Cuomo by 3 points. The margin of sampling error is plus or minus 3 percentage points.
 One explanation for the difference in these two polls may be found in the proportion of New York City voters in each poll. Thirty-seven percent of the likely voters in the Quinnipiac College Poll were from New York City, while New York City residents only made up 30 percent of the likely voters in the Marist College Poll.
 Graphs show the results of the Quinnipiac College Poll and the Marist College Poll. (pg. B5)

HEADLINE: Republicans Offer Voters a Deal for Takeover of House

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
With flags waving, a band playing and a bank of television cameras rolling, Republican candidates for the House of Representatives mustered on the steps of the Capitol today to sign a list of tax cuts and other measures that they promised to press in their first 100 days if they win control of the House in November. They called it their "contract with America."
In a way, it was a throwback to the Reagan era with pledges of deep reductions in taxes for individuals and companies, a stronger military and a constitutional amendment requiring a balanced Federal budget. In Reagan fashion, there was little mention of exactly where Government spending would be cut so that the lower taxes would not worsen the budget deficit. 
 But the 10-point list of promises made today differed with the Reagan policies in one important respect. The social issues like abortion, gun control, school prayer and flag-burning that dominated Republican dogma in the 1980's were ignored.
Democrats derided the Republicans as fiscally irresponsible.
"All told," said Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the House majority leader, "their contract would blow a hole in the Federal budget of roughly $1 trillion."
But Representative Newt Gingrich of Georgia, who is in line to be Republican leader in the next Congress and Speaker of the House if Republicans are in the majority, was not fazed by Democratic accusations that Republicans sought a free lunch -- lower taxes with no way to pay for them.
He quoted Franklin D. Roosevelt: "We have nothing to fear but fear itself." He quoted Ronald Reagan: "We have every right to dream heroic dreams; after all, we are Americans." And he said that while it might be a heroic dream to think the budget could be balanced, it could be accomplished through "tremendous creativity and new effort."
The rally on the steps off the West Front of the Capitol had the flavor of a political convention. A band of retired military musicians played Sousa marches and college fight songs. Hundreds of miniature American flags were passed out. Candidates wore large name tags, and some waved placards. Eventually, more than 300 Republican candidates, incumbents and challengers alike, filed past a table covered with red, white and blue bunting and signed their "contract with America."
The contract resembled a party platform: a constitutional amendment requiring a balanced budget in five years and a three-fifths vote of both houses of Congress to raise taxes; a tax credit of $500 per child, regardless of the parents' income; a tax reduction for married couples; tax-free savings accounts; lower taxes for middle-and upper-income Social Security beneficiaries; tax breaks to help Americans buy insurance to cover long-term medical care; lower capital gains tax rates; more favorable depreciation rules for businesses; term limits for members of Congress; more applications of the death penalty; cuts in spending on welfare; a stronger military, and much, much more.
Adding to the festivities was a large fund-raising event tonight, similar to those held during party conventions, where the Republican candidates hoped to pocket donations from the very lobbyists and political action committees that they criticize in the abstract.
A net of 40 seats would have to swing from Democrats to Republicans for them to win control of the House in the November election. The morning line is that Republicans may well pick up 25 to 30 seats but are not likely to reach 40.
But Republican leaders today expressed confidence that they would beat the conventional odds. Representative L. William Paxon of upstate New York, the chairman of the National Republican Congressional Committee, said he had reviewed all the opinion polls and believed that if the election were held today, Republicans would win the House.
One reason for the event today was to dispel the notion that Republicans are obstructionists who have no ideas of their own. Republican candidates for the Senate put out a similar platform last week.
"It's important that we stand for something," said Grant Lally, who is running against Representative Gary L. Ackerman in a district that includes parts of Long Island and Queens.
A matter that worries some Republicans is that they have given Democrats a target to shoot at for the rest of the campaign. Before this, when the Republican manifesto was limited to opposing President Clinton, Democratic candidates found it hard to take the offensive. Now, Democrats can tote up the Republican proposals and argue that they have promised to favor the rich at the expense of the less fortunate and would break the national economy.
Come what may on Nov. 8, today belonged to Mr. Gingrich. Standing in the sunshine, just below where Bill Clinton took the oath of office, he spoke with a flourish usually reserved for inaugurations and acceptance speeches at party conventions.
"If the American people accept this contract," he vowed, "we will have begun the journey to renew American civilization. Together we can renew America. Together we can help every American fulfill their unalienable right to pursue happiness and to seek the American dream. Together we can help every human across the planet seek freedom, prosperity, safety and the rule of law. That is what is at stake."

HEADLINE: Clinton and Yeltsin Smile and Hug and Set Bosnia Aside

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS JEHL, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
President Clinton and President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia tried today to sidestep disagreements on sticky issues between them, including what to do next in Bosnia, and put on a public display of shared affection as they opened a two-day summit.
The meeting between the Russian and American leaders is the first in the capital in more than two years, and from the moment Mr. Clinton threw a bear hug around Mr. Yeltsin at the diplomatic entrance to the White House this morning, almost everything about it seemed designed to project warmth and goodwill. 
 "The Russian-American relationship is at last, remarkably, a normal one, full of real accomplishment and genuine promise," Mr. Clinton declared in a welcoming ceremony on the South Lawn. He later showed off campaign memorabilia and other knickknacks to Mr. Yeltsin in the Oval Office, then escorted him to a garden patio from which the two leaders banished their aides and spent two hours swapping views on politics, both foreign and domestic.
Administration officials said that Mr. Clinton tried privately to persuade Mr. Yeltsin to halt weapons sales to Iran that amounted to $1 billion last year, and said tonight they believed he might have succeeded. They also expressed some discomfort about Mr. Yeltsin's recent talk of a Russian sphere of influence in the former Soviet Union, particularly his aggressive view of how to bring peace to Nagorno-Karabakh.
But now that Russia has withdrawn its last troops from the Baltics and Central Europe, there was finally no need for the American President to issue somber declarations about how important such a step would be, aides to Mr. Clinton noted with delight. And for the first time, they said, Mr. Clinton had no plans to complain about what he now regards as Russia's satisfactory progress toward building a market economy.
It was clear today, however, that Russia and the United States remain at least publicly divided on whether it is nearly time to allow weapons to flow freely to the Bosnian Muslims, a plan that Mr. Clinton and Congress have long endorsed to even the odds in the two-and-a-half-year-old war.
But as a compromise took shape today that would postpone such a step until next spring, it appeared that the two leaders might maintain -- at least for the duration of the summit meeting -- an appearance of common cause on Bosnia that aides to the two leaders say it is vital to maintain.
"The United States is a strong partner and not an easy one to deal with, just like Russia," Mr. Yeltsin said at this morning's ceremony on the South Lawn. The Russian Government maintains close bonds with the Bosnian Serbs, and Mr. Yeltsin declared during a photo session in the Oval Office later that his "response would be negative" if the United States moved on its own to arm the Muslim-led Bosnian Government. That is the step that Congress has said Mr. Clinton should take after Oct. 15 if he cannot win international approval to do so.
But Mr. Clinton quickly interjected to suggest that the disagreement might have become "largely academic" now that President Alija Izetbegovic of Bosnia, in an address at the United Nations today, said that his Government agreed to a six-month delay in lifting the embargo.
Even as Mr. Yeltsin and Mr. Clinton sat talking this morning across a glass-topped patio table on black wrought-iron patio chairs, their top advisers, including Secretary of State Warren Christopher and Foreign Minister Andrei Kozyrev, were inside the White House discussing whether such a compromise could hold. Mr. Clinton must still honor a Congressional demand that seeks United Nations approval to arm the Bosnian Muslims if the Serbs do not agree to peace terms by the middle of next month.
Tonight, after an afternoon meeting at which the two Presidents finally joined their aides, Administration officials said it was not yet clear that Russia -- or Britain or France -- was ready to support a United Nations resolution setting a date to lift the embargo, even if the effective date were put off until next spring. Mr. Christopher said weeks of delicate diplomacy lay ahead.
By essentially setting Bosnia aside for now, however, the two leaders left themselves to revel today in what one senior Administration official called "a close relationship, a very good relationship, a positive relationship." Even before today's meeting began, Mr. Clinton had already spent more time with Mr. Yeltsin than with any other foreign leader, and that closeness was reflected this morning in the courtly kiss the Russian President bestowed on Hillary Rodham Clinton's right hand, and in the left-right-left series of Russian-style smacks Mrs. Clinton proferred on Naina Yeltsin's cheeks.
"It wasn't so long ago that Russian-American summits were moments of high drama and sometimes disappointing results," Mr. Clinton said at this morning's ceremony, at which the flags of the two countries fluttered in the breeze and a fife-and-drum corps adorned in fake powdered wigs played "Yankee Doodle Dandy."
"Today, we meet not as adversaries, but as partners in the quest for a more prosperous and more peaceful planet."
This is the fifth time that the two Presidents have met since Mr. Clinton took office 20 months ago, but it is their first meeting in Washington, and the White House has pulled out all the stops to make Mr. Yeltsin feel at home. He and his wife are staying not in the Russian Embassy, the overnight spot at every previous superpower summit meeting for at least two decades, but in Blair House, the guest residence across Pennsylvania Avenue from the White House.
Tonight, the Yeltsins were guests at a state dinner that is just the second that the Clintons have given. And earlier in the day, to soothe Russian feelings bruised by exclusion from last summer's D-Day ceremonies, Mr. Clinton convened a Rose Garden gathering of retired Russian and American soldiers meant to commemorate the Soviet Union's contribution in helping to defeat Nazi Germany.
But Mr. Clinton also felt comfortable enough in the midst of the low-key summit meeting to dash away briefly this evening to a reception in honor of the Congressional Hispanic Caucus before returning to the White House -- already wearing his dinner jacket -- for the ceremonial dinner.
In a speech to the United Nations on Monday, Mr. Yeltsin left some Administration officials troubled with a declaration that Russia would bear "the burden for peacekeeping" within the former Soviet Union. The United States has already suggested that Russia should be more willing to work through international organizations in trying to control the war in Nagorno-Karabakh, where many people have died and a million people have been displaced over the last six years.
At a White House briefing this evening, Secretary Christopher declined publicly to criticize Mr. Yeltsin's speech. But Administration officials said the differences between the two countries over Russian peacekeeping operations had been a focus of this afternoon's meeting between the Presidents and their delegations.
It was unclear tonight what inducements the Administration might offer to persuade Russia to curtail its sales of weapons to Iran, which the United States has tried to establish as an international pariah, along with Iraq, Libya and North Korea. Last year alone, Russia sold submarines, tanks and air-launched missiles to the Iranian Government, but until now, Moscow has insisted that it should remain free to sell arms to third world countries, particularly since it badly needs hard currency and since the United States does the same.
While today's discussion focused on security issues, the summit meeting is expected to emphasize business deals. The United States has pledged $525 million to help finance and provide risk insurance for investments in Russia, and additional agreements to be announced this week are expected to boost the total of new American investments in Russia to $1 billion.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Economy
The Federal Reserve's rate-setting committee adjourned without announcing a change in short-term interest rates. Economists said it probably gave the chairman, Alan Greenspan, leeway to raise rates before its next meeting. [Page D1.] 
 The House approved a bill letting the I.R.S. collect up to $119 million in fees and keep the money for its own use. [D1.]
 The Justice Department and the Federal Trade Commission announced a new method to judge mergers, joint ventures and other arrangements in the health care industry. [B7.]
 
Companies
 Nynex and the New York Public Service Commission proposed a deal by which the company would cut some rates and freeze others for five years, while the state would lift almost all restrictions on how much profit the company can earn. [A1.]
 American International Group will invest up to $432 million in 20th Century Industries, a California automobile insurer. [D5.]
 Four Seasons Hotels said a Saudi Arabian prince had offered to acquire 25 percent of the company for $122.1 million. [D5.]
 Merrill Lynch has dismissed a broker, accusing her of creating phony accounts, and she has apparently vanished. [D5.]
 A survey shows consumers increasingly regard property insurers favorably, while the image of life insurers is deteriorating. [D5.]
 The S.E.C. charged PNF Industries with fraud. Several top officers and an outside auditor were also accused. [D15.]

HEADLINE: White House Regrouping For Round 2 on Health

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
With funeral rites barely over for the year's effort to overhaul health insurance, Hillary Rodham Clinton and other White House officials are talking inside and outside the Administration about why they failed and how a pared-back proposal might be sold next year.
How much it would be pared back will not be decided until November, after elections determine how many Democrats will be in Washington next year to offer support. A senior White House official who insisted on anonymity called the discussions strictly informal: Even the most basic issues, including whether the Clintons should put their names on a new plan, will not be resolved until after Congress goes home and serious work on the 1996 Federal budget begins early this winter. 
 Still, Mrs. Clinton is already meeting with the private groups that were the Administration's strongest backers in this summer's crusade for health care legislation, telling them not to despair and asking their advice for a future effort.
White House and Congressional officials said in interviews today that they were in agreement on two points: If they do not try again next year to overhaul the health insurance system, many voters who expects Mr. Clinton to make good on his campaign promise will exact revenge at the polls. And if a health care overhaul does not become law, the Federal deficits that Mr. Clinton has bragged of cutting will begin to grow again.
"Health care reform is going to remain not only a social imperative, but a fiscal imperative as well," said Gene Sperling, a senior official of the National Economic Council. "It is still the most logical, the most sound way to bring down the rising costs of Federal health care spending."
Experts say the explosive growth of the Medicare and Medicaid programs, which offer heavily subsidized health care to retirees and the poor, are most responsible for the annual deficit.
But with top officials consumed by Congress's last gasps and the midterm election campaign, neither the White House nor Democratic lawmakers have made much effort yet to prepare a health care strategy. "Presumably, this will stay high on the agenda," said a senior White House official.
He and others said no one has examined the potential scope of legislation, whether it would be written in the White House or Congress, how it would be marketed to voters and lawmakers, or even whether the Clintons would place their personal stamp on it, as they did this year.
Mrs. Clinton and Leon E. Panetta, the White House chief of staff, met last week with other high-ranking aides to discuss their failed legislative campaign and the odds facing a renewed effort next year, officials said, but no decisions were made.
And Mrs. Clinton has been making the rounds of the private groups that lobbied on behalf of the Administration's health care package this summer, offering sympathy and soliciting advice.
Dr. Irwin Redlener, president of the New York-based Children's Health Fund, said today that he told Mrs. Clinton in a meeting earlier this month that his group planned to support "Kids First" legislation next year that would extend health insurance coverage to all children.
At a meeting a year ago, Mrs. Clinton argued to him that the best way to help children was to enact universal insurance coverage, Dr. Redlener said.
He said of his recent meeting with Mrs. Clinton: "What I found was a person who was still incredibly committed to the big goals of fixing the health care system and getting to the point where she can again begin talking about universal coverage and access guaranteed to everybody. But she did say to me that the idea of getting children covered first was a very acceptable first step."
Dr. Redlener added that Mrs. Clinton "was frustrated and disappointed, certainly, but nowhere near giving up on this."
There is no shortage of advice on what to do next.
Moderate Democrats say the urgency of the deficit problem -- and the strong prospect that the next Congress will be more conservative and pinchpenny -- offers the Clintons an opportunity to cast an overhaul of the health care system as a budget issue rather than a new Government social program.
White House officials call that one of several options, and said the domestic agenda to be presented to the next Congress may well include other programs -- welfare overhaul, deficit reduction and so-called investments in the national infrastructure -- that would dovetail with an attempt to control health care costs.
But some say that tack would only put the Administration at odds with groups that supported this year's health care crusade, like the American Association of Retired Persons, and which oppose efforts to curtail Federal benefits.
Others suggest the White House should learn from this summer's effort, in which Republicans used Mr. Clinton's sagging popularity to tag the Democratic proposal as the "Clinton plan," and simply leave the drafting to Congress.
"The biggest lesson learned is that if you want Congress to pass something, it's got to be something that grows out of Congress," the Senate Democratic strategist said.
Geoffrey Garin, a Democratic pollster whose firm has lent its voice to past White House political discussions, said Mr. Clinton was best advised to treat this month's collapse of the health care crusade as a public loss, not a personal one.
So far, Mr. Clinton appears to be doing that. White House officials say the President is unlikely to make Republican opposition to the proposal a major campaign issue, in part because the White House is likely to need Republicans next year if any legislation is to pass.
Another official who spoke on the condition of anonymity suggested that if Mr. Clinton campaigned on the issue much, it would be to rail against the "special interests" that played a role in defeating the effort.

HEADLINE: No Fed Rate Rise, and No Big Market Moves

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
The Federal Reserve's top interest rate policy committee adjourned after a four-hour meeting today without announcing any change in short-term interest rates.
But the consensus of economists was that the committee probably gave Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, considerable leeway to raise rates again before the committee next meets on Nov. 15, particularly if Government economic data issued in the next six weeks show signs that inflation is rising 
 Financial markets showed little reaction to the central bank session. Stocks gained slightly, with the Dow Jones industrial average rising 13.80 points, to 3,863.04. [Page D8.] Long-term interest rates edged up in the bond market, with the yield on the 30-year Treasury bond at 7.84 percent. [Page D15.]
The Fed issued only a brief statement after today's meeting of the Federal Open Market Committee, which controls the interest rates that banks pay to borrow money overnight. "The F.O.M.C. meeting ended at 1 P.M.," the statement said. "There will be no further announcements."
A virtually identical statement followed the committee's meeting in early July. At that time the committee also chose to leave short-term interest rates unchanged, while giving Mr. Greenspan explicit permission to raise them in the ensuing weeks.
Mr. Greenspan did not do so, and the committee raised short-term interest rates by half a percentage point at its next meeting, on Aug. 16. Today's meeting was the first one since then.
The Fed's apparent decision not to raise short-term interest rates today makes it likely that the interest rates that Americans pay on their credit cards, adjustable-rate mortgages and home-equity loans will also remain unchanged for the time being.
The central bank has repeatedly raised rates because of concerns that the pace of inflation, now slightly less than 3 percent a year, may soon accelerate. Some members of the open market committee fear higher inflation may lie in the future because factories are operating closer to full capacity than they have in five years, a situation that could lead people to start bidding up the prices of scarce labor and materials.
Other members of the committee worry that the Fed may have supplied too much credit to banks last year, increasing the amount of money in circulation and allowing Americans to bid up the price of gold and commodities. Both methods of analysis have produced similar conclusions in Fed officials that interest rates should rise.
Those officials who look at whether factories are operating at full capacity -- a group that is believed to include Alan S. Blinder, the vice chairman, and possibly Mr. Greenspan -- may have looked at figures issued today by the Conference Board, a New York-based research group supported by businesses, as evidence that the economy is not going to overheat soon. The Conference Board announced that its figures showed consumer confidence fell for the third consecutive month, suggesting that consumers may not be bidding up the prices for the limited output of factories any time soon.
Until this year, the Fed never issued any announcement at the end of such policy meetings, leaving investors to guess its intentions from the Federal Reserve Bank of New York's purchases and sales of Treasury bonds every morning. While the open market committee could theoretically have decided on an interest-rate increase this morning but decided not to announce it, this seems unlikely given the strong support that many Fed officials are expressing privately in favor of announcing interest-rate increases.
The increase in interest rates on Aug. 16 was the fifth this year, following increases on Feb. 4, March 22, April 18 and May 17. Taken together, these increases have pushed up the Federal funds rate, which banks pay each other for overnight loans, to 4.75 percent from 3 percent.
The Fed has also raised the discount rate, which banks pay to borrow money overnight from the Fed, by half a percentage point on May 17 and again on Aug. 16. The two changes have increased the discount rate to 4 percent from 3 percent at the beginning of this year.
John Lipsky, the chief economist at Salomon Brothers, said that he thought Mr. Greenspan was likely to raise interest rates before the Nov. 15 meeting of the Fed's policy-making committee, particularly if the Labor Department reports strong growth in employment in September. The department's report will be released on Oct. 7.
While the White House took no position before today's meeting on whether interest rates should rise, some officials, including Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen and Laura D'Andrea Tyson, the chairwoman of the Council of Economic Advisers, have said repeatedly in recent days that they do not foresee any sharp rise in inflation in the coming months.
The Fed has come under slowly mounting pressure from businesses to slow the pace of interest rate increases or stop them entirely.
An informal survey by the National Association of Manufacturers of 81 of the 115 executives who attended a conference here on Saturday found that 40 wanted the Federal Reserve to stop raising interest rates. Another 29 executives wanted the Fed to wait until later this year or early next year before raising rates further, while only 12 executives wanted the Federal Reserve to continue raising interest rates soon.
Jerry Jasinowski, who was the Assistant Secretary of Commerce for Policy in the Carter Administration and is now the president of the Washington-based trade group, welcomed today's silence from the Fed. "I think it is fairly obvious that they just decided, wisely, to take a wait-and-see attitude because they don't have enough information about inflation or growth to make a decision at this point," he said.

HEADLINE: The Cuomo Legacy, as He Would Tell It

 BODY:
Governor Cuomo was asked what legacy he thought he had left to New York after 12 years in office, and what he hoped to accomplish in another term.
CUOMO I don't talk about legacies. This is a big argument I had with my son a long time ago and he said unless you go out there and talk about legacy and pick one thing and say it over and over they'll never remember you for anything. And that's probably the way it will come out. Except I was against the death penalty. 
 Q. What do you want to be remembered for?
A. What do I want to be remembered for? [Smiling.] Being a four-term governor. 

 	 	 		. . .	 	Q. Have you changed your mind about anything in 12 years as Governor?
A. Well, I've changed my mind about how to deal with the Legislature. The things I mentioned to you before, about traveling.
Q. What do you mean about traveling?
A. I haven't traveled enough.
Q. Around the state, you mean?
A. No, no, no, around the country. Oh, the state, that I will never fault myself for. Thank God, I mean, I have seen more of the state than anybody who ever lived, I'm sure. And I love it. I mean, that's been all the joy. So, no, traveling around the state. Traveling internationally, everybody was after me in the early years, you have to travel and I was naive about that. I liked the desk. I liked the work. I liked feeling that I'm dealing with it and I know what's happening and I didn't think it was important for me to go to Brazil. . . .
Q. To put it another way, what have you learned in 12 years?
A. Oh, God. I have to go to the diary. I can -- I learn every day. Oh, I mean -- what can I tell you. I mean, I'm not sure I can distill it down to -- I've learned a lot about communication. I've learned a lot about how difficult it is. I've learned that the only way to communicate successfully . . . is simplicity and reiteration. Constant reiteration of simple ideas. . . .
Q. To use the word you like, what is really the rationale for a fourth term?
A. I took you through two recessions. And at the same time laid a foundation for a powerful new economy. More powerful than any you've ever had in the state. In the process of doing that, we changed all sorts of things dramatically. We created the first centers of advanced technology and gave you an infrastructure you never had before. We changed your taxes to the lowest level in 30 years. We changed your roads and bridges with the biggest building program in our history. . . .
We changed with Battery Park, which I built almost all up with Stuyvesant, with structures like that, all over the state. We introduced the first environmental protection fund, the first acid rain law, the first park fund, a whole series of firsts. We gave you one of the best Court of Appeals in the country with the first woman, the first woman Chief Judge, the first two blacks, the first Latino, and that after 200 years of waiting. We gave you an ethics law that I had to fight the Legislature to get and the strongest ethics law in our history. We're giving you a whole new approach to elementary education. We gave you 500,000 new jobs and 50,000 new businesses. And we gave you a whole new high-tech economy.
[But that was in the past, the Governor was reminded. What would he tell New Yorkers about the future?]
CUOMO Well, the recession is behind us. Happy days are near again. Happy days will be here again before I'm through. The evidence is clear -- you've had 90,000 new jobs from July of '93 to July of '94. You have all those indicators I gave you before about things getting better here. You have high-tech products being invented and sold around the world. You have exports growing faster and investment growing faster than anywhere else. This will be the high-tech capital of the United States of America. This will be the higher education capital of the United States of America -- over the next four years. It's very close to that now.
This will be the health services capital of the United States of America. It's very close to that now. We will be the primary care capital of America. We're very close to being, if we're not there already, the most progressive welfare system in the United States of America. . . .
[The Governor was asked whether he thought he would win.]
CUOMO I don't think about that. I never have. I don't live that way. I really don't. I'm planning on winning. I'm filled with things that we have to do after we win. . . .
I'm very proud of what we did with energy last week. And the taxes. You'll like what we're going to say about the taxes. It will still be very reasonable, we're saying we're not, if we can't do it without hurting the city, we're not going to do it. I know I'm very eager to get at the Legislature again. I'm very eager to keep the voice of this state civil, sound, balanced, intelligent, honest. . . .
I have always been luckier than I deserve. I can't imagine anything happening to me that would make me feel sorry for myself. So I'm not troubled so much. I would be very disappointed if I didn't succeed this time. I've wanted this one more than any of the previous three. Because this is a different moment in American history. There is a harshness and a negativism and a fear out there that concerns me and that New York can help relieve because we are seen and heard everywhere. . . .
So I would be very disappointed if I don't get the chance.

GRAPHIC: Photo: In an interview, Gov. Mario M. Cuomo said that after 12 years he had yet to create a legacy that satisfied him. He took the oath of office for his second term on Jan. 1, 1987, from Chief Judge Sol Wachtler as Matilda Cuomo held a Bible. (Vic DeLucia/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Officer Shoots Boy Holding A Toy Gun

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA 

 BODY:
Playing with a toy gun in the Gowanus Houses in Brooklyn, where real guns are all too common, a 13-year-old boy was shot and wounded last night by a police officer.
Nicholas Heyward was one of about a dozen boys playing cops-and-robbers just after sunset, chasing each other in the project with toy firearms. The boys said the rules of the game, played in the grimy halls and dimly lit stairwells of the 14-floor tower at 423 Baltic Avenue, were these: Each boy got three lives, and after being "shot" three times had to change sides. 
 About 7:30 P.M., Brian George, 23, a housing police officer assigned to the project, was on his regular foot patrol through the building. "As he ascended the stairwell, he saw a youth with a gun," said Chief Joseph Leake of the housing police.
Officer George fired one shot from his .38-caliber service revolver. The bullet hit Nicholas in the stomach and passed out his back. He underwent surgery at St. Vincent's Hospital in Manhattan and was listed in serious but stable condition.
Whether the boy somehow provoked the shooting was unclear, the police said. The Brooklyn District Attorney's office was also investigating.
Katrell Fowler, 14, and Ronald Herron, 12, were with Nicholas when he was shot, but both Katrell, who was standing directly behind Nicholas, and Ronald, who was farther up the stairs, said they did not see the officer approach or fire. Both boys were questioned at length last night at the 76th Precinct station house.
Nicholas apparently saw the officer, Katrell said. "He said, 'We're playing.' Then I heard the shot."
Ronald added, "We thought it was an M-80" firecracker. But then he heard Nicholas say, "Somebody help me," he said.
Rosemarie Rivera, whose apartment is near the stairwell, said she heard the shot and went to the stairs, where she saw Officer George kneeling over Nicholas, who lay on his back, his eyes blinking. "He said: 'Shot in the stomach. Do you know his mother?' " Mrs. Rivera said. "I said yes, and he said, 'Can you please go get her?' "
In an interview at the hospital, Nicholas's mother, Angela Heyward said: "When they said it was my son, I ran up there, but he was already on his way to the hospital. This is a bad dream."
The boys in the project often play football or basketball outdoors in the afternoon and evening, residents said, but they chose an indoor game yesterday because it was raining. The cops-and-robbers game is also a common one, and several adults said they had warned the boys about the dangers of playing with toy guns.
Chief Leake displayed an 18-inch-long, brown-and-black plastic toy rifle that he said was recovered from the landing. But people who saw the boys playing earlier disagreed on the description of the toy gun Nicholas had been brandishing, some saying it was an orange-and-black water gun.
Katrell Fowler said that Nicholas's gun was like the one the police recovered, but had an orange tip that broke off when the boy dropped it as he was shot. Katrell said his own gun was orange.
Whichever toy he had, residents said it was clearly not a real gun. "I saw my son with a little plastic toy rifle," his father, Nicholas Heyward Sr., said at the hospital. "If you looked at it you could see it was a plastic toy gun."
Tina McCloud, a family friend, said, "You can't say the gun looked real."
The shooting occurred just below the roof-level landing of the building, a spot that residents said is one of the most dangerous in the building, where officers often patrol with their guns drawn because it common site for criminal activity. Nicholas fell below a window that is pocked with bullet holes. Chief Leake said he did not know whether Officer George, who has been on the force for two years, had his gun out as he climbed the stairs, but said that he would not want to patrol there with his gun holstered.
Nicholas, who attends Intermediate School 293, lives with his parents in another building in the project, at 413 Baltic Avenue. His father is unemployed and his mother, Angela Heyward, said she works for the state, but would not be more specific.
"My son is a straight-A student," Ms. Heyward said. "He comes home, he walks the dog, he does his homework."
Neighbors agreed with his parents' description of Nicholas as a boy who stayed out of trouble.
There have been a number of instances of people waving toy weapons being shot, sometimes fatally, by police officers or others who thought they were real.

GRAPHIC: Photo: A 13-year-old boy playing cops-and-robbers with a toy gun was shot and wounded last night by a housing police officer at the Gowanus Houses in Brooklyn. Officers and neighbors stood outside the apartment building at 423 Baltic Avenue where Nicholas Heyward was shot in the stomach. (Steve Berman for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Investors Lose Money In 'Safe' Fund

BYLINE: By LESLIE WAYNE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Money market funds have long been thought to be among the safest investments for the average person, because they hold safe, short-term assets that do not stand to lose value if interest rates change. But the Securities and Exchange Commission said today that for the first time in memory investors in a money market fund had lost money.
More than a dozen other money market funds have experienced portfolio losses as a result of ill-advised investments in recent months, but their managers have made up for those losses themselves so that their investors did not lose any money. In this case, however, the fund manager, Community Assets Management, based in Denver, cannot afford to pay off investors, and will liquidate the fund at a loss. 
 "The moral is that any fund can lose money," said Arthur Levitt Jr., chairman of the S.E.C. "This serves as a warning of the steps the S.E.C. needs to take to protect investors who are not aware of the risk they are undertaking."
The cause of Community Assets' losses was ill-timed investments in complex financial instruments, called derivatives, which used Government securities as the basis for making risky bets on interest rate changes. While the fund experiencing the loss was small and catered to large institutional investors, the loss broke an unspoken assurance that investors have grown to rely on: When investors put $1 into a money market fund, they will always get at least $1 back.
This belief has helped money markets grow to more than $600 billion in assets and become a vehicle used by average investors as a higher-yielding substitute for bank passbook accounts. These funds typically invest in short-term, high-quality securities and are considered among the safest of investment products offered by mutual funds and banks.
Yesterday's disclosure comes amid increasing concern and mounting losses from derivatives throughout the mutual fund industry -- not just in money market funds. BankAmerica this year put in more than $60 million to cover derivative losses in two money market funds and 13 other banks or brokers have had to do the same in recent months. The Paine Webber Group is putting up $268 million to cover customer losses from a derivative-laden mutual fund. Piper Jaffray Companies, said it would pay up to $25 million to investors in a fund that has paper losses of $700 million from derivatives.
But the decision by Community Assets Management to liquidate, rather than to cover $2 million in losses is a first. Investors in the fund will receive 94 cents for each $1 invested, and while their losses are relatively small, the action is highly symbolic.
Mutual fund industry leaders quickly characterized this action as an isolated situation. But others say it could well be a harbinger of more trouble. On Capitol Hill, where Mr. Levitt made the disclosure today, Representative Edward J. Markey, the Massachusetts Democrat who is chairman of the House committee that oversees the S.E.C., warned that this might be "the canary in the mine."
"This was inevitable," said A. Michael Lipper, chairman of Lipper Analytical Services, which rates funds' performance. "We only know how dangerous this is after the fact. I think much of this has been flushed out of lots of portfolios. But that's not to say there isn't more out there."
Steven E. Norwitz, a spokesman for T. Rowe Price, a Baltimore-based mutual fund company, said the incident was isolated, but that investors in T. Rowe Price funds had been expressing concern about the use of derivatives in funds, prompting the company to provide new assurances that it was careful.
Representatives for the Vanguard Group and Fidelity Investments, the nation's two largest direct-mail mutual fund companies, said that their funds invested only in low-risk derivatives, and sparingly. But they, too, have received investor questions.
"We hear from our telephone banks," said Jeremy Duffield, senior vice president at Vanguard. "And we've had to write new pieces to explain derivatives."
The fund facing the losses, Community Bankers Mutual Fund, has about $35.5 million of its $82.2 million in assets in derivatives, primarily secured notes that were created from a variety of United States Government agency securities. Their value floats with interest rates.
The use of these derviatives helped the fund become the best-performing of all United States Government Agency money market funds over the 12 months ended last August, with an annual return of 3.6 percent, according to Lipper Analytical. Its assets swelled from $86.1 million in September 1993 to $102.7 million at the end of August.
But, as interest rates rose this year, dealers who made markets in the underlying government securities that supported these derivatives stopped buying them. This made their value plunge and caused them to become illiquid -- or virtually impossible to sell.
In a letter to the fund's shareholders, 112 community banks, primarily in Colorado, the fund outlined a frantic attempt by its adviser to raise capital to cover these losses.
The letter said that the adviser first tried to get either debt or equity financing. Then it tried to seek loans from people who did business with it. Finally, it tried to sell the fund. It also negotiated without success with the broker-dealers who sold the derivatives to the fund, the fund's insurer and the Government agencies that issued the securities used to create the derivatives.
"The problem is that a lot of people don't understand derivatives," Mr. Levitt said in testimony before the House Subcommittee on Telecommunications and Finance.

GRAPHIC: Chart: "Pumping Money Into Their Funds" lists the advisers who have bailed out their funds in 1993 or 1994 to avoid "breaking the buck", which happens when losses push the share price below $1. (Source: Fund Action Research) (pg. D6)

HEADLINE: Blinking First on Trade;
The U.S. and Japan Duel for High Stakes As Clinton Wields the Threat of Sanctions

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Japanese and American negotiators engaged in a high-stakes economic duel today, with Japan insisting that it would make no more trade concessions to Washington and the Americans insisting that they would hit Tokyo with trade sanctions if more concessions were not forthcoming.
The Clinton Administration's confrontation with Japan, one American official remarked, is the economic equivalent of the confrontation with Haiti, with only one major difference: "This is really important." 
 As it did with Haiti, the Administration has spent more than a year trying to find a negotiated solution with Japan, and, as such a solution remained elusive, it gradually introduced the threat of force in the form of trade sanctions.
Now that the negotiations are in an endgame, the President finds himself in a position where he will either persuade the Japanese to make the concessions he is seeking in the next three days, or he will have to carry out his threat to initiate punitive tariffs on Sept. 30. If Mr. Clinton backs down, his credibility in economic warfare could be destroyed.
At this late stage, it is impossible to tell who will blink first. Both sides have enormous economic incentives to avoid a confrontation, but they also have enormous economic and political stakes in not backing down.
The betting within the Administration and among the Japanese is that some sort of compromise will be found in which the two sides will reach agreement in some areas and the United States will apply small sanctions in areas where it gets no satisfaction from Tokyo. Such an outcome could allow the Administration to reap at least some benefit from the last 14 months of negotiations, while imposing trade sanctions that are just enough to preserve its credibility but not enough to disrupt global currency markets.
Everyone involved in the talks, though, stresses that the outcome at this stage is unclear, and will not become clear until minutes before Friday's deadline.
Mr. Clinton's Administration is as divided on the question of using economic force against Japan as it was on using military force against Haiti.
On one side is the use-of-force camp -- primarily the Commerce Department and the United States trade representative's office. Their argument goes like this: If the history of American-Japanese trade negotiations teaches anything, it is that the Japanese will never make concessions except under extreme pressure. This would seem particularly so with the current weak and ideologically divided Japanese Cabinet.
The use-of-force camp insists that the importance of these talks with Japan goes well beyond the Japanese market. With the new global trade agreement, the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade, soon to take effect, the world is about to enter an era in which official, formal tariff barriers between countries will be disappearing. The GATT accord lowers or phases out most tariffs -- taxes that countries impose on imports -- over the next 10 years in 123 countries.
Because of this phase-out, it becomes all the more critical for the United States to work to lower non-tariff barriers -- regulations, monopoly agreements, sweetheart deals between companies in a country to deter foreign competition, and other techniques that nations use to keep out imports and send business to their own corporations.
No country is more adept at non-tariff barriers than Japan, which practically invented the art form. For instance, in the last few years Japan has eliminated all formal tariff barriers to American glass exporters, but through informal agreements between Japanese companies, Tokyo has managed to limit the American share of the Japanese glass market to less than 1 percent. There are hundreds of such cases.
The hard-liners argue that Washington will never really break into the Japanese market, the world's second largest, unless it takes a stand now and wins some real Japanese concessions in eliminating nontariff barriers to imports in automobiles, insurance and Government purchasing of medical and telecommunications equipment.
Moreover, they point out, China, South Korea, Singapore, Indonesia and the rest of Asia are watching. If these countries see Washington cave in, they will emulate Tokyo's restrictive trade practices more than they already do.
On the other side of this argument are the Treasury Department and some White House economic advisers. They contend that, yes, it is a new world, and it is a world in which financial markets rule.
These officials say that while Japan should lower its trade barriers, imposing sanctions if it does not would bethe economic equivalent of dropping a nuclear bomb to get the generals out of Haiti. The financial markets, they say, would take any American trade sanctions against Tokyo as a sign that the Clinton trade policy had collapsed and that the Administration's only option would be reliance on a weak dollar and a strong yen to address Japan's $60 billion trade surplus.
The dollar, already sagging at 98 yen, will drop through the floor, this view holds, and whatever millions of dollars the United States gains by trade sanctions, it will lose billions from a weaker dollar, higher import costs, inflation and interest-rate rises. This school also thinks that Japan's overregulated, overprotected economy is already suffering from inbreeding. As a result, it is falling behind on many of the technologies of the next century, from software to high-definition television. Their attitude is to let the Japanese choke on their own regulations.
President Clinton seems to be torn between the two camps. Aides say his heart is on the side of getting tough with the Japanese, but his mind is aware of the economic costs.
The Japanese are not making it easier for him to decide. Trade Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto flew to Washington today to take part in the last-minute talks. Before leaving he declared, "I have no intention of making any new proposals or changing Japan's current position."
This tough talk could be just that smokescreen for last-minute concessions. That has been the Japanese style in the past. But then again, as with Gen. Raoul Cedras in Haiti, it may take not just the threat of force but its imminent use to win concessions from Tokyo.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Carlos Lleras Restrepo, 86; Pressed Latin American Unity

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: BOGOTA, Colombia, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Carlos Lleras Restrepo, who as President of Colombia led the campaign for economic union in Latin America in the 1960's, died today. He was 86.
The cause of death was a respiratory infection, his family said. 
 Mr. Lleras Restrepo, President from 1966 to 1970, was the driving force behind the Andean Pact, a trade agreement that linked Venezuela, Colombia, Ecuador, Peru, Bolivia and Chile. Chile later pulled out.
"His death is a great loss not only for Colombia but for the hemisphere," said Belisario Betancur, another former Colombian President.
The current President, Ernesto Samper Pisano, speaking in New York before cutting short his visit to the United Nations, said Mr. Lleras Restrepo "leaves an indelible mark with his passage through national history."
Born to a wealthy, patrician Bogota family on April 12, 1908, Mr. Lleras Restrepo had a 60-year career in politics, holding an array of posts, including minister, ambassador and congressman.
He was briefly head of the Liberal Party in 1941, then returned to the post in 1948, replacing the assassinated Jorge Gaitan. The killing was the trigger for what later became known as La Violencia, the 1948-58 decade of violent confrontation between the Liberals and the opposition Conservatives. An estimated 200,000 Colombians died in the political violence.
Mr. Lleras Restrepo was a die-hard Liberal, once forbidding members of his party to say "good morning" to Conservatives. His house was burned in 1952, and he spent two years in exile in Mexico. But in 1966, after the parties made peace and formed the National Front, he was the alliance's standard-bearer, winning easily over a former military dictator, Gen. Gustavo Rojas Pinilla.
His presidency included the imposition of an unpopular austerity program and price controls, as well as the continent's most sweeping and successful land reform program.
The 1970 elections to choose his successor as President were marred by fraud, beginning a new cycle of turbulence in Colombia. General Rojas Pinilla, once again his main opponent, asserted that he had been cheated of victory. Some of General Rojas Pinilla's supporters organized a guerrilla group called the April 19 Movement, known as M-19, which carried out spectaculr attacks on the Government. M-19 disbanded in the 1980's, but other rebel groups are still fighting.
Mr. Lleras Restrepo ran for re-election in 1978, but the Liberal Party didn't support him. Though he never fully retired from politics, he devoted much of his time to journalism, writing for his magazine Nueva Frontera.
