HEADLINE: Cuomo on the Record: Excerpts From Views On Campaign Issues

 BODY:
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo met with editors and reporters of The New York Times for 5 hours 15 minutes on Monday to discuss the issues in the gubernatorial race, his record in 12 years in office, and his goals for a fourth term. A transcript of the session ran to almost 44,000 words. Here are excerpts.
 
ECONOMY
 
The Budget
 [In a wide-ranging discussion of the state economy and budget, the Governor was asked how his proposals to cut taxes, still partly unspecified, would affect both local governments and the state's own recurring gap between revenues and expenditures.] 
 Q. I just wanted to make sure that I understood your view about the projections of budget gaps in the years ahead. Are you saying you don't believe them or you're saying --
A. No, we've had them like for, every year you have them -- $2 billion, $3 billion, $1 billion -- and then you adjust. What you do is you spend less. You draw back on commitments. You had Liberty Scholarships three years ago, $70 million. I didn't fund it. Why not? Can't fund it. This is the argument I had with the Legislature all the time. I said be careful about what you're promising people. Explain to everybody that every year we must redo the budget or we'll lose credbility.
When they did the trust fund on transportation, I said don't do the lock box. You know, you say you're going to lock it up. You know we can't guarantee it's going to be locked up. What if you need the money for something else? And that's exactly what happened. We had to dip into the locked box.
 
Cutting Taxes
 Q. What you're saying, if I understand it, Governor, is the public hunger for tax cuts is so strong that you are willing to cut services that you know the public wants to give them a tax cut. Why not instead say, hey, I'd rather give you Liberty Scholarships, I think that's more important, that poor people can get college education. . . .
A. I think that those are all adjustments you have to make as Governor. And I would make you pause, though, over your first assumption . . . which is that this is what the people want. If you gave the people what they wanted, there'd be no taxes. You can't give them enough to satisfy them with tax cuts. That's not why you do it. I guarantee you no matter what tax cuts you do they will not be satisfied.
[Asked about the burden of state and local taxes in New York, the Governor said, "Local taxes are high because the local government made them high." He complained that a Times editorial had sought to blame him for passing costs along to localities, which compensated by raising their own taxes, and denied that he had done so.]
A. It doesn't make any difference, except it's The Times and now it's out there in the atmosphere for everybody to read, that the -- the state is responsible for the local taxes. What you failed to mention is that we picked up $7.3 billion [in long-term Medicaid costs] since 1984 -- we're trying to pick up more -- that our budget has gone from 58 percent for the local government to 70 percent for the local government. That our budget has gone from 26 percent for the state -- superintendent of police and this job, all the other things -- to 18 percent. I have done more -- not I, the Legislature and I have done more to lift the burden off the shoulders of local government than ever before in the modern history of the state. No one's even close, no one even tried, except Hughie [Carey]. In 1975, we took courts off your hands -- remember we had to bail out New York City. That was the last time we did anything significant.
Now, the -- this isn't being mentioned. You fail to note that we reduced our own expenditures over a billion dollars. How? I reduced my public payroll from '90 to '92 14 percent. I laid off 6,000 people, I gave no raises for two years. Until Rudy Giuliani when did that ever happen at the city level? When has it ever happened at the Federal level? When has it ever happened in Nassau or Suffolk or Westchester or Rockland, the people whose taxes you want us to cut? . . .
Q. If you have a billion and a half dollars that you could use, why not use it in some form to relieve local tax burdens?
A. A good possibility that what would develop, what you'd have to do, is you'd have to call in the locals and say: "Look, guys and gals. There are two ways we can do this. I can reduce my taxes or we can figure out a guaranteed way we use this wealth for you to reduce yours. I want to do Medicaid pickup. Medicaid pickup is not contingent upon you reducing your taxes. Let's sit down. Let's talk turkey. Let's talk topics. Come on, bring in the county executives. If we give you this many dollars, what do you do at the local level to reduce your own costs?"
I have no way of ordering that. I have no way, you know, of passing a tax bill. Politically, it would be impossible, but even legally, jurisdictionally, I couldn't pass a tax bill that said to the local governments, "You're going to get benefits from us and Medicaid pickup and you must all cut your payrolls 10 percent." I can't do that. It would have to be negotiated. . . .
The local government makes its judgments. It judges not to reduce public employees. That's fine, that's your local judgment. O.K. It's not a fault. I'm not finding fault with them. I'm saying, if I lay off 6,000 people -- as I did, approximately -- if I give no raises to -- for two years to people who are changing sheets in mental institutions and they're only making $19,000 -- if in effect I cut her salary 7, 8 percent because of the effect of inflation, and then the locals come up, as they did, and say, "You must give us more money or we will raise our local taxes," and they do none of the things that I did, then I'm in a position where I say, "Now, wait just a moment. It doesn't make sense for me to impose these burdens on my people to take the money and give it to you so that you don't have to impose the burdens on your people." And that's what I did. . . .
Q. What is the role of the Governor in trying to influence these high level taxes, high local tax burden for New Yorkers, the Medicaid problem of local governments, and to what extent should the Governor be held accountable for the failure to make changes? . . .
A. Our role is to encourage them every way we could to reduce the cost and even to do some of it for them. For example, managed care. Managed care in New York City now is being done at our prodding and our request, even at our insistence. And we have about 400,000 people in managed care, and the last time I looked that brought down costs to about 9 percent.
Now that's a -- that's a formidable way to help the local governments. In other areas, for example, school expenditures, which are so great in so many places, we have been counseling, instructing, asking for consolidation. They won't consolidate at the local level. Why not? It's politically unpalatable. On Long Island and Nassau, specifically, you have about one-half the students you had in 1972 but you have the same number of school districts. Why? Because it's inconvenient politically to change the number of school districts. As a political matter you can't change those school districts without their permission.
So our role is to point out to them how they can save money. We have been doing that over and over. There is great reluctance -- in some places they -- they respond. Mayor Giuliani has now responded very forcefully with this budget by taking down personnel expenditures or at least controlling them. . . .
Q. From the point of view of the average citizen, whose responsibility is it that some individuals' taxes are higher than they would be out of New York State?
A. The citizens as much as anybody, because if you don't demand the services, or you don't elect these people who make these judgments.
Q. You get the government you pay for?
A. See, it's not as easy . . . The simplistic thing is, low taxes are good. Are they really? What would you do about AIDS if you reduced the taxes much lower in New York City and New York State? That's a concern I have. What do you do about crack? What do you do about addicted children? What do you do about H.I.V. babies? What do you do about Stuyvesant High School, where it cost you $127 million? Was that worth 127 million bucks? I thought so. So you have all these other judgments to make.
If you didn't reduce the taxes, what do you do with those tax dollars? How do you spend them? About 28 -- all the health care -- if you took all education and health care, I'll bet it's 65, 66 percent of our budget.
 
Making Things Work
 Q. So are people just grousing and things really aren't as bad as people think they are?
A. No, they are bad. The perception all over America is -- and I said this to the Ross Perot thing last week . . . What I said was all over America, the people believe that the price of government is too high, and that they're not getting their money's worth from the government. That's true everywhere.
Q. Are they right or are they wrong?
A. I believe that government is a lot less efficient than it should be and, worse than that, could be here in New York State -- and probably everywhere else, but I know my state. This is not an efficient government. I suspected that when I started attacking this government as a lawyer a long time ago, and did that for nearly 20 years. I was absolutely convinced when I came into government in '75 that it was not efficient. And needlessly inefficient. I'm more convinced now than ever, and each year I have said so. . . .
But I think yes, the answer is it is inefficient in a lot of different ways. The legislative process is totally inefficient. There is no excuse for the budget being late every year for 10 years. They do it deliberately to leverage the Governor. . . .
[Mr. Cuomo acknowledged that the state budget process was often painfully deadlocked because of a divided Legislature, and said he had erred over the years by not seeking to co-opt individual Republican State Senators, instead of dealing exclusively with their leaders, who often opposed him. He conceded that voters were looking for a change.]
Q. What's the difference between the change that they get when elected you and the change they get by electing your opponent?
A. Well, you'll have to ask my opponent, and I don't know what change he's offering. What change could he possibly be offering? Senator Marino is going to convert? The 34 members, the Senate Republicans, are going to be different with him? What's going to change? What's he going to do differently? What's he ever done?
 
CRIME
Death Penalty
 Q. Governor, we've been at this issue for so long that it sometimes doesn't get asked, but would you briefly state for us why you're against the death penalty? Briefly.
A. I tried doing on this what I did for abortion because I couldn't answer -- I couldn't answer it briefly, so I wrote a speech for St. Rose of Lima once. And I just hand that out when people ask me.
The whole Western world has given up on the death penalty. Israel voted against it in 1954; they got rid of it except for treason. The experience everywhere has been that the death penalty does not deter. As a matter of fact, there is a considerable amount of evidence that the death penalty encourages killing by being a kind of instruction in brutality and more. . . .
You know who is the most honest, I think, one of the most honest in arguing it is Ed Koch. Ed Koch will tell you: "Forget about deterrence. All I know is it's right -- I feel good, they killed and you should kill them. Period." Call it whatever you want. He's very honest about it. So is my mother. So I don't think it deters. I think it is an instruction in brutality. I think it sends exactly the wrong message. If you make a mistake it is irretrievable, and we have made the mistake 50 times in a half century, eight times in New York. Are you absolutely positive? . . .
And -- and I tried to imagine a situation where you make the mistake and you kill somebody and it's clearly a mistake, and there's plenty of evidence of the possibility of that happening. And now his family comes in and says, "Governor, why did you approve of that execution?" "Well, I had to do it, it was the law." "But why did you have to do it, what good did you do with it?" "Well, the people are all angry, they're all upset, you know, and they felt better because ----." "Well, how do they feel now that they know that they killed an innocent person as the price of their kind of nourishing this feeling they have for vindication?" "Well, I don't know what to tell you, lady and sir, you know, we just -- we felt we had to do it."
What -- what kind of answer is that? What -- how can you explain that? . . .
Q. Now if we go back to the point that you blocked the death penalty in New York for so many years ----.
A. I don't block the death penalty.
Q. By vetoing it.
A. I do not block the death penalty. I voted -- I'm trying like heck to ----.
Q. Well, if you had not vetoed it, it would have been on the books.
A. You could get a two-thirds vote and get it on the books.
Q. O.K., but if you hadn't vetoed it, it would now be on the books. I think that's a given.
A. I blocked it, you're right.
Q. Thank you. Why then -- why then propose again that it go to a referendum and that the people decide ----.
A. I don't -- I didn't propose it go to a referendum. They proposed a constitutional amendment for the death penalty. I said, "If you're going to have a constitutional amendment, it's got to be death penalty or life in prison without parole." I do that every year and every year that ends the argument, and it ended it this year.
Q. But why would you allow it if ----.
A. Because I may die or lose an election. Because if I lose this election you have the death penalty and that is an abomination especially for this state -- the last great voice for civility. Forget about it, we're going to have a New York State electrocution. But unless people are ticked -- everybody knows it does not deter. Everybody knows it's an instruction in brutality. . . .
 
Prison and Prevention
 Q. Governor, one of the major investments, if you want to call it that, of your administration has been in prison cells. If the ----.
A. May I say I really don't think of it as an investment. When I put people -- when I build cells, knowing that these are drug addicts who are not violent, who probably are not going to get the drug treatment they need, and who are costing us -- now it's down to about $28,000 a year, it was above that a year -- in a $150,000 or a $100,000 cell, I don't see that as an investment, I see that as stupid. We shouldn't have to do that. That person should be punished -- probation, drug treatment and $15,000 -- and we should save that cell for a violent person. Just to let you know I don't think of it as an investment, because it's not making them better.
Q. Let's back up and ask the question that way: First of all, characterize for us how you feel about [having] built more cells than any governor in history.
A. I'm unhappy about it. It had to be done because those people have to go to jail, we have no alternative. We are required to put them into jail, so you need cells for them because you know you want cells for violent people. You have to build more cells. We have no choice. I'm now at 129 percent of capacity. We have people [in] double bunks, which is not good. I'm not thinking about the inmate, I'm thinking about the corrections officers. . . .
[Mr. Cuomo was asked about the level of spending on prisons compared to programs to prevent crime.]
A. We're spending much too much on prisons. That's what I tried to say before. Because we have this group of prisoners who ought not to have to go to prison. And I spend the money because I have to. I have all sorts of diversion programs that my opponent is blaming me for. We have [Drug Treatment Alternatives to Prison] programs and we have deferred sentencing programs because we don't see prison as a salvation. We do believe that violent people have to be locked up because a disproportionate number of the violent crimes are committed by a very small percentage of the violent people. You have to lock them up, just to protect the rest of the population. But we are locking up an awful lot of nonviolent people and we would like to change that, and I try every year. And it would be more intelligent to spend that money on programs of prevention. . . .
Q. What proportion of the population in New York State prisons today do you believe would be better handled elsewhere?
AIDE One-fifth?
Q. I'm sorry, you ----.
CUOMO Oh, no, it's 20,000, not one-fifth. It's closer to a third.
Q. One-third of the current population shouldn't be there?
A. Well, they're nonviolent, therefore they should be somewhere getting drug treatment. Maybe not all of them. I would leave it up to the judges, incidentally. I'm not saying that you should have a law that does this. I'm saying you should let the judges, who have this case before them, make the determination. Second-felony offenders, now they don't get a choice. . . .
 Q. How could the fear of life in prison deter someone from committing a crime that the fear of death wouldn't?
A. Because living the rest of your life in a 6 1/2-by-8[-foot] cell, for an hour or two a day outside, with no intercourse of any kind with human beings that's meaningful, with no role in life, with no future, with no dignity, is regarded as a fate worse than death.
 
EDUCATION
School Financing
 [The Governor was asked why so many New York City schools are failing and whether the city was receiving a fair share of education aid.]
CUOMO The funding is unfair and has to be corrected. We did extremely well this year, relatively. We got it up to 34 1/2 percent. We have 37 percent of the children, we should -- they call us average wealth, so we should have probably gotten about 37 percent of the money.
Q. We, New York City?
A. Yeah, New York City. The truth is New York City, you probably should have gotten more because the problems are greater than they are anywhere else because of the sociological difficulty. Whose fault is it --
Q. Is money ----.
A. No, no, money is not the solution. But it could be useful paying teachers, having more teachers, better schools, better schools, better schools, the buildings you need. . . . And we made progress, made a lot of progress, but it's not enough.
Who's responsible for that? Well, I'm running again because I don't think I've gotten enough good things done. This is one of the good things I haven't gotten done. But I think the answer on the school formula is going to be, eventually, a lawsuit which declares it unconstitutional. We tried that 11 years ago or so and were disappointed by the Court of Appeals judgment. I think it may be different this time and there are lawsuits working their way up. . . .
 
Public Universities
 Q. I wonder if you think that the public university system in this state has diminished and how you think it compares with that of other states.
A. I think it's strengthened. I think it's a great system, and compared to other schools in the country and in the region, you know, the tuition is reasonable, to put it mildly. I'll get some numbers here in a minute.
I stopped tuition increases for seven years and then stopped it again when the Legislature almost unanimously adopted a tuition increase of $45 million -- I vetoed it. And I said, you give me seven days and I'll find the $45 million in the administrative budgets of the two systems -- CUNY and SUNY. In six days we found the money and there was no tuition increase.
Q. But it has doubled in five years.
A. Still very reasonable. What you left out is for eight years you didn't have an increase. . . .
Q. Governor, is it too reasonable?
A. Look, it should be zero.
Q.Should it be zero?
A. Of course it should be zero.
Q. What proportion of the costs should be borne by students and should it be based on the ability to pay?
A. My view? The Republican-Conservative view was that you should apply a means test which would mean that the tuition could go to something like $17,000. I think that would be devastating. . . .
I believe in a society that regards education as so valuable that it is made available to all of the people at the least possible expense to those people so that you can synthesize the society. I believe that this is a community obligation that when people go to schools it's more than just a benefit for them and a privilege for them; it is good for the entire community that they should go to school. The whole community is uplifted by a high degree of expertise and knowledgeability. And therefore, the way you pay for public schools . . .
Why should a rich person be able to go to District 26 free? I'm rich. Why should my child go free? It's exactly the same logic. And if you want to do it at the college level, why not the high school level, where we never did it that way?
 
 
SOCIAL PROGRAMS
Welfare
 Q. Can you actually take people who don't have experience in work and get them off the welfare rolls?
A. . . . In my State of the State, and in my inaugural, I think, I said, "Work is better than welfare." That was one of my emphases from the very beginning. We since then have probably been the most progressive state in the United States of America in terms of making changes in the welfare system. . . .
And that is a program that starts with a requirement that you produce the father of a child and a support award, to establish his responsibility and to get his revenues, and then allows you to work, the mother usually -- theoretically it could be a father -- allows the mother to work without the disincentives of the current system. She doesn't lose her health care, she doesn't lose her money. . . .
The question really should be, does everybody have the programs we have, and the answer is no. Does everybody have the innovativeness we have? The answer is no. Does anybody have programs out there we don't have -- now, you'll have to tell me that. Because I haven't heard from you that others have programs we don't have and we ought to be looking at. . . .
Q. And why then do we have so many people on welfare?
A. Because there are no jobs. In 1987 I had the lowest level in 17 years because there were a lot of jobs. I wasn't a genius: 1987, lowest level of people unemployed on welfare in 17 years. If I had had a little more shamelessness I would have gone out and said, "See what I did for you? I put all these people to work." I did nothing. The economy took care of it. When you have jobs they wish to work. And they will work.
Now the real problem are the babies having babies. It's the children having children. And then working on to welfare before they know how to parent, before they know how to show up for work, before they've ever been given the requirement of performing a task predictably well for a long period of time, before they're educated. That is the problem.
When you find the older welfare mothers with one or two kids, the ones who have had a little work experience, they will kiss your hand for a job. America Works works because they go to those women -- they're terrific. They're all dying to work. They don't make money on welfare. You can't live well on welfare if you're limited just to the welfare money. They all want to work, and when there are real jobs, the ones who are quick to work will be the first ones off welfare. . . .
 
Limit on Benefits
 [The Governor was asked about the idea of limiting welfare benefits to two years, as President Clinton has proposed.]
A. You have to explain to me first what they do. I mean, if you are saying that the objective should be to get the welfare client into work, then of course, that's where we began. If you're saying that you should push very hard for them to take advantage of education opportunities and to train themselves for work, well, of course, we agree. That was the new social contract. If you say that after two years they can be expected to find work, I don't know what you mean by that. What if they don't, because you have a recession and there is no work. Now that is the crucial question.
If you're saying that if there is no private-sector job for them you will create a public-sector job -- well, then you'd better show me where you're going to get the money to create this whole bureaucracy. I'm not saying that's a bad thing, but if you're going to create public-sector jobs for them, how are you going to do that? And how are you going to deal with public employees who have been laid off in a state like New York. So it remains to be seen what you do with the person who does not have a job. If you're saying, "Create a public-sector job," I'm not sure that that is going to work economically. If you say, "You just drop them from the rolls and let them starve," that's not going to happen. . . .
 
Mental Health
 [The Governor was asked if the state had spent enough on housing and other services for the homeless mentally ill.]
A. Hold it, hold it. Of course not. Of course you're not spending enough, are you kidding? What are you spending enough on? Are you spending enough to solve your education problem? No. Are you spending enough to solve your primary health care problem? No. Are we spending more than anybody in the United States of America? By far. Are you spending so much that they call you a neo-socialist state? Yes.
Q. But, Governor ----.
A. Are you spending more than the Republican-Conservatives want you to? Yes. Are you spending more than you will if they win? Absolutely.
The answer is the same to all of these questions. Underspending? Of course you're under --.
We have the richest mental-health budget in the United States of America by far. It's grown at, what -- 237 percent increase.
Q. In what period of time?
A. Since I became Governor, a 237 percent increase. . . .
Q. Governor, I'm probably asking the same question but in a less technical form. Do you feel that during the 12 years of your administration the state has done what needed to be done to deal with the homeless mentally ill or the mentally ill population that turned out to be the people on the streets of the city that so frighten New Yorkers?
A. No, of course not. But we've done more than anybody in America, by far. We've been extremely generous by every measure, but it's not enough. What Andrew [Cuomo] is saying in his report is what I said about Forest Hills in 1970. What Andrew says is with the homeless people it shouldn't be a question of shelter, it should be a question of addressing their individual problems. There is one percentage of them who have no problems other than homelessness, and for them you should find housing. But there are more who are either substance abusers, mentally ill, both, alcoholic, and you have to deal with those problems.
And you have to deal with them not in a shelter context, because that does not deal with them. Or even a housing context, because that does not deal with them. They need care -- for mental illness, for substance abuse, for alcoholism. And you have to get them into that care. And you need case management, you need a lot of other things. And that's what we have begun to do, what we're working with the city to get that done.
 
GOVERNANCE
Term Limits
 [Mr. Cuomo's opponent, State Senator George E. Pataki -- whose name the Governor never mentioned in more than five hours -- has broadcast commercials accusing Mr. Cuomo of opposing term limits for public officials and a ballot initiative allowing voters to enact government proposals directly. The Governor was asked his view.]
CUOMO When my opponent says he wants initiative and referendum and he wants term limitations and the Governor does not, that's very effective on television, I know. But it is clearly a distortion. It is saying to the people, "The Governor is stopping you from having initiative and referendum and term limitations."
The Governor is not stopping. The Governor is saying over and over, "Call a constitutional convention,' which the Legislature could do. It could put it on the ballot for -- it could have put it on for this year. If they won't, vote for a constitutional convention in 1997 and then you can consider all these things -- even things I don't like, like casino gambling. I'm against it. But I am very much for your considering it. . . .
Term limitations, I have not concluded. My previous conclusion was I think it is not intellectually sound. But if you can't find another way to end this permanent government you have here, maybe you have to do term limitations. And I've said that to the legislators over and over. The alternative would be financing, campaign financing, more ballot access. I prefer that. I prefer campaign financing and more ballot access. . . . I'll take that over term limitations.

HEADLINE: From Governor Whitman, A New Quest for Tourists

BYLINE: By JOSEPH F. SULLIVAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TRENTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
A decade ago, a popular New Jersey Governor sought to bolster the state's image, along with his own, in an advertising campaign that featured the slogan, "New Jersey and You, Perfect Together." Gov. Thomas H. Kean appeared in a frequently aired television commercial, walking barefoot along the beach while extolling the state's virtues.
Gov. Christine Todd Whitman, who would also like to lure more tourists and businesses to New Jersey, said today that her administration had reinvented Mr. Kean's idea. For six months, state officials have been researching and field-testing a new slogan, and today they made public the results: "New Jersey, What a Difference a State Makes." 
 The slogan, or more correctly, the "advertising theme line," is the first attempt to promote a positive image of the state under a broad unifying banner since the Kean administration's campaign a decade ago. Mrs. Whitman will be seen in a number of ads, including one showing her on a fishing boat.
George Nagle, director of economic research for the state's Commerce and Economic Development Department, said the advertising and marketing campaign, beginning next month in newspapers and magazines and on radio and cable television, had to answer two questions: why New Jersey and why not someplace else?
The theme that the state is banking on was culled from an original list of 59, which was reduced to 12 after testing reactions inside the state and out, and then to 4 before the final selection was made.
Officials said that when New Jersey residents were shown parts of the new campaign during testing, 7 percent reported that they were more likely to vacation within the state. Of the out-of-state residents who participated in the research, 18 percent said the ads increased their interest in coming to New Jersey.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasury Issues Retreat As Rates Don't Change

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury long-term securities fell yesterday after the Federal Reserve left interest rates unchanged.
The 30-year bond fell 18/32 , to a price of 962/32 , for a yield of 7.84 percent, up from 7.79 percent on Monday and the highest since June 23, 1992. The rates on shorter-term Treasury bills fell, however, as investors sold long-term notes and bonds into less volatile three-month and six-month bills. 
 The market moved after the Fed announced in the afternoon that its Federal Open Market Committee has decided not to raise its target for short-term interest rates, which is now 4.75 percent. An increase would have been the sixth of this year as the Fed sought to fight future inflation by slowing economic growth.
Donald Fine, chief market analyst for Chase Securities, said because the Fed did not tighten yesterday doesn't mean that it will not happen. "I suspect they'll hike rates a few more times," he said. "The last time the Fed raised rates was on Aug. 16, and they said then it would suffice for a time."
"People were keyed up for the Fed to tighten at the meeting simply because capacity utilization at 84.7 percent, close to the historic inflation threshold, and thought the Fed would get nervous about that," Mr. Fine said.
"Given global competition, my instincts tell me that the inflation threshold today is higher than in the past." Equally as important, he added, is that the factory sector today is not as great a percentage of the economy as it was in the past. As a result, Mr. Fine said, "You can't look at capacity utilization by itself as a guide to inflation."
One sign of concern about inflation may have been the surge in the price of gold to over $400 an ounce just after the announcement by the Fed that it would not raise interest rates. The concern by some that the Fed was not being tough enough in its fight against inflation may have also pushed long-term yields higher yesterday, traders said. Longer maturities, which are viewed by some as a gauge of investor concerns regarding inflation, also came under pressure yesterday from the supply side, as the Treasury gets ready to sell $11 billion of five-year notes today. In addition, dealers and traders braced for more economic reports, like the August durable goods report due today, followed later this week by the second-quarter gross domestic product, new home sales, corporate profits, personal income and the Chicago purchasing management report.
Joseph V. Battipaglia, managing director of Sterling Advisors, saw the weakness in bond prices as a message to the Fed. "The prospect of waiting another four weeks for additional economic data or more intepretations of the data has thrown the market into confusion," he said. "Had the Fed come out with a defintive statement as to what they were doing and why, it would have been enough to generate a sharp bond rally," he said. "The bond market doesn't like to be left in the dark and with the end of the quarter approaching, your certainly not going to see many buyers."
In the market's wait for some indication by the Fed of its intentions on interest rates, dealers had a difficult time setting up before yesterday's auction of new two-year notes.
As it turned out, those who bought before the Fed made its announcement managed to make some money, said Matthew F. Alexy, a government trading strategist for CS First Boston. "In general terms the auction was average and probably ok given some much uncertainty in the market," he said.
At auction the high yield for a two-year issue was 6.55 percent, up from 6.27 percent last month and the highest since 6.94 percent in July 1991.
During the early part of trading, the budget director, Alice Rivlin, was quoted on the news services as saying that the Fed had not tightened too much yet, and also saying that she sees no imminent threat of inflation. She described the Fed's decision on whether or not to tighten furthers a tough call.
Yesterday the Conference Board's consumer confidence index edged down to 88.4 in September from 90.4, slightly lower than expected. The market got a brief pop on some quick short-covering before settling lower. A poll by Reuters showed economists had expected a 90.2 September reading.
The following are the results of yesterday's two-year note auction:

Noncompetitive . . . $1,660
Interest set at . . . 6.5%
 
The two-year notes mature on Sept. 30, 1996.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Saudi Prince Is Buying Big Hotel Stake

BYLINE: By EDWIN McDOWELL 

 BODY:
Four Seasons Hotels, a Canadian-owned chain as praised for its luxury and service as its accounting practices have been criticized, said yesterday that a Saudi Arabian Prince had offered to acquire 25 percent of the company for $122.1 million.
The investor, Prince Walid bin Talal, has gained increasing prominence since 1991 as he took major stakes in Citicorp, Saks Fifth Avenue and Euro Disney. 
 Yesterday, he also agreed to buy one-quarter of the personal holdings of Isadore Sharp, Four Seasons' chairman and founder, for $22 (Canadian) a share -- $16.28 (United States). Mr. Sharp controls 20 percent of the company's stock but owns 80 percent of voting stock. The company had said in April that it was seeking a buyer.
Prince Walid, a billionaire born in 1954, who lives in a 130-room palace in Riyadh, the Saudi capital, will provide $79 million (United States) to finance the company's expansion.
In becoming a major investor in North America and Europe, the Prince has bought into businesses with lackluster performance but good growth potential. His investment in Four Seasons comes as luxury hotels are leading the industry's recovery from recession.
The Prince, a nephew of King Fahd, became Citicorp's biggest shareholder in 1991, buying $590 million of the banking company's convertible preferred stock. Early last year he acquired roughly 10 percent of Saks Fifth Avenue for about $100 million. Three months ago he agreed to acquire 24 percent of the financially troubled Euro Disney theme park near Paris for about $400 million, and in July, he bought 50 percent of the Fairmont hotel chain for an undisclosed amount.
In Four Seasons, he will invest in a 44-property chain in 19 countries, including a hotel on East 57th Street in Manhattan and another in London.
Yet like many chains, Four Seasons has increasingly avoided ownership in favor of joint ventures, limited partnerships and minority investments, preferring the far less risky business of managing the properties for a fee. It said yesterday that it would also receive a contract to manage a hotel Prince Walid is developing in Riyadh.
The Four Seasons chain is known for attentive service, and in the July issue of Consumer Reports was ranked a close second in customer satisfaction to the Ritz-Carlton hotels in the luxury category.
By using Canadian accounting rules, it issued quarterly profit-and-loss statements that did not show the financial weakness of many of its holdings. It also converted large ownership positions in six hotels into small passive stakes, using an accounting loophole.
Howard M. Schilit, an accounting professor at American University, who wrote a report on the company last December, said Four Seasons was economically weak. "The financial statements are misleading," he wrote, "even if they're not in technical violation of the rules."
In April, Four Seasons hired Goldman, Sachs to help it find a buyer, Mr. Sharp said he would insist that the buyer postpone closing the deal for three to five years to allow for a smooth transition. Yesterday, he told Bloomberg Business News that the transactions "create a long-term relationship which will help insure access to significant capital and accelerate our growth plans globally."
Irene Nattel, hotel analyst for BZW Canada, a Montreal brokerage firm, said the transaction indeed fulfilled Mr. Sharp's objectives and left room for growth.
"For shareholders, the investment will facilitate Four Seasons' continuing growth as the premier manager of luxury hotels globally," she said. "As the Middle East develops, any relationship with the Prince is bound to be valuable."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Prince Walid bin Talal, a prominent investor in major Western companies, plans to acquire 25 percent of the Four Seasons hotel chain. (Reuters/Bettmann)

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Gold Tops $400 an Ounce As Inflationary Fears Rise

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Busines News 

 BODY:
Gold prices settled above $400 an ounce yesterday for the first time in more than a year as concerns over inflation heightened when the Federal Reserve left interest rates unchanged.
Traders fear consumer prices could go up, and the value of assets like stocks and bonds could erode. 
 "It's quite obvious that inflation is accelerating," Leonard Kaplan, vice president of the Imperial Bank in Los Angeles, said. "The economy is too ebullient."
Yet any decision by the Fed at its meeting on monetary policy yesterday would have been interpreted positively by gold traders, George Milling-Stanley, an analyst with Lehman Brothers in New York, said. "The fact that we were close to a big number attracted us toward it," he added.
Gold for December delivery jumped $3.60, to $401.40 an ounce, the highest the active contract has been since Aug. 2, 1993. December silver rose 8.8 cents, to $5.76 an ounce, on the Commodity Exchange in New York. October platinum rose $1.10, to $419.40 an ounce, on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
The price rises come as jewelers, who account for about 70 percent of the world's demand for gold bullion, are increasing their gold buying ahead of the holiday season. Analysts estimate that jewelers buy about half their gold for the year in the months before Christmas.
On other markets, coffee and crude oil fell, pushing the Commodity Research Bureau index down 0.59 point, to 231.65.
Traders said that a drought in Brazil, the world's largest coffee producer, that threatened the coffee crop could be easing. Light rain fell on the crop over the weekend.
December coffee fell 6.65 cents, to $2.1995 a pound, on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange in New York.
Crude oil fell for a second day, while gasoline prices fell to a six-month low, amid concern over abundant supplies of refined products.
United States' inventories of gasoline are more than 3 percent above a year earlier, while stockpiles of distillate fuels including heating oil and diesel fuel are more than 10 percent higher, according to the American Petroleum Institute.
After the close of trading, the Institute said crude oil stocks surged by 6.6 million barrels, or 2 percent, to 328 million barrels. Gasoline stocks fell by 1.5 million barrels, less than 1 percent, to 209.5 million barrels.
Crude oil for November delivery fell 12 cents, to $17.55 a barrel, on the New York Mercantile Exchange, while October gasoline slumped 0.69 cent, to 43.58 cents a gallon, the lowest price since March 28. October heating oil slipped 0.12 cent, to 47.66 cents a gallon.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar, Following Bonds, Falls on Fed Rate Inaction

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar fell against most leading currencies yesterday after a decline in American bond prices.
Bond prices fell after the Fed's policy-making committee, the Federal Open Market Committee, ended its meeting yesterday without announcing any change in short-term interest rates. Currency traders often watch the performance of the bond market to gauge foreign investors' appetite for American assets, and the dollars needed to buy them. 
 Both bonds and the dollar steadied later in the day because many investors suspected that Fed policy makers had given Alan Greenspan, the Fed chairman, authority to raise rates at his discretion before the next policy meeting on Nov. 15, said David Durst, a currency-options trader at Bear, Stearns & Company.
In late New York trading yesterday, the dollar fell to 1.5417 marks, from 1.5527 marks on Monday. The dollar also fell to 1.2771 Swiss francs, from 1.2870 francs on Monday.
The dollar fell against the yen yesterday on speculation that United States and Japanese trade negotiators were having trouble meeting a Friday deadline for progress in talks.
In New York trading yesterday, the dollar fell to 98.07 yen, from 98.75 yen on Monday.
The Clinton Administration has threatened to impose sanctions against Japanese goods if Tokyo does not agree to buy more American goods for Government use.
In addition to sanctions, many traders expect the Administration to resume calls for a strong yen, or at least let the dollar slide, if the deadline passes without an agreement. The dollar fell as much as 20 percent against the yen last year after Mr. Clinton and his aides said a strong yen could help balance trade by making Japan's exports more expensive.
In other currency trading, the dollar was quoted at 5.2625 French francs, down from 5.3060 francs on Monday and at 1,555.50 Italian lire, down from 1,563.50 lire on Monday.

HEADLINE: Breast Cancer Twice as Deadly in Blacks

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Black women are more than twice as likely as whites to die of breast cancer, in large part because their disease more often reaches an advanced stage before it is diagnosed, researchers have reported.
They said their new study also provided some evidence that cancerous tumors might be more aggressive in black women, but the researchers said the most important conclusion to be drawn was that early screening for breast cancer was essential. 
 "We're doing O.K. treating" black women, said Dr. J. William Eley of Emory University, the chief author of the study. "So we have to concentrate on access to mammography and physical breast exams and educating women to understand the risk of breast cancer."
Part of the problem, the researchers said, is that because of poverty or other factors, black women are less likely than white women to get to a doctor for early detection of cancer.
The study, being published on Wednesday in The Journal of the American Medical Association, involved more than 1,100 women who received a diagnosis of breast cancer in 1985 and 1986. Participants were roughly split between blacks and whites in Atlanta, New Orleans and Oakland, Calif.,
Researchers looked at women's social and economic backgrounds, illnesses, the stage when the cancer was discovered, treatment and other factors. They tracked deaths through 1990 and found that black women were 2.2 times as likely to die as white women.
Forty percent of the higher death rate was attributed to the cancer's being more advanced when it was detected.
"The importance of this study is to nail down that the single most important factor to be addressed is how advanced the disease is when black women are diagnosed," Dr. Eley said.
Fifteen percent of the difference in death rates was attributed to tumors' being more aggressive in black women. "That's not to say the difference is due to genetics," Dr. Eley said. He said differences in diet and environment might account for more aggressive tumors.
Many experts recommend that women do monthly breast self-examinations, get annual physical examinations and have a mammogram every year after the age of 50.
Dr. Funmi Olopade, who specializes in breast cancer treatment at the University of Chicago Medical Center, said the study should send important messages to black women and to doctors.
"Black women should see themselves as being at risk for breast cancer, and they should do all that's in their power to reduce their chances of being killed by breast cancer," Dr. Olopade said.
And when a black woman seeks treatment for an illness or emergency, she said, "that's the opportunity for a physician to see them and order that mammogram instead of checking their blood pressure and sending them on their way."

HEADLINE: A Boom for New York Restaurants? Maybe

BYLINE: By BRYAN MILLER 

 BODY:
JUST 10 days old, the refurbished Monkey Bar in the Hotel Elysee on East 54th Street in Manhattan resembles halftime at a football stadium's concession stand. On a recent evening, its cocktail lounge teemed with after-work revelers. In its stylized 1950's dining room Sylvester Stallone and his entourage held court in a corner banquette, food groupies were exchanging opinions about the penne with shredded lamb shank, and the irrepressibly hip dropped by to case the scene -- but not to eat.
Down at C. T., the acclaimed new restaurant of Claude Troisgros on East 22d Street, the dining room was packed with customers ordering the likes of $24 crispy snapper with confit of eggplant and $26 beef tenderloin in red-wine sauce with yuca biscuits. Earlier in the day at Cafe Centro, the gleaming new 230-seat brasserie in the Met Life (ne Pan Am) Building, lunch customers were 12 deep at the reception desk. And at Gramercy Tavern, reservations for a weekend night -- if you are lucky enough to get through on the telephone -- must be made three to four weeks in advance. 
 Have New York restaurants bounced back? The answer, it seems, is maybe. There are certainly signs that the city's slump is over. Openings, particularly of high-end, high-profile restaurants, are at their highest point in six or seven years, according to many owners and suppliers. What's more, restaurant revenues in New York are up 8 to 10 percent, said Fred Sampson, the president of the New York Restaurant Association. In 1993 revenues grew less than 5 percent; in 1992 they were flat.
The scenario around the country is, in general, upbeat this fall. Restaurateurs in Boston, Chicago, Seattle and Dallas report relatively strong sales. Two exceptions are Miami, which has suffered a serious drop in tourism after a series of tourist shootings, and the Los Angeles area, which has endured three urban nightmares -- earthquakes, fires and riots -- in the last few years.
The surge in New York City tourism is clearly helping. Over the summer, hotels experienced the highest occupancy rates in a decade -- 80 to 85 percent -- said Jeanine Moss, a spokeswoman for the New York Convention and Visitors Bureau. "Finding a hotel room right now is very difficult, and getting a restaurant reservation can be the same, which is good, I guess," Ms. Moss added.
But while tourism has played a big part, Mr. Sampson said, New Yorkers also appear to be dining out more frequently. Yet, beneath all this cheer runs an undercurrent of wariness among restaurateurs who in the last six years have been beaten more than souffle batter. On their menu of woes have been a feeble national and local economy, rising food and labor costs and escalating rents. On top of that, the tax deduction for business meals has dropped to 50 percent, from 80 percent.
The opening of stylish new restaurants may not always be a valid barometer of industry health. Some restaurateurs say this surge reflects prosperity on Wall Street and the abundance of investors willing to back a business they consider glamorous. "All that means is that there are more 48-year-old divorced Wall Streeters who want to meet girls," said Brian McNally, a restaurant entrepreneur in the mid-1980's who now runs 44, the dining room in the Royalton Hotel.
Indeed, while more restaurants are opening, more are closing, too. Closings are up nearly 30 percent from 1993, said Lawrence Sarf, the president of Market Services Inc. a company in Great Neck, L.I., that monitors the food industry. But this is not unusual in a fast-growing market, he said, because when a large number of inexperienced investors enter a field, some are doomed to fail.
"The odds against success are still tremendous," Mr. Sarf said. "If you are in a good neighborhood, the rent can be $40 to $60 a square foot. Ridiculous!"
Still, there are signs of improvement, particularly for some of Manhattan's older restaurants, many of which have suffered significantly in recent years.
Le Chantilly, the 16-year-old French restaurant on East 57th Street, is having one of its best years ever, and patronage is up about 30 percent since 1992, said Camille Dulac, the owner.
"Dinner is very strong," Mr. Dulac added. "Lunch is coming back but not as strong as it used to be." .
At Oceana, a three-year-old luxury seafood restaurant on East 54th Street, business is up 25 to 30 percent over 1992, said Paul McLaughlin, an owner, and revenues are about 15 percent better than at this time last year.
"Everything is more upbeat," he added.
Companies that supply restaurants are noticing the change. Christopher Pappas, the president of Dairyland USA in the Bronx, a food distributor to restaurants in the New York City area, said business was much better this year. Much of the growth, he said, is in sales to inexpensive, mostly Italian restaurants where dinner checks total $15 to $20.
The majority of the new restaurants, while sporting flashy designs, are essentially modest in size and moderately priced. Even their names are economical: L'Udo, Arte, Lora, Alva, Aja, Zut.
The owners, too, "are a little bit shrewder than we were in the 80's," said Mr. McNally, the restaurateur. "They're not looking for a four-star restaurant, but they might have plans for spinoffs, like a Morton's or a Planet Hollywood."
As for food, Italian menus or those that offer some hybrid that includes pasta or risotto are still hot, as is anything related to the Mediterranean.
Arte, the former Acrobaleno on East Ninth Street, is typical of that trend: dishes on its menu are from France, Spain and Turkey.
The most significant growth on the dining scene appears to be at dinner rather than lunch.
"Lunch is still off because of the tax situation," said Alan Stillman, whose New York Restaurant Group operates four Manhattan establishments: Smith & Wollensky, Cite, the Manhattan Ocean Club and Park Avenue Cafe.
But Alex von Bidder, a partner in the Four Seasons, said he was "guardedly optimistic" about the return of business lunchers.
"There seems to be a lot of pent-up energy and a desire to get back" to the business lunch, he said.
What's hard to gauge is the meaning of the interest in the new restaurants, a boom that has not been seen since before the stock-market collapse in 1987.
"Over all, there is a keener interest in going out, but a lot of that could be voyeurism by people who want to try all the new places that are opening," said Drew Nieporent, whose stylish new Japanese restaurant, Nobu, in TriBeCa is turning away more than 100 people a day.
Perhaps the prevailing wariness of most restaurateurs was best expressed by Mr. Sampson of the restaurant association when he said, "Call me on the 15th of February."

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Washington Legislators Ready to Take the Field Again

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
The nation's capital, which hasn't had a baseball team since 1971, has become the only place for baseball in the absence of games and labor negotiations.
For the second successive Thursday, a Congressional subcommittee will hold a hearing aimed at ending the season-shattering strike and insuring a whole 1995 season. Tomorrow's session deals with a House of Representatives bill that would impose binding arbitration on the dispute between the owners and the players if they have no agreement by Feb. 1. 
 Pat Williams, Democrat of Montana, introduced the bill in the House last Friday. He will hold tomorrow's hearing as chairman of the labor-management relations subcommittee of the House Committee on Education and Labor.
Among those scheduled to appear are Richard Ravitch, the owners' chief labor executive; Charles O'Connor, general counsel of the clubs' labor relations committee; Gene Orza, the union's associate general counsel; Bernie Williams of the Yankees, and Orel Hershiser of the Los Angeles Dodgers.
The bill would create a three-member arbitration panel that would conduct hearings into the reasons for the dispute and baseball's economics. The panel would have the power to subpoena books and records from the clubs and witnesses to testify. It would be made up of one person designated by each side and a third selected by both sides. The bill would require the panel to render a decision by March 15 to insure that next season would start on time.
Because Congress is scheduled to adjourn no later than Oct. 15 and probably by Oct. 7, the bill is not very likely to go anywhere this session. But if the players remain on strike when Congress returns in January and Representative Williams has developed any support for the idea, it's not inconceivable that the bill could move forward.
It would probably have a better chance than the House bill that would strip baseball of its antitrust exemption if the clubs impose their salary cap. That bill was the subject of a hearing last Thursday.
Bud Selig yesterday reiterated the clubs' opposition to binding arbitration.
"I can't see where the arbitration process would play a role," Selig, the acting commissioner, said by telephone from Milwaukee. "We'll be there and express that opinion. In the end, to strike any reasonable deal, it has to be done at the table. Collective bargaining is the only way you can do that."
Asked if binding arbitration might be the only honorable way out for two sides that have been unable or unwilling to compromise, Selig said: "I don't now. I still can't conceive of any long-term peace that is meaningful and at least gives both parties a chance to have participated in the process other than through the collective bargaining process. You could have something imposed that both parties are terribly unhappy about. That doesn't produce a result that's any good."
The players association has not endorsed the idea of binding arbitration. Donald Fehr, the union's executive director, was in Los Angeles for a meeting with players and was not available for comment on Williams's bill.
The two sides have not met at the bargaining table for nearly three weeks. Fehr and Ravitch met last Friday in a one-on-one session, but nothing emerged from it. No negotiating sessions have been set.
Fehr has been conducting a series of seven regional meetings with players. He has two left, in Phoenix today and in Dallas tomorrow. Selig had said earlier that he was considering having an owners' meeting in the first or second week of October, but yesterday he said that if there is a meeting, it won't be held before mid-October.
"There's been no great clamor for one," Selig said.

HEADLINE: Price of Higher Education Becomes Even Dearer

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: BOSTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
The average tuition at the United States' four-year colleges rose 6 percent this year -- the smallest increase since 1989, but still twice the rate of inflation, according to an annual survey by the College Board, an association of 2,800 institutions of higher education.
Separate Government statistics also show that the proportion of money used by colleges for instruction, libraries and maintenance is shrinking, and that the amount spent on public relations, marketing and fund-raising is increasing.
Average tuition is $11,709 at four-year private colleges and $2,686 at four-year public colleges and universities, both 6 percent increases over last year. The cost of two-year private institutions rose 5 percent, to $6,511, and two-year public colleges, 4 percent, to $1,298. 
 When room, board, books, supplies and transportation are added in, the average total cost of a college education comes to $18,784 for resident students at four-year private institutions and $8,990 at public ones.
"We're paying more and getting less than we got 10 years ago," said Stephanie Arelonio, president of the United States Student Association.
Much of the tuition increase was caused by health-care benefits for faculty members and staff, said David Warren, president of the National Association of Independent Colleges and Universities.

(Source: The College Board)

HEADLINE: The G.O.P's Deceptive Contract

 BODY:
Over 300 Republican candidates for the House of Representatives promised yesterday that, if elected, they will provide a positive new vision for their party and for the country. The body and soul of this vision is to be found in 10 bills they plan to offer in the next session of Congress.
It is a vision that looks backward, Reaganism in a rear-view mirror. What this self-styled "Contract With America" says to voters is that these Republicans do not speak candidly. 
 Tote up the promises. Led by the minority whip, Newt Gingrich, this group of Republicans would balance the budget while adding to defense spending and cutting taxes on capital gains and Social Security benefits for wealthier recipients. They would also ease the tax burden on non-poor families with children, corporate investment, retirement savings and the estates of wealthy families.
The Republicans, historically a deficit-conscious bunch until Ronald Reagan came along, estimate that their largesse would cost the Treasury about $150 billion over five years. The White House estimates the cost to be about $800 billion. Even the White House estimate may be low.
The Congressional Budget Office says that it would take more than $700 billion in budget cuts over five years to balance the existing budget -- before the Republicans start hiking defense spending and cutting taxes. The Republicans would keep costs in line by finding fat in Federal programs, though the contract does not say where. In the past John Kasich of Ohio has drawn up bold proposals, but even if Congress adopted every one -- a politically preposterous prospect -- the Republicans would be hundreds of billions shy of their promises.
The contract is not only reckless but deceptive. The tax cuts on capital gains income, corporate investment and retirement savings are structured to cost little money during the five-year period for which the Republicans do their accounting. But the proposals are certain to pry open gigantic holes in the budget beyond five years.
Nor does the contract reflect a sense of fair play. The cut in the capital gains tax would largely benefit high-income families and, because it would also apply to profits on old investments, is not targeted to stimulate new investment. The tax credit for children would yield nothing to families too poor to owe taxes. The Social Security tax cuts apply only to high-income families. Tax breaks for investment and savings, even if they favor the rich, can be defended. But they would have to be carefully designed to boost productivity and be part of an overall package that spreads tax burdens fairly. By this standard, Mr. Gingrich's compact flunks.
The other bills that make up this vision deal with issues ranging from welfare to crime to long-term health care. Some are good, some bad, but most are presented honestly. That is not true of the economic nostrums. The Republicans ought to admit that their programs would require cutting hundreds of billions out of about $1.5 trillion in Federal spending -- thus slicing 20 percent or more out of every program from Social Security to the F.B.I. Mr. Gingrich promised a positive vision. What voters got instead was duplicitous propaganda.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Pataki, a la Whitman, Proposes Broad Cuts in State Income Tax

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO 

 BODY:
Following the lead of Gov. Christine Todd Whitman in New Jersey, State Senator George E. Pataki, the Republican candidate for governor, yesterday proposed deep and broad-based cuts in personal income taxes that would reduce the tax bill of virtually every New Yorker.
Coming a week after he was bitingly criticized by other Republicans for not detailing an economic plan, Mr. Pataki said he would cut the top income tax rate by 25 percent over four years and reduce the number of people paying that rate. By 1998, the plan would reduce state revenues by $5.6 billion, making it one of the largest tax cuts in state history. 
 "Taken together, these measures will go a long way toward making New York once again the place the most enterprising people flock to, not the one the flee from," Mr. Pataki said in a speech in Manhattan to trustees of the Citizens Budget Commission, a fiscal watchdog group financed by businesses.
Mr. Pataki offered only a general outline of how he would pay for the tax cuts. He said he believed the state work force could be cut by one-third and state agencies could be consolidated without reducing services. But that would raise only $300 million of the $5.6 billion. He also included one-time revenue sources like selling the governor's 28-year-old airplane and two helicopters.
The bulk of the tax cut would be paid for by holding the growth of state spending, most of which provides aid to local governments and schools, to the rate of inflation, 3 percent this year. At the same time, Mr. Pataki is counting on a vibrantly growing economy to increase state revenues by 6 percent a year, even with the tax cuts.
Economists said that even a slight economic downturn could undermine the plan, driving down tax revenues and forcing the state to cut services, which could in turn pressure local governments to raise local property taxes.
"If everything was hunky-dory now, there is a chance it would work," said Frank J. Mauro, director of the Fiscal Policy Institute, a nonprofit group financed by labor unions and foundations that frequently opposes tax cuts. "But we're not at a stable position now."
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo, a three-term Democrat, called the proposal a "magic act" that would require "holy water" to get through the State Legislature. And he called himself the only real tax cutter in the race, having signed income tax cuts in 1985 and 1987, though the final phase of the 1987 bill has been postponed since 1990 because of the recession.
"The other candidate does not have the same credibility," Mr. Cuomo said of Mr. Pataki after he announced a child-health program in Harlem yesterday. [Page B9.]
As much as it was an economic plan, Mr. Pataki's proposal also seemed a political position paper. It placed him on the offensive in a tax-cut duel with Mr. Cuomo, who last week hazily outlined $1.5 billion in income and business tax cuts. It gave Mr. Pataki a platform plank that would appeal to almost every taxpaying New Yorker. And it sought to answer, at least in part, critics who have said his ideas are shallow and unformed.
Mr. Pataki cited the example set by Mrs. Whitman, who has promised to cut state income taxes by 30 percent over three years, as his model, saying she has shown that reduced state spending can pay for deep tax cuts. One of the architects of her plan, Malcolm S. Forbes Jr., the publisher of Forbes magazine, was also an economic adviser to him. And like her, the candidate released the plan with six weeks left in the campaign.
Income taxes are New York State's single largest source of revenue, accounting for an estimated $17.6 billion this year, 54 percent of the $32.9 billion the state expects to raise through taxes and fees.
Mr. Pataki would cut the tax in several ways. He would reduce the top rate from the current 7.875 to 5.9 by 1998, a 25 percent decrease. He would raise the annual income for the top tax bracket from the current $26,000 for a married couple to $50,000, removing "several hundred thousand" middle-income families from that top level, he said.
He would also keep the bottom tax rate at 4 percent, which he said would help low-income New Yorkers. That rate is to rise to 5.5 percent under the 1987 tax cut law.
Other provisions in the plan include replacing the $1,000 exemption for dependents with a tax credit of $200 per dependent, a change that Mr. Pataki said would be most beneficial to less wealthy taxpayers, and increasing the standard deduction to $13,000, from $9,500, for married couples. Mr. Pataki said he would also phase in an existing earned income tax credit for low-income families in one year instead of three.
For a family of four with an adjusted annual gross income of $45,000, Mr. Pataki's plan would reduce their state taxes to $985, from $1,920, a 49 percent decrease, according to figures provided by the Pataki campaign.
There is a risk for Mr. Pataki in modeling his plan after Mrs. Whitman's and praising her ability to balance her budget despite $122 million in tax cuts this year. The balancing act involved laying off state employees, freezing state aid to most municipalities and school districts and resorting to about $1.8 billion in "one-shot" savings.
In the past week, Mr. Pataki has been sharply criticized by one of his own running mates, Herbert London, the candidate for State Comptroller, for not offering a detailed plan to stimulate the economy. Yesterday, Mr. London's campaign said they would not comment on Mr. Pataki's new plan until after they had met with him.
Another Republican critic of Mr. Pataki, New York City Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, also expressed concerns that the tax-cut plan would lead to reductions in state aid to the city. "It depends very much on how you do it," said Mr. Giuliani.
Mr. Pataki has been pursuing Mr. Giuliani's endorsement, and in his speech to the budget commission he spoke glowingly of Mr. Giuliani's efforts to cut city spending.
Mr. Pataki also used the 35-minute speech to underscore his differences with Mr. Cuomo. "The Cuomo philosophy, basically, is that whatever sounds virtuous is, ipso facto, the business of government," Mr. Pataki said. "That's wrong."
Mr. Pataki's aides said they expected to release this week television commercials about the tax cuts. One spot is intended to spoof Mr. Cuomo's claim to be a tax cutter by suggesting that the appearance of Elvis Presley on Long Island is more likely than a tax cut under Mr. Cuomo.

HEADLINE: Disney Drops Plan for History Theme Park in Virginia

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA 

 BODY:
The Walt Disney Company announced last night that it had abandoned its chosen site in Northern Virginia for a sprawling American history theme park, a project that was reviled by historians and environmentalists and hotly debated at local planning boards as well as the United States Senate. The company said it would seek another site for the development, possibly in Virginia.
Peter S. Rummell, president of the Disney Design and Development Company, made it clear in a statement that the company had bowed to a torrent of criticism, in part out of a fear that opposition could delay the project. "Despite our confidence that we would eventually win the necessary approvals, it has become clear that we could not say when the park would be able to open, or even when we could break ground," he said. 
 The company planned to build the park, Disney's America, 35 miles southwest of Washington, near the site of the First Battle of Bull Run, the first big conflict of the Civil War, and in a region that contains many battle sites from that war. The proposed park, which was to be the centerpiece of a 3,000-acre, $650 million development, inspired fervent opposition from some historians who said it could blot out important Civil War sites and from environmentalists who said that it would bring crowding, road congestion and smog to an area where all these problems have mushroomed in recent years.
In a meeting late yesterday afternoon, Disney officials told Gov. George F. Allen and other state officials who had backed the project that they had decided against the rural site at Haymarket, in Prince William County. The Governor had said the project would provide 19,000 jobs and $47 million in annual revenue for the state. He was the driving force behind a $163 million bond issue that would have paid for new roads to serve the area surrounding the park.
"I would characterize our reaction as absolute shock," Robert T. Skunda, the state Secretary of Commerce and Trade, said last night. He noted that just in the last week, the project had cleared two important hurdles, winning the approval of the Prince William County Planning Commission and the regional Transportation Planning Board.
Mr. Skunda said Disney officials still hoped to find another site in Northern Virginia, within reach of the millions of tourists who visit the nation's capital each year. But the process of finding another location and once again negotiating the thickets of public opinion and governmental approval could delay the park, which was to open in 1998, by years.
In his statement, Mr. Rummell said, "In our mind, Virginia would still be an ideal place for this park."
James M. McPherson, a Princeton University historian who was a leader of the efforts to block the project, said Disney began to reconsider its choice of location about a week ago but held off making an announcement.
"We do feel good about it," said Mr. McPherson, president of Protect Historic America, a group of historians, scholars and authors opposed to the development. "Disney recognized what it was costing them in terms of image, public relations and the potential for a long, drawn-out controversy and lawsuits from environmental groups. They decided to respond to all the criticism and what we have emphasized all along, location."
Opponents said it was not the project itself they opposed, but the location. "The proposed site would have worsen traffic and air quality," said Fred Krupp, the executive director of the National Environmental Defense Fund, which led a coalition of 30 groups that opposed the project. "In a different location closer to public transportation, this development can strengthen the economy and improve air quality."
Despite polls showing that the proposed park had the support of a majority of Virginians, it attracted a wide range of opponents. In June, 16 representatives introduced a resolution in the House opposing the project, and the Senate's public lands subcommittee held hearings on the subject.
Opposition also came from the Interior Department, where some officials objected to the consequences such an ambitious project could have on nearby national park lands.
Last week, Sierra Club officials said they might go to court to stop, or at least delay, the project.
Some historians and writers questioned Disney's ability to address the darker issues of American history in a realistic or sensitive fashion. "I have doubts whether the technical wizardry that so entrances children and grown-ups at other Disney parks can do anything but mock a theme as momentous as slavery," the novelist William Styron wrote in an Op Ed page article last month in The New York Times. "To present even the most squalid sights would be to cheaply romanticize suffering."
Disney mounted a huge public relations campaign to try to overcome the opposition, but to no avail.
The outcome had a bitter taste for supporters of the project. "It's been a campaign of misinformation by well-financed out-of-state interests, and it worked," Mr. Skunda said. "No company wants to have its reputation dragged through the mud, and that's what happened here."
The park was to include a ride through a blast furnace; virtual reality Revolutionary War battles, in which visitors would have had a chance to fire muskets, and nightly recreations of the Civil War battle between the Monitor and the Merrimack, the first duel between ironclad ships.
In addition to the American heritage theme park, Disney had requested approval to build as many as 2,281 homes, 1,340 hotel rooms and 1.96 million square feet of retail and commercial space.
