HEADLINE: Banker Hired For a Top Job With Schools

BYLINE: By SAM DILLON 

 BODY:
The New York City Schools Chancellor, Ramon C. Cortines, who has been criticized for trying to run the schools by himself, yesterday filled a newly created post to oversee the day-to-day management of the school system. But he drew immediate criticism from Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani for giving the job to a former banker who served as a deputy mayor to David N. Dinkins.
The new post of chief operations officer for the system of 1,100 schools will be filled by Barry F. Sullivan, who served for the final year and a half of the Dinkins administration as Deputy Mayor for finance and economic development. Since then he has led the New York City Partnership, whose members include 150 leaders of the city's biggest corporations, and the city's Chamber of Commerce. He has close ties to the city's business elite, like David Rockefeller, the former chairman of Chase Manhattan Bank. 
 Mr. Cortines said that Mr. Sullivan's fiscal experience and his ties to business would be valuable to the schools. "I know he will bring the benefit of those executive experiences and practices to the school system and help us improve," he said.
But in naming him, Mr. Cortines brushed aside entreaties from City Hall. He said last night that Deputy Mayor Ninfa Segarra had urged him in a meeting Tuesday evening not to appoint Mr. Sullivan to the powerful post, with oversight over the board's $8.5 billion budget and its 135,000 employees. And Deputy Mayor Peter J. Powers intervened directly to ask Mr. Sullivan not to take the job, said the Mayor's press secretary, Cristyne F. Lategano.
After Mr. Cortines announced his decision yesterday, Mr. Giuliani called the selection "a mistake."
"I don't think Barry Sullivan is the right choice for that position," Mayor Giuliani said. "I think you would have been far better to have brought in someone that first of all is independent of the prior administration and comes to it with a new spirit, with an independent spirit."
Mr. Sullivan, a Republican, led First Chicago Bank until December 1991. In an interview yesterday, Mr. Cortines said that given his eight-month feud with Mr. Giuliani over the school budget, he had tried to select an administrator who would be able to both work with City Hall and build new partnerships with the city's business community.
He suggested that he had been taken aback by Mr. Giuliani's objections to Mr. Sullivan, and he said he had considered them carefully before finally putting them aside out of the belief that Mr. Sullivan was the right man.
"I was looking for someone who could work with City Hall, who understood the importance of working collaboratively with the city, even though we are an independent agency," Mr. Cortines said.
Even Herman Badillo, the Mayor's special deputy commissioner for school finances, had kind words for Mr. Sullivan. At City Hall yesterday he said he thought it would be useful for Mr. Cortines to turn outside the central bureaucracy in filling one of the top two management positions.
Former Chancellor Joseph A. Fernandez had two deputy chancellors: Argie Johnson, for instruction, who handled the board divisions dealing with curriculum and other classroom issues, and Stanley S. Litow, for operations, who oversaw the board's business and operational side. Both Ms. Johnson and Mr. Litow left the board on Mr. Fernandez's departure in June 1993. Mr. Cortines appointed Beverly Hall earlier this year to replace Ms. Johnson.
He changed the name of the deputy chancellorship for operations to chief operations officer, but the post remained vacant for 15 months.
Mr. Sullivan's appointment comes at a time when many business leaders have criticized the school system for failing to provide the well-educated labor force the city needs.
In the new post, which he is scheduled to assume later this fall, Mr. Sullivan will oversee the board offices that administer the budget, personnel, labor relations, payroll, computer services, school lunches and busing. He will share oversight over the division that manages custodial services and maintenance in the city's 1,070 school buildings.
Mr. Sullivan was vacationing in Europe, but he issued a statement through the Chancellor's office.
"This is the right job at the right time with the right Chancellor," Mr. Sullivan said. "Nothing is more important to the future of this city than our schools, and I am glad to be part of an effort to improve them."
Mr. Cortines would not discuss details of his meeting Tuesday night with Ms. Segarra. "It was a professional conversation I had last evening with the Deputy Mayor, and she shared their professional concerns," Mr. Cortines said. An official who insisted on anonymity said that City Hall officials followed up on Ms. Segarra's meeting yesterday morning in a phone call to Mr. Cortines, again urging him not to appoint Mr. Sullivan.
"I considered the information that City Hall shared with me, because there's an improved relationship," Mr. Cortines said. "But I had to consider the good of the system."
John Fager, co-chairman of the Parents Coalition for Education in New York City, said Mr. Sullivan, as chairman of First Chicago, had helped push an act through the Illinois Legislature in 1988 that led to Chicago's radical decentralization of the school system, in which committees of parents, teachers and administrators manage schools.
"If he's going to bring the kinds of changes to New York that he helped bring to Chicago, then it's an exciting appointment," Mr. Fager said. "If he's simply being brought in to manage the status quo, then it's disappointing."
Mr. Sullivan is a Jesuit-educated native of the Bronx -- he graduated from the prestigious Regis High School in Manhattan -- who turned down a chance to play basketball with the New York Knicks before beginning a career in banking.

HEADLINE: U.S. Planning to Hold a Lottery To Pick Some Cuban Immigrants

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
Having promised President Fidel Castro that it would grant entry rights to at least 20,000 Cubans a year, the Clinton Administration has decided to select about one-quarter of that total by a lottery of Cuban applicants.
Administration officials who are putting the finishing touches on the immigration plan said this lottery is intended to create opportunities for Cubans who have no hope of being admitted under American immigration law because they neither have close relatives in the United States nor qualify for refugee status. The other 15,000 would be accepted largely by broadening the definition of refugees and close relatives. 
 One official said that so many Cubans -- perhaps more than 100,000 -- might apply for entry through the lottery that it could become an embarrassment for Mr. Castro and increase pressures on him to liberalize his economy.
"One advantage of the lottery over accepting people on a first-come- first-served basis is you won't have people standing in long lines outside the American Interest Section in Havana," said an Administration official.
Another advantage of the lottery, Administration officials say, is that it might encourage some of the 30,000 Cuban refugees being held at Guantanamo Bay Naval Station to return voluntarily to Cuba -- something the Administration wants them to do -- to try their luck in the lottery. If Cubans were granted entry on a first-come-first-served basis, the Guantanamo refugees would have little chance to qualify and thus little incentive to go back to Cuba.
State Department officials repeated this week that the refugees at Guantanamo Bay have no chance to go directly to the United States. They noted, however, that Venezuela, Mexico and other Latin American countries have agreed to resettle several hundred of the Cubans.
The Administration agreed on Sept. 9 to admit more than 20,000 Cubans a year as part of a deal in which it won Mr. Castro's promise to stop the exodus.
Last week, Ricardo Alarcon, Cuba's chief negotiator in the immigration talks with Washington, complained that the Administration was dragging its feet in announcing exactly how it would grant entry to the 20,000 Cubans a year. At a news conference on Sept. 9, Attorney General Janet Reno indicated that the details would be released late the following week.
In explaining the delay, one Justice Department official said, "It's more important to do it right than to do it fast."
State Department officials said they were pleased by two steps the Castro Government took this month: it met in Madrid with three prominent exiled dissidents and it permitted farmers to sell their produce.
But Administration officials said these steps did not go far enough to warrant the "carefully calibrated" response that the United States promised if Havana took major steps toward democracy.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Croat Presses U.N. to Act Against Serbs

BYLINE: By RICHARD D. LYONS, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, Sept. 28

 BODY:
After hearing pleas this week for the protection of Muslims in Bosnia, the President of Croatia told the United Nations today that Belgrade must also be forced to stop supporting Serbian forces occupying large areas of Croatia.
The Security Council voted last week to ease economic sanctions on Serbia in return for its help in controlling the Bosnian Serbs, but Croatia's problems were not dealt with. 
 President Franjo Tudjman told the General Assembly today that the time had come for tougher action by the United Nations to end the fighting in the former Yugoslavia that has gone on for almost three years.
During meetings with President Clinton, Secretary General Boutros Boutros-Ghali and members of the Security Council, Mr. Tudjman said he emphasized "that Croatia's patience with respect to its occupied territories may be wearing thin." He also demanded "stronger and urgent commitment."
"Croatia welcomes Belgrade's announced readiness to isolate the Bosnian Serbs with their suicidal war option, but expects Belgrade to do the same with regard to the insurgent Serbs in Croatia," he added.
The Security Council last week also adopted two resolutions aimed at punishing the Bosnian Serbs for continuing to wage a "persistent and systematic campaign of terror." But again, no mention was made of the problems within Croatia.
After speaking to the General Assembly today, Britain's Foreign Secretary, Douglas Hurd, defended his Government's opposition to the lifting of an arms embargo on the former Yugoslavia. The Muslims were put at a disadvantage because the Serbian-controlled Yugoslav Army stocked the arsenals of the Bosnian Serbs.
British and French opposition to the Security Council's action to ease sanctions in Serbia played a large role in changing the position of the Bosnian Government. President Alija Izetbegovic told the assembly on Tuesday that he would agree to a six-month delay in pressing for the lifting of the embargo.
Mr. Hurd said, "I welcome the decision of the Bosnian Government to accept that it is not the moment to lift the arms embargo." But he left open the possibility of changing this position, bearing in mind that British and French United Nations troops are at the greatest risk of casualties should the war intensify.
"We have said before that if the Bosnian Serbs do not join the peace process and agree to the map presented by the contact group," he said, "then our present approach may be exhausted, lifting the arms embargo may become unavoidable" and United Nations forces would have to be withdrawn. The United States, Britain, France, Germany and Russia make up the negotiating team known as the contact group.

HEADLINE: U.S. and Russia to Speed Disarmament

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr., Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
In a cordial, almost jocular atmosphere, President Clinton and President Boris N. Yeltsin agreed today to speed destruction of nuclear weapons, but they made only limited progress on several other issues.
"Relations between our nations are moving forward at full speed," Mr. Clinton said at a joint news conference in the East Room of the White House after the signing of documents pledging cooperation on economic and security matters. 
 On most things, Mr. Yeltsin said, "for peace, for humanity, for mankind, for our whole planet, we have agreed."
Two things made this, the fifth summit meeting between the two leaders, different from the others.
First, the contentious questions today are small potatoes compared with those of the recent past, like arms control and the very survival of the Russian reformers. Second, Mr. Yeltsin appears to have regained his stride after having seemed off-balance for months following the attempt to oust him a year ago.
The two leaders, burly extroverts who obviously enjoy each other, agreed to speed implementation of the Start II agreement, reached in 1993, which calls for the reduction of each nation's arsenal to between 3,000 and 3,500 long-range nuclear warheads by 2003. "Instead of taking the nine years allowed," Mr. Clinton said, dismantling will begin as soon as the accord is ratified.
But Start II cannot be ratified until an earlier agreement, Start I, comes into effect. The Administration hopes that will happen by the end of the year and that ratification can be completed -- and dismantling can therefore get under way -- by mid-1995. Congressional approval seems sure; a struggle is expected in the Russian Parliament, but Mr. Yeltsin will probably prevail.
Obviously delighted with what he had accomplished during two days in Washington, the Russian leader hammed it up with reporters, taunting them for falling behind. "You can't think fast enough?" he asked, stretching out his words to comic extremes. Mr. Clinton did not quite manage to keep a straight face.
Earlier, at the Library of Congress, Mr. Yeltsin robustly rebutted those who have suggested recently that the United States and Russia will eventually come into conflict.
"I would like to tell you," he said in his booming baritone, "that we never fought the United States, and I believe I can say as President of Russia that we will never fight the United States in the future. Instead, we will focus on building a world of decency and welfare."
But on Bosnia, cooperation between the two nations is still imperfect, and only limited progress was made. A close adviser to Mr. Yeltsin said, "If it were easy, the thing would have been settled long ago."
Russia, in part because of its cultural links to the Serbs, has long opposed the lifting of the international arms embargo against the former Yugoslavia, which would benefit the Muslim-led Government in Bosnia. The British and the French, who have peacekeepers in Bosnia, are also opposed. But under Congressional pressure, Mr. Clinton had pledged to ask the United Nations to lift the ban by Oct. 15.
An open split was averted when the Bosnian Prime Minister, Haris Silajdzic, suggested a six-month delay in authorizing arms shipments.
Secretary of State Warren Christopher expressed hope that some settlement could be reached during this period, but Mr. Yeltsin was blunt. After a meeting with United States Senators, he said that at the end of six months, "perhaps we can decide once and for all that this should not be done at all."
On another vexing question, that of Russian arms shipments to Iran, Mr. Clinton said only, "We've made progress." In fact, American officials said, Mr. Clinton was not able to extract from Mr. Yeltsin any firm pledge to slow the flow of submarines, tanks and air-launched missiles, which amounted to more than $1 billion last year.
The Russian leader said Russia would honor a contract signed with Iran in 1988 but would sign no new deals. But "we don't know how long or how wide the current pipeline is," an American expert said. Without that information, it is hard to say whether the Russian's new pledge has much significance.
Mr. Yeltsin also refused to budge much on Russian intervention in other nations of the former Soviet Union. Concerned about what might happen if Russian troops went into Nagorno-Karabakh, which is disputed between Armenia and Azerbaijan, the Conference on Security and Cooperation in Europe has asked to monitor any intervention.
So far Russia has balked, and Mr. Yeltsin showed no sign that he would yield. Instead he reasserted his claim to a Russian sphere of influence not unlike that which Washington claims in its hemisphere.
"These are our neighbors," he insisted. "Yesterday we lived in the same house, in the Soviet Union. They're our blood, right? Come on, let's be honest."
In the economic discussions, the emphasis was on American private investment, not American Government aid. The Russians promised to improve the laws governing business, which are so lax and change so often that most foreign business executives have shied away.
The Clinton Administration, for its part, pledged $525 million in financing and insurance for new investments in Russia, and additional agreements are expected to raise the total to $1 billion.
In the days when squabbles between the United States and the Soviet Union shook the world, American presidents encountering stormy political weather at home looked to summit talks for a bit of distraction. But this meeting was so low-key, so "normal," that Mr. Clinton, hard-pressed in the mid-term elections, can probably expect little in the way of direct obvious benefit.
Still, the change in the relationship has been remarkable.
When Mr. Clinton was in Moscow in January, he spent most of his time with Mr. Yeltsin, even though the political situation was confused, with reformers and pragmatists struggling for power. Some Russians and some diplomats complained, arguing that Mr. Clinton should have paid more attention to Prime Minister Viktor S. Chernomyrdin, an economic gradualist.
All that seems like ancient history.
Mr. Chernomyrdin did not come with Mr. Yeltsin to Washington; he is required by the Constitution to stay at home when Mr. Yeltsin is out of the country. But the two have long since reached an understanding on the pace of economic reform, and Mr. Yeltsin praised his erstwhile rival this week.
Several times he even used a very Chernomyrdin-like word -- pragmatism. Eight months ago that word would have upset American officials because it would have sounded like a signal that Mr. Yeltsin planned to ease reform measures as a concession to Russian nationalists.
This week, none of the Americans who heard him batted an eye.

HEADLINE: Kremlin Aide Says Yeltsin Faces a 'Struggle'

BYLINE: By MICHAEL SPECTER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Sept. 28

 BODY:
Suggesting that it will take more than democracy to chase intrigue from the Kremlin, one of President Boris N. Yeltsin's closest aides asserted today that an "unseen struggle" was under way among Mr. Yeltsin's advisers over his political future.
"A struggle is going on for a democratic president, practically speaking, for the next term of Russia's history," the Russian President's press secretary, Vyacheslav Kostikov, told the Interfax News Agency in an interview released this evening. "All the rest are emotions and the dust of political sideshows." 
 The fight is apparently over whether Mr. Yeltsin should run again for President in 1996, whether he should put the elections off until economic reforms have more time to take hold, how he should conduct himself as President, and what, if anything, Mr. Yeltsin should say about it all now.
Mr. Kostikov, often more outspoken than his boss or most other advisers to Mr. Yeltsin, was unexpectedly struck from the list of officials who traveled to the United Nations and Washington this week with the Russian President. So were several other close advisers to Mr. Yeltsin, prompting the newspaper Izvestia to predict mighty power struggles to come.
It is not clear why Mr. Kostikov was did not travel to the United States. But it is also not clear -- despite what Izvestia suggested today -- that the Yeltsin Administration has been torn apart by a split between democrats like Mr. Kostikov and more hard-line guardians of power like Viktor Ilyushin, a former Communist Party official and long a close ally of Mr. Yeltsin.
Mr. Yeltsin has jettisoned many democrats from his inner circle, and with opponents like Aleksandr Rutskoi and Gennadi Zyuganov, the Communist Party leader, already campaigning together with strong appeals to Russian nationalism, it puts Mr. Yeltsin in a difficult political position.
"It is not surprising that the President's name and position are giving rise to open and covert polemics," Mr. Kostikov said in the interview. "The stakes are too high.
"The President is about to take vitally important decisions on the date of the elections," he continued. "He is to decide whether he will seek another term, as a number of leaders are proposing, or use his influence to put off elections."
Clearly, Mr. Yeltsin faces political problems. There are many indicators of severe economic problems -- unproductive factories, unrealistic budgets, and inflation that, though greatly reduced, is still too high. On the other hand, foreign capital is returning to the country and Mr. Yeltsin's political opponents look far weaker than they have in months.
"Will he remain the democratic-minded President we knew in 1991 and 1993?" Mr. Kostikov asked. Will it be "power at all costs?" Mr. Kostikov continued, "or power for the sake of democracy and reform?"

HEADLINE: METRO MATTERS;
Cuomo States the Cuomo Case Best, but Will It Pay Off at the Polls in November?

BYLINE: By Joyce Purnick 

 BODY:
SAY this for Gov. Mario M. Cuomo. Whatever happens on Nov. 8, he will have run what is probably his last campaign, win or lose, on his own terms.
Oh, he's given a perfunctory nod or two to the prevailing political winds. He pledges to cut taxes, has opened the door to endorsing term limits and reminds people that a referendum on the death penalty is O.K. with him -- always carefully adding that he is sure the public would agree with him and vote for life sentences without parole instead. 
 But the essential Mario Cuomo remains the same as he always was as he seeks a fourth term. With the political center shifting to the right, with his own city now led by a Republican who believes in reducing the role of government as a matter of philosophy as well as fiscal need, Mr. Cuomo leaves little doubt that he is comfortable with himself. "I have been an old liberal for a long time," he said during a question-and-answer session of five hours-plus at The New York Times on Monday, later amending the label to "progressive pragmatist."
He may be both, but if a singular observation could be gleaned from so wide-ranging an interview -- irreverently dubbed the "Mariothon" by some participants -- it is that Mario Cuomo is not only an old liberal but an unreconstructed one, in words if not always in deed.
AT a time when people hate government and politicians are turning themselves into pretzels to prove they hate government, too, Mr. Cuomo says hey, wait a minute -- government is O.K., we need it.
At a time when his Republican opponent, State Senator George E. Pataki, says, "We can have better government with less government," the most Mr. Cuomo will allow is, "You must have all the government you need, but only the government you need."
At a time when the polls show people's frustration with government and politicians, the Governor of New York State quotes Lincoln, says today's poor should not be denied welfare any more than his immigrant parents should have been denied help, calls public education "the great doorway to opportunity" and reiterates his belief in government's "obligation to all of the disadvantaged people who can be helped with public education and public health care to give them that."
It may never become an issue in this campaign because Mr. Pataki attacks the Democratic Governor from the right, but it is curious -- supporters would say disappointing -- that despite his elegantly expressed philosophy, Mr. Cuomo cannot point to accomplishments that match his words. Mr. Pataki might not like to admit this, but Mr. Cuomo has not turned New York into the liberal bulwark of the country. Maybe in Mr. Cuomo's heart of hearts he wanted to. But he did not.
His most distinctive achievement is building more prisons than any governor in the state's history. "I'm not happy about it," he says, but he did it because he had no choice: more prisoners needed more cells.
DESPITE the Governor's regard for public education, cities, including New York City, still get less than their fair share of school aid compared with the suburbs. "The funding is unfair and has to be corrected," says Mr. Cuomo, calling his failure to provide more school aid to cities "one of the good things I haven't gotten done." The welfare housing allowance has not been increased in years; local governments still contribute to Medicaid despite the Governor's call for a state takeover of Medicaid costs, and though he favors free tuition in the public university system, tuition has gone up during his tenure.
The three Cuomo terms have had successes, too. And he has been limited in what he could do by a weak national economy and a Republican-led State Senate that, Mr. Cuomo frequently complains, often blocked his initiatives. The Governor says he wants a fourth term to complete the job because, limited by economic problems and that balky State Senate, he didn't do enough "good things" in his three terms. He says he wants to win so he can combat "a harshness and a negativism and a fear out there" that troubles him.
Nobody states Mario Cuomo's case better than Mario Cuomo. Over these next several weeks, his problem may turn out to be that as he campaigns so eloquently on his own terms, in words that ring with the best in American idealism and with a poetic sense of history, those words will come back to haunt him.

HEADLINE: PRESIDENT VOWS VICTORY ON TRADE

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
President Clinton vowed today to keep Congress in session for as long as it takes to approve the global trade agreement after a key Senate Democrat, Ernest F. Hollings, threatened to block a vote on the pact. Failure to approve the accord could imperil the President's economic diplomacy and would be another legislative defeat after health care's demise.
Moving quickly to prevent that, President Clinton used a news conference with President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia to argue for quick passage of legislation carrying out the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade and to make clear that he was ready to adopt extraordinary measures to insure that it happens this year. 
 "This is the biggest trade agreement in history," Mr. Clinton said. "It's the biggest worldwide tax cut in history by reductions of tariffs. It will give us 300,000 to 500,000 new high-wage jobs in the next few years. So I urge the Congress to pass the GATT before they go home."
Partly by accident, partly by the failure of the White House to get the trade accord up to Congress for approval earlier and partly because of quirky Senate rules, Mr. Hollings is in a position to hold up the agreement. [Man in the News, Page D7.]
Mr. Hollings, a Democrat of South Carolina, called the pact a "tremendous danger" to American workers because, he said, its free trade provisions would encourage the flight of low-wage jobs abroad.
The view is shared by few economists or other politicians. Most Republicans support the global accord and most lawmakers of both parties agree that it would win easy passage in both the House and Senate if it can come to a vote. Republican legislators, though, were largely quiet today, apparently enjoying the spectacle of a Democratic Senator wounding his own President.
If the bill "passes the House and doesn't pass the Senate," Mr. Clinton said, "I will urge that the Senate stay in session, take a recess for the election, come back afterward and pass this. The United States has to lead on this, and I intend to do everything I can to see that we do lead."
Less than a quarter of the legislatures or cabinets of countries signing the agreement last December in Geneva have formally approved it since, something that every nation is supposed to do by the end of this year. Most countries are waiting for the United States to do so first. If Washington balks, it could indefinitely stall the pact, which was negotiated over seven years and involves simultaneous tariff reductions by 123 countries. The total tariff cuts worldwide are estimated at $740 billion over a period of 10 years. The GATT accord reduces other barriers to trade, too, and provides patent protection for the American software, drug and other industries.
With such stakes, the betting in Congress and the Administration is that some way, somehow, the President will get around Mr. Hollings and get the enabling legislation passed this year.
Senator Hollings is in a position to obstruct it as chairman of the Senate Commerce Committee, one of several committees in the House and Senate that have jurisdiction over the bill. The White House formally sent the legislation to Congress on Tuesday.
Under so-called fast-track rules intended to speed approval of the measure, all committees with jurisdiction have 45 working days to review the legislation. But since all the details have already been worked out by Congress and the White House in advance, all the committees were expected to send the bill to the floor for votes next week, before scheduled adjournment in mid-October.
For instance, the House Ways and Means Committee voted 35 to 3 today in favor of the measure. By a voice vote, the House Energy and Commerce Committee decided to send it to the floor without a recommendation.
Senator Hollings, though, refused to play along. Ideologically opposed to the global agreement, he argued that it would only encourage the trend of moving low-wage manufacturing jobs out of the country, particularly textile jobs in which his own state specializes. He said he intended to take all 45 days to review the bill in hopes that Congress would adjourn for the year and postpone it until 1995. President Clinton unsuccessfully tried to sway him at an Oval Office meeting on Tuesday.
Mr. Hollings told a news conference today that he was not tying up the bill just to protect the textile industry. "Yes, we'll lose a million textile jobs, but I'm not shilling for a single industry," he said, "I'm shilling for the United States of America."
At the sometimes rambling news session, Mr. Hollings said that the Smoot-Hawley tariff act was "a good thing at the time." The Smoot-Hawley law was a protectionist measure that steeply raised American tariffs in 1930, supposedly to protect jobs. Many economists believe that it helped deepen the Depression.
The point of his hearings, Mr. Hollings said today, would be "to expose GATT for what it is -- a tremendous danger." He said he could not understand why the Administration waited so long to send the bill to Congress, adding that he had told the United States trade representative, Mickey Kantor, in April not to "come in here at the last minute with a we've-got-to-save-the-President kind of thing."
Mr. Hollings said "No chance at all" when asked whether he would permit a vote on the global trade accord by his committee before the 45-day deadline.
Technically, there are cumbersome procedures by which a Congressional committee can vote without the support of its chairman. But it virtually never happens because of the power of the chairman to retaliate.
A senior Administration official said today of Mr. Hollings that "we tried to reason with him, but just could not get through.".
The House Speaker, Thomas S. Foley, said the President had the authority to command senators to return to session, and permit the House to adjourn for the year, assuming it approved the GATT legislation next week, as widely expected. In the Senate, the majority leader, George J. Mitchell, said he was also committed to doing whatever it took to get the pact approved there this year.
A delay in approving the accord this year is critical because the bill is being debated under the fast-track provisions, which mean that no amendments can be added now that it has been introduced. That fast-track authority expires at the end of the year, and many lawmakers believe that it may not be applied to GATT legislation if it is reintroduced next year -- even though it was negotiated under the fast-track rules. If the measure is reintroduced in Congress without the protection of the fast-track procedure, all 535 lawmakers could conceivably try to amend it.
President Clinton has been rather quiet up to now about the global trade legislation, even though economic diplomacy and the promotion of trade have been highlights of his foreign policy. Today, though, he spoke out strongly in favor of the bill and made clear that he would not allow it to be delayed until next year.
Not only would this be bad for the trade pact's prospects, but it would also be embarrassing for the President to attend the Asian economic summit meeting in Indonesia in November or the Latin American economic summit meeting in Miami in December without the accord in place.
"I will do whatever I can within the law to pass the GATT this year," Mr. Clinton said. "It's important that it pass this year. It's important for the United States and our leadership, our efforts to get others to drop their trade barriers, to open their markets to move forward. We have to set an example here."

HEADLINE: Man in the News: Ernest Frederick Hollings;
A Made-in-America Warrior on Free Trade

BYLINE: By EDMUND L. ANDREWS, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
Senator Ernest Frederick Hollings, Democrat of South Carolina, knows exactly what he thinks about free trade agreements: he doesn't like them and he never has.
He returned to that theme today, dismissing the pleas of a President from his own party and singlehandedly delaying passage of the most sweeping effort to reduce trade barriers in more than a decade. 
 In so doing, Mr. Hollings only reinforced a reputation he has acquired over the years as a stubborn if unpredictable and sometimes even eccentric lawmaker.
At 72, he is a commanding presence as he strides into committee rooms with his mane of carefully coiffed white hair and his impeccably tailored suits. But with a booming voice and a penchant for occasionally tasteless rhetoric, he has been known to infuriate listeners with blunt words.
Earlier this year, for example, he incurred the wrath of black groups in the United States when he remarked that African political leaders liked to attend global trade meetings because "rather than eating each other, they'd just come up and get a good square meal in Geneva."
And he created a small furor three years ago, amid trade tensions with Tokyo, by boasting that Japan had been pulverized by American atomic bombs. "You should draw a mushroom cloud and put underneath it, 'Made In America,' " he said, while touring a South Carolina factory that made roller bearings.
Supporters of Mr. Hollings say he has been consistent over the years in his views about trade legislation. He has a deep concern, they say, that other countries do not play by the same rules as the United States.
As chairman of the Senate Commerce Committee, Mr. Hollings has effectively blocked legislation to enact the new General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade by saying he will demand a full 45 legislative days to consider the measure. That would put a vote far beyond the scheduled adjournment of Congress in mid-October, forcing lawmakers to return after the November elections, if the President so decides, or start the approval process all over again next year.
"I'm not shilling for a single industry," Mr. Hollings said today. "I'm shilling for the United States of America."
Mr. Hollings has long sought to protect textile companies in his home state from the low-priced exports of clothing from Asia and Latin America. He worries at length about the loss of manufacturing jobs in America, and he strongly supports the use of Government money to fuel business development.
But he has steadfastly opposed reducing tariff protections for American companies, arguing that such moves will weaken the country.
He was an ardent but unsuccessful foe of the North American Free Trade Agreement, approved by Congress in November, which reduced tariff barriers among the United States, Mexico and Canada. He has also been a persistent critic of the GATT agreement, and tried to prevent the trade pact from winning "fast track" Congressional consideration.
"These treaties promise more of the same 'free trade' magic that has created $100 billion deficits for the past decade," he wrote in 1991 newspaper commentary, attacking both pacts. "Congress is fed up with playing Uncle Sucker."
Mr. Hollings has labored mightily for legislation intended to improve American competitiveness. This year, he was the principal author of a bill to rewrite the nation's telecommunications laws, which would have made it easier for telephone and cable television companies to compete in each other's markets. That bill, which had considerable bipartisan support and was strongly favored by the Clinton Administration, died last week amid last-minute opposition from the regional Bell companies.
Born in 1922 in Charleston, S.C., Mr. Hollings was graduated from the Citadel, received a law degree from the University of South Carolina, then served in the Army in World War II. Before his election to the Senate in 1966, he served as Governor of South Carolina from 1959 to 1963.
He sought the Democratic nomination for president in 1984, but dropped out after losing several early primaries. In the Senate, he has been involved in many issues, first as chairman of the Senate Budget Committee and then as chairman of the Senate Commerce Committee. Among other things, he was a principal author of the Gramm-Rudman-Hollings bill in 1985, which forced automatic spending cuts if Federal deficit targets were missed.
In the trade battle, supporters of Mr. Hollings say he feels that other governments do not abide by the same free-trade tenets as the United States does. "He really believes that there are competing economies that have developed under different principles," an aide to the Senator said today.
Free-market advocates say Mr. Hollings is simply wrong, and that he is being short-sighted about the true merits of expanded global trade. "Anybody who tries to stop the GATT is acting against the interests of the U.S. as a whole," Carla Hills, the United States trade representative for the Bush Administration, said.
She said Mr. Hollings was merely protecting the textile industry. "It's extraordinary that one man on one sector can deprive the nation of the economic benefits that would flow from this agreement."

HEADLINE: Income Tax Resurfaces in Connecticut Campaign

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: HARTFORD, Sept. 28

 BODY:
It's back.
The debate over whether Connecticut should have an income tax, which might have seemed finished in 1991, when 40,000 shrieking protesters on the Capitol lawn failed to roll back the tax, or in 1992, when the legislature was unable to muster the votes for repeal, has re-emerged in 1994 as an unexpected centerpiece in the race for governor. 
 John G. Rowland, the Republican candidate, who said earlier this year that he believed the income tax, imposed in 1991 during a fiscal crisis, was probably here to stay, has recently staked his campaign on a promise to repeal all taxes on income in the state over five years. An independent third-party challenger, Tom Scott, who led and organized the 1991 protests, has gone one better, promising to do it in four years.
The issue here is not just cutting taxes, though both men hold up New Jersey and Massachusetts as tax-cut role models. What has been proposed is an agenda of wholesale amputation: complete elimination of the single largest category of state revenue, which accounts for more than one-third of all state tax collections.
The question, which generated some of the only sparks in an otherwise lackluster debate in Hartford tonight, is whether a repeal plan is realistic.
Only one state -- Alaska, buoyed by its billions in oil industry revenues -- has ever repealed an income tax. And if it is done, at what cost? Is the income tax really responsible, as Mr. Rowland and Mr. Scott argue, for Connecticut's bumpy low-grade recovery from the recession? And would huge tax cuts jolt economic activity into high gear or simply blow apart the state government?
One of the first hurdles for the new abolitionists would be surviving the ire of the state's biggest business lobby, the Connecticut Business and Industry Association. A traditional supporter of Republican causes, the group strongly supports the tax, arguing that the income tax has actually made the state's business environment better.
A spokesman, Joseph F. Brennan, said that because of the income tax, the legislature has been able to cut the corporate tax rate, overhaul the workers' compensation law and create new tax breaks for business, like exempting computers from the personal property tax. Anything that threatens those benefits, or that would add to the tax burden on business, Mr. Brennan said, would be a threat. "We would be opposed to anything that would focus exclusively on individual tax reductions," he said.
Another issue is how much the income tax is even to blame for the state's economic problems. Nonpartisan economists and budget experts who have looked at the Connecticut situation say that the $1 billion increase in taxes that walloped the state in 1991 when the income tax took hold -- a 22 percent jump in one year -- probably did deepen and prolong the recession, by taking money from people's pockets during an uncertain economic period.
On the other hand, State Labor Department figures suggest that many of the job losses since then have had little to do with the tax structure. Cuts in Federal military spending and turmoil in the financial services industry, especially among the big insurers in Hartford, accounted for much of the employment problem.
Since February 1992, for example, when the state's unemployment rate reached a peak at 8.1 percent, through last month, when it stood at 5.5 percent, about 15,000 manufacturing jobs were lost in the state. But all of that loss, and more, was accounted for in the category of transportation manufacturing, which includes most military contracting in the state. About 19,000 jobs in that sector were lost as Navy submarine contracts and jet engine orders sagged. About 10,000 insurance jobs disappeared over the same period.
One factor that has made it easier to argue for tax cuts now is the changing revenue picture. In 1991, tax collections were plummeting so drastically that an income tax came to be regarded by many state lawmakers as the only workable solution to the budget crisis.
The outlook now calls for a string of surpluses. Bonds sold in 1991 to pay off accumulated budget deficits will be paid off by 1996. Tax collection revenues from income and sales taxes are expected to produce a $115 million surplus this year.
The Democratic candidate for governor, William E. Curry Jr., has seized on the revenue growth numbers in pledging a $1 billion reduction in property taxes, through a dedicated fund system that would rebate state money to towns and cities. The gubernatorial candidate from Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr.'s independent A Connecticut Party, Eunice S. Groark, has said she would lower the sales tax, from 6 percent to perhaps 5 percent.
The other big question is the cost of repeal. Mr. Rowland, in his plan this week, outlined $400 million in savings -- achieved in part through reorganization of state agencies and reductions in borrowing and state employee overtime -- in the $9.4 billion state budget. He said that over five years, half of the $2.6 billion in lost annual revenue from the income tax would be offset by these reductions. The remaining revenue, he said, would result from increased economic activity that would spur revenues from other taxes.
Mrs. Groark, who cast the deciding vote for the income tax as Lieutenant Governor, breaking a tie in the State Senate, has attacked those assumptions. She said that the budget's biggest line item, aid to local towns, could not be cut without forcing up local property taxes, and that Medicaid payments, the second largest budget line, could not be significantly cut without causing sanctions from the Federal Government. A spokesman for Mr. Curry argued that the Rowland plan misses the point, and that high property taxes are the real drag on Connecticut economic activity.
In the debate tonight, Mr. Curry also attacked what he called the vagueness of Mr. Rowland's proposal.
"At the heart of it is a set of mammoth tax cuts," Mr. Curry said. But he said that how the state would pay for those cuts, especially after the first year, was unknown. "None of the revenues beyond that are explained, not one penny of them," Mr. Curry said.
Mr. Scott, for his part, says the Rowland plan is just wishful thinking. Eliminating the income tax, he says, can not be done without breaking a few eggs. In the first year, for example, a Scott administration would eliminate all job training and general assistance welfare, and begin dismantling the State Department of Education.
Both men also do more than restore the status quo ante, calling for income on interest, dividends and capital gains, which was taxed for years before 1991, to be fully tax free.
Steven D. Gold, a researcher at the Rockefeller Institute of Government at the State University of New York at Albany, said that abolishing an income tax has been done once in American history: in Alaska, when the Prudhoe Bay fields started pumping crude oil and tax bounty to the state in the 1970's. Gov. Fife Symington of Arizona has also proposed phasing out that state's income tax this year, arguing that with the economy booming, the money is not needed.
Eliminating it in a place like Connecticut, Mr. Gold said, where the recession seems barely to have ended, would be different.
"It could not be done painlessly or easily," he said.

HEADLINE: G.I.'s Take Joy and Relief on the Road

BYLINE: By RICK BRAGG, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: GONAIVES, Haiti, Sept. 26

 BODY:
The rivets that held the cast-iron Jesus on His concrete cross rusted through months ago, and the Savior tumbled to the ground. Instead of putting Him back on the cross, residents of this poor, sun-bleached seaside city just propped Him up at the base.
The people of Gonaives are impatiently waiting for a different kind of savior. 
 Since Saturday, when United States soldiers first flew in on roaring, dust-whipping helicopters and freed them from the hated army and police here, Haitians have been singing and praising the name of the exiled President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide. Gonaives, one of Haiti's poorest, most repressed cities, is like a terminal patient blessed with a miracle cure.
"It is happiness," said Robert Jean, 33, who has a scar on his neck where, he said, a soldier's bullet passed through. But United States soldiers with the Army Special Forces detachment sent to secure the town say Gonaives is just one of many cities that could explode at any time because the old hatreds have built for so long.
In the last three years, said residents in this city of about 25,000, anyone who even mentioned the name of Father Aristide was jailed and tortured. Their freedom is like rum to them, the people said: They are drunk on it.
Residents have gathered by the thousands around the Haitian Army barracks in the center of the city, and have been peaceful so far, said Maj. Mark O'Neill of the Special Forces. That is a good thing, he said, since there are only about 100 United States troops in Gonaives.
Many of the people, poorer and more insulated than those in Port-au-Prince, had never seen a helicopter except as a distant speck in the sky. For three days they have run cheering to the landing area, a dirt soccer field, then run away again screaming as the rotor sent stinging sand into their eyes. But they laughed anyway.
"There must have been 10,000 people who welcomed us here," said Major O'Neill. "I felt like Montgomery going into Salerno."
Major O'Neill, a square-jawed but smiling officer who looks like he just stepped off a recruiting poster, is proud that his soldiers have made Gonaives a less fearful place. Beatings have stopped, he said, and the jails -- once filled with political prisoners -- have been emptied.
But the American presence has shifted the fear from one people to another: Haitian soldiers keep inside the old yellow barracks they share with the Army Rangers, and seldom walk outside unless accompanied by American soldiers.
The ring of people around the barracks, too peaceful to be called a demonstration, is constant. The people point out individual soldiers who beat relatives, and name Haitian officers who they say ordered the killing of friends.
"They can't lead us anymore," said Jean Remy Dophen, who had two pictures of Father Aristide pinned to his shirt. "The Americans give us security, but we are still waiting for deliverance."
Mr. Dophen said the people in the crowd would love to surge forward and take justice on all the soldiers who persecuted them, but one thing held them back, and it was not the guns of the American soldiers.
"We won't take vengeance," he said, "because Father Aristide told us not to."
One Ranger sergeant said American troops had no intention of firing into the crowd to protect the Haitian soldiers.
"We see people in the crowd, stirring them up," said Sgt. 1st Class George Scott. Today one man walked through the crowd passing out leaflets that showed a man wielding a bloody machete, encouraging people to take control of the city.
"We can't control a mob the size of these crowds," Sergeant Scott said, and we're not ready to start shooting civilians."
The American soldiers are beginning a campaign to defuse the explosive situation in Gonaives, a campaign that is largely public relations.
The Rangers sent emissaries today to meet with religious leaders and ask their help in calming the people, and in identifying the plain-clothes auxiliaries called attaches who may represent the greatest threat to peace.
"We need peace," said the Rev. Max Mesidon, a Catholic priest, "so you must take the guns from the people in civilian clothes."
The Rangers plan to ask the priests to help them identify the attaches so they can be disarmed; it would be the first such partnership between the occupying army and the Catholic Church.
The Catholic hierarchy in Haiti has aligned itself with the country's elite, who backed and financed the coup that toppled Father Aristide on Sept. 30, 1991. But in poorer sections like Gonaives, priests are more likely to be on the side of the poor.
The Rangers, usually in combat, find themselves in the role of peacemaker, but it is essential if they are going to prevent a civil war in this isolated city. "It's p.r.," said Sergeant Scott, who has been trained to establish relations with townspeople to ward off confrontations.
Major O'Neill said there was one other thing the Rangers could do to calm the people of Gonaives, if they could: he wishes the army could distribute food. "These people are hungry," he said.
Many people in the crowd have been afflicted by the United Nations embargo on Haiti and by the internal strife. Although relief aid was exempt from the embargo, corruption and isolation have choked much of the aid here, and the embargo has destroyed the economy.
"If the U.N. would end the embargo, it would help them," said Major O'Neill. "But the army doesn't drive the train."

Jean-Bertrand Aristide as a different kind of savior, and some people are beginning to feel a new sense of hope. (Phillippe Diederich/Contact Press Images, for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Stocks Climb Modestly in Heavy Trading

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
Following Tuesday's signal by the Federal Reserve that it would not immediately raise interest rates, the stock market rose modestly yesterday -- its third consecutive day of advancing prices. So far this week, the Dow Jones industrial average has regained about 46 points of last week's 102-point fall.
After rising as much as 28 points, the Dow closed at 3,878.18, up 15.14 in heavy trading. The broader Standard & Poor's 500 rose 2.76 points, to 464.81, and the smaller-company Nasdaq composite index rose 4.64 points, to 760.01. 
 But yesterday's market may have little to do with any change in investor sentiment after the Fed's non-action. The rally was helped by strong demand yesterday at the $11 billion auction in five-year Treasury notes, which helped lower long-term interest rates, and by some optimism about United States-Japan trade talks, which helped lift the dollar against the Japanese yen and German mark. Both developments make stocks more valuable.
Such support for stocks could prove temporary. Forthcoming economic data -- like unemployment, factory-use and consumer price data -- may persuade traders that the Fed will raise interest rates and prompt traders to sell stocks.
Still, data released yesterday by the mutual-fund industry shows that equity portfolio managers have gotten even more money to invest and therefore are under pressure to buy stocks.
During the first eight months of 1994, inflows of new cash into stock funds totaled $93.5 billion, or roughly the annual revenue of Exxon. That is up 12 percent from the 1993 period, according to the Investment Company Institute, the trade group for mutual funds.
But redemptions, or investors cashing out of funds, were up, too, for stocks. That is especially worrisome since the redemptions occurred in a month when stock prices were up sharply and presumably meant that some investors thought prices had peaked.
August redemptions rose 18 percent from the previous month to $11.1 billion. The Dow reached its second-highest peak of this year on Sept. 15.
By contrast, bond fund redemptions leveled off in August, even before long-term interest rates headed decisively from 7.4 percent to 7.8 percent this month. Bond redemptions at $13.6 billion in August were little changed from $13.5 billion in July. Net cash outflow increased, however, probably because bondholders in the previous months had their principal cut sharply.
Stock market analysts worry that as bond yields approach 8 percent, hundreds of thousands of individual investors will pull their money out of stocks and put it into bonds, sending stock prices falling.
Volume on the New York Stock Exchange at 330.3 million shares was about 15 percent above this year's average of 287 million shares.
Roughly seven stocks rose for every four that fell on the New York Stock Exchange. Cincinnati Milacron, a machine-tool maker, rose 2 1/2, to 26, for example, after a Merrill Lynch analyst raised her recommendation to intermediate "buy" from "above average." The Andrew Corporation, a supplier of telecommunications equipment, rose 3 3/4, to 48 1/4, after it got a large contract to supply television-broadcast antennas to the Fox network.
The American depository receipts of Telefonos de Mexico, often used by institutional investors as a proxy for the Mexican stock market, fell 1 1/8, to 63 1/4, after a top official of the ruling Institutional Revolutionary Party was shot and killed.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index rose 1.50 points, to 256.37, with advancing stocks outnumbering declining stocks by 1,401 to 806. The American Stock Exchange market value index rose 2.78 points, to 456.20.

HEADLINE: Favorite in Brazil to Stress Private Sector Over State

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: SAO PAULO, Brazil, Sept. 28

 BODY:
With this country's presidential election only five days away, its front-runner, Fernando Henrique Cardoso, promised today to break up Brazil's longstanding state monopolies and attract foreign investment in oil, mining, telecommunications, and highway and dam construction.
"We have 16 hydroelectric dams paralyzed," Mr. Cardoso told foreign journalists here, and said this tied up the equivalent of $2 billion. 
 "Why not have foreign capital participate?" he said.
Formerly Finance Minister, Mr. Cardoso while in office introduced a plan to curb inflation that has cut Brazil's monthly rate to 1.5 percent in September from 45 percent in June. A new currency was introduced, and prices, wages, rents and contracts were no longer based on the practice of indexing for inflation.
Mr. Cardoso is forecast to win the election on Monday by a margin of seven to nine percentage points, according to polls this week.
Quietly confident of victory, the 63-year-old Social Democrat today offered his clearest vision so far of the future for foreign trade and investment in Brazil, Latin America's largest economy. This is the last major country in South America to remove restrictive barriers to foreign investment in key industries and businesses.
Mr. Cardoso has supported Brazil's recent reductions in tariffs, as part of a free trade policy that is increasing United States exports by 25 percent this year.
"Nafta doesn't frighten us," he said today, referring to the North American Free Trade Agreement involving Mexico, the United States and Canada, a pact some Brazilians regard with suspicion.
"We are a continental nation," he continued, noting that Brazil's trade is divided among Latin America, North America, Europe and Asia. "We cannot ally ourselves only in one direction."
Mr. Cardoso held out the possibility of a Western Hemisphere free-trade accord as a final goal, but he said his first priority would be to speed integration with the rest of Latin America, and push for the rapid creation of a South American free-trade zone.
"Manaus needs energy. Why not run a power line from Guri Dam to Manaus?" he said, referring to a proposed power link between Venezuela's largest hydroelectric dam and the biggest city in the Brazilian Amazon.
"Colombia has coal near the coast," he continued. "Why not build a thermoelectric plant in Pernambuco," a city in northeastern Brazil.
Breaking with Brazil's tradition of closed markets, Mr. Cardoso and his campaign advisers have said that such major public projects should be opened to bids from foreign construction companies.
"Either they enter partnerships with the private sector, or there will not be expansion in these areas," he said, referring to companies working in infrastructure development, oil production and telephone service. "We should make the oil monopoly more flexible."
Mr. Cardoso, who served for 10 years in the Brazilian Senate, said that early next year he would seek to change the Constitution, which bars private foreign investment in oil and telecommunications and limits it to minority participation in mining.
A previous attempt to change the Constitution failed in May. But polls predict that the voters will give Mr. Cardoso's center-right coalition three-fifths of the seats in both houses of the Brazilian Congress, enough to amend the Constitution.
Mr. Cardoso has previously indicated that he wants to open the economy. Last week, Brazil's leading business magazine, Exame, quoted him as saying, "Certainly, there is no reason to maintain the state telephone monopoly."
In the interview, Mr. Cardoso added that he would sell the mining corporation Vale do Rio Doce, which is Brazil's sixth-largest state company, with $2 billion in sales last year.
"We are going to speed privatizations enormously," he said.

HEADLINE: Inflation-Wary Investors Are Leaving Bond Funds

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS 

 BODY:
Inflation fears are striking mutual fund investors, who are moving money into gold stock funds while taking it out of bond funds, which have been damaged by rising interest rates.
"Investors are clearly responding to a perceived inflationary environment," Neal Litvack, an executive vice president of Fidelity Investments, said yesterday. He pointed to a survey of investors, done for Fidelity by the University of Michigan, which showed more than 50 percent of those questioned this month think inflation will turn up in the next six months. In recent months, that figure had hovered near 20 percent. 
 At T. Rowe Price Associates, another large mutual fund company, officials reported a surge of interest in its New Era Fund, which invests in natural resources companies and is often viewed as an inflation hedge. Such flows have helped sustain the inflow of money into stock funds, although the flow appears to have fallen off this month from a very strong pace in August, when $14.1 billion poured into stock funds.
At the same time, it appears that the outflow from bond funds, which has been going on since last spring, is increasing amid concerns that the Federal Reserve will raise interest rates this fall. Figures released yesterday by the Investment Company Institute, a trade group, showed that bond funds suffered a net cash outflow of $2.8 billion in August, and fund executives say the figure has almost certainly worsened this month.
When it comes to fighting inflation, there is a certain chicken-and-egg factor regarding the Fed and the public. The evidence of growing inflation fears could help persuade the Fed it needs to tighten credit further, to help counter those fears. Yet, with little acceleration in the Consumer Price Index, one factor that has helped fan those fears is the Fed's highly publicized previous efforts to raise interest rates to fight inflation.
At Fidelity, Mr. Litvack said that the net inflow of cash into the firm's gold stock fund this month was the first this year. For the industry as a whole, however, such funds have seen net inflows since May.
The cash flow figures include sales and redemptions of mutual funds, as well as net transfers between funds, but they do not include reinvested dividends.
While bond funds now offer much juicier yields than they did when interest rates bottomed last October, investors seem to be focusing more on the principal losses that have come from rising interest rates, which they fear will continue. As a group, bond funds have seen net cash outflows every month since March. And government bond funds have suffered withdrawals in every month since November.
While government bond funds took in a net $5.9 billion in 1993, they have seen withdrawals of $13.7 billion in the first eight months of this year. As a result of those withdrawals, as well as losses from rising interest rates, total assets of government bond funds fell below $100 billion in August for the first time since January 1992; they are down 23 percent from the peak reached in July of last year.
Among stock funds, some managers say that the public has lost its appetite for the riskier funds, and instead has moved either toward funds viewed as inflation hedges or as less aggressive. While most said cash kept coming into stock funds this month, Kemper Mutual Funds said it had seen many withdrawals.
Although stocks have done little this year, the continued inflows have been remarkable. The August figure was the best since February, and the fourth-best month ever. So far in 1994, $93.4 billion has gone into stock funds, leaving them on a pace to break the full-year record of $127.9 billion set last year. Much of that strength has come from international funds, which took in $35.9 billion in the first eight months, just below the 1993 full-year record of $37.8 billion.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: Copper Falls After Raising Of Margin Requirements

 BODY:
Copper prices fell yesterday, pulling back from a four-year high set Monday, after the Commodity Exchange of New York moved to decrease speculation by raising the deposit required to buy contracts.
On other markets, gold and soybeans fell. The Commodity Research Bureau index of 21 commodities fell 0.77 point, to 230.88. 
 The Commodity Exchange increased by sevenfold in two days the amount speculators and commercial copper traders must deposit with their brokers. It took the action hoping to slow the price rise in copper for September delivery. The September contract had risen 6.7 percent since Sept. 13 as plunging inventories forced copper consumers to buy contracts that would deliver copper quickly.
Yet even with the higher deposits prices held steady. The September copper contract fell 0.80 cent, to $1.235 a pound, down from Monday's four-year high of $1.278. Most traders agreed to pay the higher deposits, also known as margins, to secure adequate supplies, analysts said.
"The fact that so many people stayed around meant they had to stay around," said Richard Hirsch, vice president with Deutsche Bank's Sharps Pixley unit in New York. "This is real demand, real consumption. They're not just putting it on the shelf somewhere."
Gold dropped below $400 an ounce as a rise in the United States dollar made the metal more expensive for foreign investors and raised confidence that inflation was not damaging the economy.
On Tuesday, gold prices settled above $400 an ounce for the first time since August 1993 after the Federal Reserve left interest rates intact. Traders were concerned that a growing United States economy would speed inflation without higher interest rates. A higher dollar eased some of those concerns yesterday.
December gold fell $3.50, to $397.90 an ounce, on the Commodity Exchange. December silver lost 11 cents, to $5.65 an ounce.
Soybeans fell to their lowest price in almost two years as drier weather allowed a speedy harvest after last week's rain. November soybeans fell 5.75 cents, to $5.485 a bushel, at the Chicago Board of Trade.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Short-Coverings Spur Rise in Treasury Issues

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities moved higher yesterday, sparked by short-coverings as the dollar strengthened and gold weakened. The rally weathered a strong economic report, helped by talk of some progress in the Japan-United States trade negotiations and a better-than-expected five-year note auction.
The 30-year bond rose 10/32 , to a price of 9613/32 , for a yield of 7.81 percent, down from 7.84 percent. Shorter bill rates were down as well, as demand eased toward the end of the quarter. 
 Kevin McClintock, head of fixed- income investments for Aeltus Investment Management Inc., said the market's performance was much better than he would have expected.
The Commerce Department's durable goods report for August, he said, was largely sloughed off by the market, even though the 6 percent gain was much greater than the expectations of a 3.9 percent rise. However, many traders considered it together with the July decline of 4 percent decline. "My read is that it is further evidence of strong momentum in the economy," Mr. McClintock said. "It's much like a stagecoach that tends to gain momentum and feed on itself if left unrestrained. The Fed in my view has not been overly restrictive. They've picked up the reins and snugged backed some, but have not yanked back. But until they yank back, the coach will gain momentum."
Mr. McClintock predicts the next rate rise will most likely be 50 basis points, as were the last two. "If you ask me if that will be enough, I'd have to say I don't think so," he said. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
"The rate direction bias is upward over the next couple of months," Mr. McClintock continued, "and another Fed tightening is highly likely. A modest Fed tightening is not necessarily good for the bond market, it is the last tightening that it gets that is good for bonds."
Employment, price and industrial reports for September will be released in the next two weeks, giving the Fed additional data to consider.
Traders said Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen in a news conference yesterday before his trip to Madrid added to the positive tone of the market, making some cautious upbeat comments about the dollar and the United States-Japan trade negotiations. He said that a strong dollar was important for the economy and that any trade sanctions against Japan would be narrowly devised rather than broad-based. Analysts thought those comments were consistent with expectations among most market participants that some form of limited progess would emerge from the current trade talks.
Many in the market predicted the Treasury's $11 billion auction of five-year notes would be a much tougher sale since many investors have been reluctant to buy longer maturities in a bear-market environment. But Tuesday's $17.25 billion two-year sale also drew less demand than expected because of the uncertainty about a possible rate rise.
While the Fed's Open Market Committee did not change its policy on Tuesday, market speculation puts the next likely date for tightening as the Nov. 15 F.O.M.C. meeting. Strong data like the durable goods report today, some traders said, add to the risk of tightening.
The 50-basis-point move on Aug. 16, bought the Fed some time to assess the impacts of the five actions to date. But if the economy shows signs that inflationary pressures have not been kept in check, the Fed will have to move quickly to keep its credibility as an inflation fighter intact.
Mr. McClintock said there was good institutional and retail interest in the five-year note. Some desks reported that demand from both sides was above the average of the last 12 auctions. The results were better than expected and the market responded with more short-covering going into late afternoon. The bill sector also enjoyed strong bidding, especially the three-month maturity. The three-month rate fell 21 basis points, to 4.50 percent, from 4.71 percent the previous day.
Even with a new quarter traders expect demand for bills might not altogether disappear simply because they have a defensive value in a bear market.

HEADLINE: Durables Orders Post A 6% Rise

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
Orders for big-ticket factory goods in August made their largest gains since late 1992 as new-model cars and trucks dominated the Census Bureau's monthly tally, suggesting only a modest slowing of the economy.
Although last month's 6 percent jump followed a 4 percent decline in July, analysts said the economy was proving remarkably resilient in the face of efforts by the Federal Reserve to restrain its growth. 
 "The economy's slowing a little, but perhaps not enough" to avoid further Fed increases in short-term interest rates, said Ellen Hughes-Cromwick, an economist with Mellon Bank in Pittsburgh. "The slowdown is modest at best."
For now, however, the Fed is taking a wait-and-see approach. On Tuesday, its top policy committee met but announced no change in rates.
While automobile manufacturing was responsible for more than half the rise in August, all major categories of orders for durable goods advanced. This included civilian capital goods other than aircraft, a widely watched gauge of business investment, which climbed 1.5 percent, to a level 20 percent above that of August 1993.
Today's report also showed shipments of durable goods, which range from fax machines to ships, rose 6.1 percent in August while unfilled orders eased three-tenths of 1 percent.
The bond market took the latest figures, which were a bit stronger than most analysts had expected, largely in stride. Prices fell initially, raising interest rates, but soon recovered. [Page D9.]Stock prices also rose today, with the Dow Jones industrial average closing up 15.14 points. [Page D10.]
"The industrial sector is healthy, but not accelerating as the August durables report might suggest," said Bruce Steinberg, an economist with Merrill Lynch & Company. Policy makers are seeking to prevent the economy from overheating, thereby presumably keeping inflation at bay.
On Thursday, the Commerce Department is to publish what is expected to be another small upward revision, to about 4 percent, in its calculation of the economy's growth pace in the second quarter. Last month the estimate was 3.8 percent.
The 6 percent climb in orders last month, to $154 billion, was the 11th in 13 months and the biggest since December 1992, the bureau noted.
This summer's statistical gyrations in the automotive industry, analysts said, were probably magnified by the lag in conforming seasonal adjustments to the relatively new industry practice of shutting plants down for two weeks in July for retooling and vacation. Previously, the closings were spread over a longer period that extended into August.
Orders for cars and other transportation equipment soared 19.1 percent for the month, far surpassing the gains of 4.8 percent for primary metals, 4 percent for industrial machinery and equipment, and 1.2 percent for electronic and other electrical equipment.
For capital goods, like engines and communications equipment, the overall rise was 3.7 percent, with military orders up 7.9 percent following a 21.4 percent drop in July, and civilian orders including aircraft up 3 percent.
Not surprisingly, the rise in shipments paralleled orders for durable goods, because the Government regards new orders and shipments for automotive assembly as identical. The 6.1 percent rise in shipments was the fifth increase this year and the biggest since August 1989.
Although backlogs of unfilled orders eased a bit over all, when military and civilian aircraft are excluded, backlogs have been soaring for more than a year, said Ed Yardeni, economist at C. J. Lawrence/Deutsche Bank Securities.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
Race and gender data for small-business loans break new ground.

BYLINE: By Peter Passell 

 BODY:
ANOTHER shoe dropped in President Clinton's campaign to stamp out lending discrimination against poor neighborhoods and minorities, as Federal regulators proposed standards this week for what banks must do to meet obligations that have been law for nearly two decades. But that is hardly the end of the tale. For the proposal breaks new ground in one area -- race and gender discrimination in small-business loans -- that many economists and some regulators think would better be left untouched.
Indeed, Lawrence B. Lindsey, the Federal Reserve governor who helped negotiate the new rules, is in rebellion. "A change in policy of this magnitude is best left to Congress, not to a small coterie of regulators," he said in a statement. 
 In 1974 Congress passed the Equal Credit Opportunity Act, which barred discrimination in lending by race, religion and gender. Three years later it added the Community Reinvestment Act, an equally uncontroversial piece of legislation at the time, but a horse of an entirely different color.
In sweeping (if unspecific) language the Community Reinvestment Act required banks to lend within their own communities, an idea that strikes almost everyone other than economists as a matter of equity and common sense. Many of the latter -- among them, Lawrence White of New York University's Stern School of Business -- say banks' real job is funneling capital to its most productive use, whether that happens to be in the next block or the next state.
"Bankers ought to be doing their best for shareholders and depositors," Mr. White argued; if that means the savings of Brooklynites end up in Silicon Valley, so be it. If government wishes to solve social problems with investments, he said, it ought to be up front about bearing the risk.
No one quite knew what was required to meet the act's standards, and the regulators were not much help. Typically, when a bank had a request for a new branch or a merger pending, it would produce a flurry of activity to show that it was community-minded -- much the way television stations show "Buckle Up for Safety" ads when their licenses are up for renewal.
Interestingly, though, Mr. Lindsey, who might best be classified a soft-nosed conservative, thinks the act did little harm and some good. The unmeasurable threat from the regulators, he suggests, served as a check on lenders who would otherwise have been too stodgy or myopic to seek profits in low-income neighborhoods. But neither banks nor community groups were happy with this nonpolicy. And after President Clinton was elected, he vowed to put teeth into the law.
The initial idea was to set specific targets for low-income neighborhood lending. But when push came to shove, the regulators could not come up with a test that could be broadly applied. So they settled for something more than the current policy of muddling through and something less than the President apparently wanted.
The proposed rules fill 100 double-spaced pages. The essence, though, is straightforward: more focus on actual loan performance in low- income areas, less on kaffeeklatsches with community groups. Regulators will retain great discretion in assaying compliance, but they will be under pressure to back their decisions with statistics. And while nobody is dancing in the aisles, the public response has largely been positive.
The same cannot be said, however, for the new data collection requirements on small-business loans, the portion of the new regulations pressed by the Comptroller of the Currency -- which by no coincidence happens to be the bank regulatory agency most closely tied to the White House. Under the proposal, businesses whose stock is not publicly held will be asked to identify the race and gender, by percentage, of their owners.
One problem here, Mr. Lindsey notes, is that the Community Reinvestment Act is supposed to be about geography, not race or gender. Another is that categorization raises practical problems. Is a business owned by a Japanese national a minority? The rule says yes. Is a minority business that is 40 percent black and 60 percent white a minority business? The rule says no.
But the deeper issue, Mr. Lindsey argues, is whether it makes social, economic or political sense to identify businesses as having races and genders. Such classifications are, on their face, offensive to many Americans. And the numbers, he believes, have little potential for providing insight into discrimination, since the criteria for judging the creditworthiness of businesses are far less quantifiable than, say, car loans.
The real mystery, perhaps, is why the data question was tossed into the rule-making. For while there is no evidence that President Clinton favors race or gender quotas in private lending, he is opening himself to the charge. And unlike most of the scars President Clinton bears from playing two years of Washington hardball, this one would be self-inflicted.
