HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
House Subcommittee Passes Altered Antitrust Bill

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
Efforts to end the labor dispute that has wiped out the World Series for the first time in 90 years moved further on Capitol Hill today than they have in months of negotiations between owners and players. But a House of Representatives subcommittee that approved a bill dealing with baseball's antitrust exemption and its labor problems altered the bill just enough to raise questions about its effectiveness.
The subcommittee on economic and commercial law, in an overwhelming voice vote, sent to the House Judiciary Committee a bill that would make unilaterally imposed rules, such as the owners' salary cap proposal, subject to antitrust laws. Baseball has been exempt from antitrust laws in all matters since a 1922 United States Supreme Court decision. The partial removal of the exemption would enable the players to challenge a salary cap in court if the owners decided to impose it during the off season. 
 "It's not surprising," Bud Selig, the acting commissioner, said by telephone from Milwaukee. "Jack Brooks said he was going to do this so nothing about it is surprising."
Brooks, a Texas Democrat, is chairman of the Judiciary Committee, which is expected to approve the bill today. It was introduced by Mike Synar, Democrat of Oklahoma.
"It's got momentum going now," Synar said. "I have little doubt we will have an overwhelming vote tomorrow. We're putting both sides on notice that Congress will no longer remain silent on the issue."
But before sending it on, the subcommittee amended the bill, eliminating the provision that said if the players union went to court to block a unilaterally imposed salary cap, the cap would be stayed automatically until a decision was rendered.
Donald Fehr, the head of the union, had said he would recommend the players end their strike and return to work if the bill were passed as originally written with the stay provision. After hearing about today's development, he said the bill "could still be a positive step," but he said he didn't know if he could make the same recommendation.
Nevertheless, he said by telephone from Phoenix, where he held a regional players meeting, "I treat it as very encouraging, especially because there was only one dissent."
Hamilton Fish Jr., Republican of New York, voiced the lone "nay" on the bill.
Synar said if the House didn't act on the bill by the end of this session, which very likely will be next week, he would expect it to be one of the first items on the agenda in February or March. Synar himself, though, will not be around to vote for his own bill. He lost his primary race.
The development, however, offered encouragement to authors of a similar bill in the Senate, Howard Metzenbaum, Democrat of Ohio, and Orrin Hatch, Republican of Utah.
Metzenbaum said he would try to attach a floor amendment with similar implications to a District of Columbia appropriations bill set for debate on the Senate floor Thursday, although an aide said he may wait.
"It may be better to wait until they get their bill out of committee," the aide said. "They've got a little momentum going. We may go tomorrow afternoon; we may wait a little longer. We didn't want to rush it. And we may just go ahead and use their language."

HEADLINE: CHRONICLE

BYLINE: By NADINE BROZAN 

 BODY:
Former President GEORGE BUSH and his wife, BARBARA, have a new neighbor in Houston with whom they can talk politics -- if they want to. Secretary of the Treasury LLOYD M. BENTSEN bought a six-bedroom, six-bathroom town house in the Tanglewood district earlier this month from Joseph D. Jamail, according to the deed filed with the Harris County Appraisal District.
The purchase price was not disclosed. The secretary paid "considerably more than $500,000," Howard M. Schloss, a spokesman for Mr. Bentsen, said yesterday. 
 Mr. Bentsen's three grown children, two sons and a daughter, live in Houston, and he wanted a house in a city of his home state that could be reached by a nonstop flight from Washington, Mr. Schloss said. Mr. Bentsen, 73, who made a fortune in insurance and savings and loan associations before running for the Senate in 1970, also has a town house in Washington and a vacation house in Southern California.
His newest acquisition has fueled persistent rumors in Washington that he is planning to retire in the near future.
Mr. Bentsen downplayed the speculation, saying through his spokesman: "It's a great time to be Secretary of the Treasury. I couldn't have written better economic numbers myself."
Then he added, "If you keep asking me often enough, eventually you'll be right."

HEADLINE: Trade Bill Sabotage

 BODY:
Ernest Hollings, chairman of the Senate Commerce Committee, declared yesterday that he will keep the Senate from voting this session on the trade accord that the Clinton Administration signed with more than 100 other countries last April in Marrakesh, Morocco. This is a craven and destructive bow to the demands of textile firms in his home state of South Carolina, who fear competition from abroad under the accord. If Mr. Hollings is successful, as he well might be, he would thereby delay and possibly block for good approval of a bill that would add $200 billion a year to the U.S. economy.
Mr. Hollings is the main culprit, but the White House was his enabler. It dallied in getting the legislation to Congress. Then it rejected advice to sidestep Mr. Hollings's committee. The danger is that the Senate vote could be delayed until next year, or longer. Delay, in turn, could jeopardize approval here and abroad. 
 The legislative rules that govern the trade accord are known as fast track -- which requires Congress to vote quickly and without amendment. Under the rules, Mr. Hollings's committee has 45 days to deliberate before sending the bill to the floor. But the 45-day period did not begin until Tuesday, when the Administration delivered its bill to Congress, and Congress is scheduled to break for the election campaign in nine days.
The Administration took months to settle upon ways to pay for the bill; it spent precious weeks fighting, unsuccessfully, for a provision to apply fast track to future trade deals. By the time the trade bill arrived at Mr. Hollings's door, he could put off a vote past the end of the session -- in effect nullifying fast track.
Even as late as Tuesday, however, the Administration could have run around Mr. Hollings by removing two nonessential provisions from its bill, thereby denying the commerce committee jurisdiction. But the Administration was not certain the strategy would work and it feared alienating the powerful chairman. With the benefit of hindsight, the White House seems to have erred.
If the trade bill is put off until next year, it might not qualify for fast-track status because the statute expires at the end of the year. That would open up the bill to deal-breaking amendments and parliamentary delay. The loss would be large. For 50 years, the U.S. has led its partners through successful rounds of open trade accords, each one giving a powerful boost to the world economy. The Marrakesh accord would add trillions to world production by dismantling barriers to trade and investment.
The Administration has possible ways, none good, to get the trade bill back on track. It could pressure Mr. Hollings, but that is likely to fail. It could try a quick parliamentary maneuver to grab the bill away from the commerce committee, but the tactic is legally suspect and perhaps politically unrealistic. The fallback tactic, mentioned yesterday by the President, is to keep the Senate in session, at least officially, through elections so that the 45 days elapse and the Senate is forced to vote after elections. This strategy seems feasible, but it would also be ugly -- visibly displaying the Administration's inability to push high-priority bills through a Democrat-controlled Congress just as voters prepare to cast their ballots.
This has been a brutal week for Mr. Clinton. The Senate majority leader, George Mitchell, buried health care reform, blaming obstructionist Republicans. Now, a fellow Democrat knocks down the Administration's second most important initiative, free trade. The Administration unwittingly aided and abetted. But Mr. Hollings has single-handedly jeopardized the economic fortunes of the world for some short-lived, parochial interests.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial

HEADLINE: Journal;
Fire the Messengers

BYLINE: By FRANK RICH 

 BODY:
When a President speaks out of both sides of his mouth with the whole country watching, no press secretary's spin can save him. So it's hardly Dee Dee Myers's fault that even some of Bill Clinton's loyal partisans use phrases like "the last straw" to characterize the President's shotgun marriage to the Haiti strongmen formerly known as thugs. The televised images of the thugs' thugs wielding crowbars in Port-au-Prince may overpower any words emanating from the White House for some time to come.
Just the same, who can doubt that Mr. Clinton has the worst public relations of anyone in politics, Dan Quayle included? The President has done more than a few things right, but such is his low estate that even the foolproof tonic of a growing economy won't lift his poll numbers. When Mr. Clinton isn't busy being his own worst enemy, sending forth mixed messages about Haiti or his numerical definition of universal health care, his inept messengers work overtime seeing to it that the good news is garbled as well. 
 This is why Mr. Clinton was right to give Leon Panetta the authority to overhaul the White House staff. But instead of ratifying Mr. Panetta's overdue personnel shuffle, Mr. Clinton botched it -- so much so that he turned poor Ms. Myers into the human correlative of his Haiti policy.
The pattern of the two flip-flops was identical. The White House signaled Ms. Myers's exile in advance as clearly as it had Gen. Raoul Cedras's. At the last minute, Mr. Clinton was persuaded in an end-run -- Ms. Myers usurping Mr. Panetta as Jimmy Carter had Warren Christopher -- to reverse course: Ms. Myers stayed put, as the general did, receiving new respect and, in her case, a promotion. (And even this resolution is provisional, since the White House let it be known that Ms. Myers, unlike the general, will definitely flee by New Year's.)
What prompted the abrupt reprieve was Mr. Clinton's fear that Ms. Myers's departure would look bad for a White House that treats her and other women as second-class citizens. But the President's inability to allow women to flourish in top White House jobs, however lamentable, is irrelevant to the crisis in communications at hand.
Administration achievements now happen largely in obscurity (the May signing of the education bill) while its most inept p.r. schemes (the hapless health care bus caravan) unravel in prime time. In my experience, even simple requests for information are beyond the press office's competence. Why promote a key player in this mess just long enough to do more damage during the high-stakes election season?
Surely the transparent tokenism of Ms. Myers's temporary reprieve will fail to preserve the Administration's waning feminist reputation, any more than the don't-ask-don't-tell compromise placated those who thought Mr. Clinton sold out gays in the military. What the reversal on Ms. Myers has mainly accomplished, besides reinforcing the President's image as a waffler, is to destroy the credibility of the latest White House savior, Mr. Panetta.
The previous savior was David Gergen, engineer of the last press-office upheaval. Mr. Gergen's legacy as a communications guru was summed up in a valedictory interview with Ted Koppel last Friday.
From his perch in the State Department, Mr. Gergen deftly slipped a new knife into whatever remains of Warren Christopher's back by noting that the Secretary had to tear himself away from an Embassy party as the apparently unanticipated Port-au-Prince beatings surfaced on TV. (At least Mr. Christopher, who had attended "Quiz Show" during the Carter-Cedras negotiations, didn't have to be tracked down at "Natural Born Killers.") As for Ms. Myers, Mr. Gergen oozed, "Everybody cares about Dee Dee, everybody loves Dee Dee," in a tone that couldn't have been more patronizing.
The message the departing Mr. Gergen sent was that of an out-to-lunch Administration buffeted by events rather than in charge of them, and of a White House in which every man (and I do mean man) is out for himself.
When the news is this bad, must the Administration always be the first to spread the word?

HEADLINE: If Schools Fail, Don't Blame Disabled Students

 BODY:
To the Editor:
A Sept. 23 news article reports that the Committee for Economic Development, a leading business organization, has determined that public schools are failing to produce graduates who are ready for work in today's businesses. The committee placed part of the blame for this on "inclusion," the practice of educating students with disabilities within regular classrooms. 
 We all agree that many public schools in this country have failed to prepare students adequately for employment. A great number of those schools are in our cities.
But to blame the practice of teaching students with disabilities in regular classrooms doesn't wash. Very few schools in our cities practice inclusion; even fewer did so when the study supporting this report was undertaken. Outside the cities, inclusion is not widely available. Therefore, there is little evidence that inclusion limits the ability of schools to prepare students for work.
Indeed, now that inclusion is becoming more widely available, there is growing evidence that it contributes to learning and preparation for work, for all students. With inclusion, the aids and supports that the student with disabilities is entitled to under law are brought into the classroom to support that student. Those aids, for which substantial Federal funds are available, have a spillover effect for the other students.
Schools that poorly manage most activities poorly manage the education of students with disabilities. They either waste the Federal funds on maintaining separate special education buildings and large bureaucracies, or they "dump" students with disabilities into regular classes without the mandated supports.
Big-city schools have additional problems that spill in from the streets. And teachers' unions, whose priorities are minimizing the time teachers spend teaching, maximizing pay and resisting the demise of special education bureaucracies, have not been helpful in this regard.
The solution to the problem raised by the committee's report is not to pick on those students least able to defend themselves, but to reform the schools, scale down bureaucracies, involve parents and provide for an appropriate level of resources for all students.
 
DONALD C. BUELL 3D
Oak Brook, Ill., Sept. 26, 1994
 
The writer, health policy and economics director of Abbott Labs, heads People for Inclusive Communities.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Letter

HEADLINE: Jeans Makers Flourish on Border

BYLINE: By ALLEN R. MYERSON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: EL PASO

 BODY:
Here on the Mexican border, in the world's jeans-making capital, as many workers are cutting and sewing and stone-washing as ever.
In an industry that is among the most vulnerable to Mexican competition, a combination of corporate efficiency, low wages and the prestige of a "Made in the U.S.A." jeans label help explain why apparel employment here has reached a record 20,500 jobs. But they also explain why El Paso is the country's poorest large metropolitan area, with personal incomes only 59 percent of the nation's average. 
 It was not supposed to happen this way. Even the strongest proponents of the North American Free Trade Agreement conceded that the pact would cost 10,000 or more full-time apparel jobs a year in the United States.
But on the outskirts of this city, the VF Corporation opened a new Wrangler sewing plant and distribution center at the beginning of the year, expanding its work force here by 450 jobs.
Skilled sewing machine operators at the bright, air-conditioned building earn $8.50 an hour with full benefits. Many less-skilled workers have been replaced by conveyor belts, computers and machines.
The company considered moving more of its operations to Mexico, where it has been making jeans for discount chains, but decided against it. " 'Made in the U.S.A.' does count for something," said Joe Maxey, the division manager of Wrangler here.
El Paso's apparel revival has a second aspect, however, which is visible off a back alley near downtown. Behind a door left ajar lies the narrow, windowless cinderblock work space of 3NH Fashions. There, 14 workers hunch over sewing machines whose black enamel has been chipped and worn away from decades of use. They earn the $4.25-an- hour minimum wage and minimal benefits, plus a piece rate for those who can keep their bobbins whirring the fastest.
It could be worse, said Jose Armendariz, looking up from the pile of denim skirts he was stitching. A previous employer shut down with no notice, leaving him without two weeks' back pay.
For many local leaders, the apparel industry seems as constraining to economic growth as a new pair of jeans two sizes too small. Besides the low pay, jeans makers drain endless water from a near-desert. The local government has responded with a retraining program to turn El Paso into a center for plastic-injection molding, where workers start at $6 to $8 an hour and can earn $14 or more.
"If a person enters the apparel industry making $4.25 an hour,' said Terry J. Squier, director of the Advanced Technology Center at El Paso Community College, "I don't want to see them stay there.'
It was cheap labor and rising demand in the Southwest that began bringing jeans makers to El Paso in the 1960's. Since then, automation, a nimble fashion sense and global demand for an American label on this most American garment have raised El Paso's output to more than 100 million pairs of jeans a year.
Nationally, however, apparel employment continues to shrink, from an annual average of 1,075,700 in 1989 to an estimated 959,500 this August.
By the late 1980's, the VF Corporation, based in Wyomissing, Pa., was down to 800 workers in El Paso, but since then the local payroll has tripled. Mr. Maxey is as intent on updating the business's reputation as its factories.
"What has always bothered me is the sweatshop image,' he said. At Wrangler, sweat has given way to microchips and steel, with labor now accounting for only 20 percent of production costs.
At the plant, computer-guided slings hanging from overhead rails dump 144 pairs of jeans at a time into truck-sized washers and dryers. Tanks, pumps and pipes automatically feed in the right mix of chemicals for the specified finish.
Sewers and pressers have their place, but they seem shunted to the side of a seven-acre complex, which includes a warehouse that looks ready to run itself. Retailers' sales go by satellite from the stores to Wrangler's headquarters in Greenboro, N.C., where computers then supply a seasonally adjusted forecast of sales. Restocking signals bounce from there to the El Paso warehouse, where conveyor belts and electronic eyes do much of the sorting and carrying.
Even out of the earshot of their managers, workers appear to have few complaints. For Cesar Quinonez, $6.80 an hour to sort jeans was not a bad switch a year ago from parking cars at a restaurant across the border in Juarez for $150 a week, a generous paycheck there.
Wrangler jeans top out at $35, but those made at smaller, far more primitive shops paying lower wages often cost much more when they carry designer labels.
One of the three owners of 3NH Fashions, Pablo Puentes, said his company had worked for Calvin Klein and Guess, among others. Bundles of denim pieces crowding his office were waiting to be stitched into jackets, skirts and pants more redolent of Seventh Avenue than the Santa Fe Trail.
Outsiders driving past the convenience stores and repair garages of his factory's South Side neighborhood, bounded by railroad yards and highway ramps, might not realize they were in the middle of a garment district.
Small contractors -- known locally as talleres de hambre, or workshops of hunger -- still account for a third to a half of El Paso's apparel jobs. Statistics are few, but street talk suggests that lower apparel tariffs under the trade agreement are squeezing the smaller makers most of all because they are competing on labor costs.
At the offices of La Mujer Obrera, which means "the working woman,' the message is succinct: "Halt the exportation of jobs and unemployment!' says a banner, in Spanish, showing a pair of scissors stitched from silvery fabric.
From a garment district storefront, La Mujer Obrera teaches workers about their rights, arranges medical treatment and sues employers for back pay. "Immigrants themselves usually don't complain," said Maria Flores, the group's education coordinator. She lost her own garment job in 1989 after she tore some shoulder tendons.
Besides shorting their workers, some small contractors also try to avoid paying the Internal Revenue Service, La Mujer Obrera organizers say. When the I.R.S. shuts them down, they open up nearby under different company names, sometimes with a relative nominally in charge, the group says.
The Department of Labor is holding garment makers responsible for tens, sometimes hundreds of thousands of dollars if their contractors fail to provide minimum wages, overtime or, after a shutdown, back pay.
Mr. Puentes readily confirms that smaller shops like his have thin cash cushions. He wants to install modern cooling and a second bathroom for his workers, but added that he was also saving for a few more used sewing machines.
His workers attested to his consistency in paying them but not his concern for comfort. "Pablo won't buy air-conditioning," said Mr. Armendariz, almost loudly enough for the boss to hear.
Mr. Puentes said that small garment makers often go out of business because fashion houses are so slow to send checks. "They squeeze us on the prices, and then they don't pay,' he said.
His costs are low enough and his response to fashion dictates quick enough to keep clients from shifting work to Mexico.
For its future, El Paso would rather take a technological jump ahead of the Mexican labor force. Mr. Squier, who runs the community college training center, said his program had helped attract 29 plastic-injection molding companies to El Paso since the late 1980's.
Now, the area has more than 2,000 workers making a variety of products, like seat-belt components, toys, vacuum-cleaner cases and electrical connectors. Most of these items feed the maquiladora assembly plants owned by American companies just across the Rio Grande.
In a classroom at the training center, under a sign saying "Plastics spoken here," about 16 students were learning enough about electronics, hydraulics, pneumatics and plant organization to be turned loose in what amounts to a practice factory next door.
"It's not something where you just flip a switch and the part comes out," Mr. Squier said.
El Paso's apparel industry is unlikely to expand much further, Mr. Squier said. But injection-molding specialists can always find work, especially those with the skills to make tools and dies, including the metal forms used to mold plastics. Here they can earn more than $40,000 a year.
"Tool and die mold-making isn't a blue-collar career," he said. "It's a gold-collar career. If we brought in a plane with 79 tool and die makers, they would be working here tonight."

CORRECTION-DATE: October 1, 1994, Saturday

 CORRECTION:
An article in Business Day on Thursday about a boom in the jeans-making industry in El Paso referred imprecisely to the number of jobs in the United States that some proponents of the North American Free Trade Agreement said would be lost to Mexico each year. About 10,000 jobs would be lost over all, they said, not just in the apparel industry.

HEADLINE: New Signs of Growth Fan Inflation Fears

 BODY:
The economy showed signs of persistent vigor in Government reports on housing and employment. And the Commerce Department released a revised report on the gross domestic product, showing the economy expanding at an annual rate of 4.1 percent in the second quarter, up from an estimate of 3.8 percent.
Taken together, the reports fanned fears that overly rapid growth could revive inflation, and could lead the Federal Reserve to raise short-term interest rates again, perhaps after next week's monthly job report for September, to slow the economy down. 
 Unnerved by the combined force of the data, investors drove interest rates higher and stock prices lower in markets in the United States and in Europe.
Articles, page D1.

HEADLINE: Town 'Devastated' by Loss of Project

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: HAYMARKET, Va., Sept. 29

 BODY:
When Mayor Jack R. Kapp reached City Hall this morning, he found the flag at half-staff for the first time since Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis died. He knew many residents of his town were angered and disappointed that the Walt Disney Company had scrapped plans to build a theme park five miles away. But lowering the flag, he decided, was an inappropriate response. So he raised it.
Two hours later, he found it down again. 
 "People around here are devastated," Mr. Kapp said shortly after a post-mortem lunch today with Disney officials. "It's an economic blow to Prince William County. I feel like I've been to a funeral today."
While environmentalists, Civil War historians and citizen groups opposed to the $650 million theme park celebrated Disney's decision to seek another site, many other Virginians bemoaned the economic losses for the region and the potential losses to the state if Disney were to build the park elsewhere.
Before the company selected the site near Haymarket, a town of 500 people about 35 miles west of Washington, the company had considered more than two dozen other sites in the region. Within hours of Disney's decision, announced late Wednesday night, business and civic leaders in Maryland, including Gov. William Donald Schaefer, were expressing excitement over the possibility of competing with Virginia for the project, even though Disney officials indicated today that the site would remain in Virginia.
A 3,000-acre theme park, Disney's America, was to be the centerpiece of an extensive project that would have included a hotel, schools, golf courses, office buildings, shops, restaurants and 2,281 residential units. Historians and environmentalists fought the project, saying it would desecrate nearby Civil War battle sites like Bull Run and increase congestion and air pollution.
In a statement issued after Disney's decision to withdraw, Gov. George Allen of Virginia said he remained committed to a Disney theme park in the state, adding, "I'm pleased the Walt Disney Company shares that commitment."
But now, the shape and size -- let alone, the site -- of any subsequent Disney park are uncertain.
Robert T. Skunda, the Virginia Secretary of Commerce and Trade, said the $163 million incentive package to provide new roads to serve the area around the theme park was "site specific" to the development near Haymarket.
Speaking by telephone from Richmond, Mr. Skunda said that it was now his understanding that Disney planned to build only a theme park on a new site. Any new incentives, he added, "are now subject to negotiation and debate."
Christopher Miller of the Piedmont Environmental Council, which led the fight against the Disney project, said his group had identified 30 possible sites for a theme park in Virginia, Maryland and Washington itself that he said were more suitable than the Haymarket area.
By some estimates, Prince William County expected to gain as many as 19,000 jobs. Kathleen Seefeldt, a member of the county Board of Supervisors and a strong supporter of the Disney project, said the county had expected $11 million to $12 million in new revenue the first year of operation, 1998, and more each year after that.
With projections that the development would pump $47 million a year into the state's economy by 2007, Governor Allen became a strong supporter of the Disney project and last year pushed the incentive package through the State General Assembly as an enticement to bring Disney to Virginia. Just last week, Disney vaulted two obstacles, when the Prince William County planning commission approved zoning changes to accommodate the project, and a regional transportation planning board authorized $130 million for road projects to improve access to the site.
With its bedroom communities to Washington, Prince William County has been growing rapidly for years, reaching a current population of about 240,000, up from 150,000 in 1980. Some county officials insisted that other developers would fill the vacuum created by Disney.
But others worried that the staunch opposition mounted against the Disney project here would deter prospective investors.
"I think this will cause people to look at us a little more carefully," said James H. Mullen, the Prince William County Executive. "With the opposition Disney faced able to claim success, thwarting the efforts of a major company like Disney, other developers will be skittish about moving ahead with their plans and proposals for the same area."
Ms. Seefeldt, the leading supporter of the project on the county Board of Supervisors, predicted that "ultimately the right project or projects would create less opposition." But that was a hard sell to Mr. Kapp and others in Haymarket today.
Sitting in the quiet office of "Welcome Disney," an 8,000-member citizens' group organized to blunt the opposition, Ron Hancock, the group's treasurer, pondered a question he never imagined before Wednesday night. "What does this mean to the town?" he asked. "Whew. A lot of lost revenues and new business. People here are so disappointed, considering we were so close to having Disney's America a reality. Then, for this to happen."
Mr. Kapp, who won a second-consecutive two-year term this year after his appointment to office in 1990, said he was especially disappointed because the town will not get a water line Disney promised to provide. Most people in town use well water, he said, and many of the wells are old and rusting. "A number of people are drinking contaminated water," he said. "Now, I don't know how we can help them."
Disney had mounted a huge public relations campaign to overcome the opposition, but to no avail.
In the end, said James M. McPherson, the Princeton University historian who was a leader of efforts to block the project, "Disney recognized what it was costing them in terms of image, public relations and the potential for a long, drawn-out controversy and lawsuits from environmental groups."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Residents of Haymarket, Va., disappointed over the Walt Disney Company's decision to scrap plans for a theme park, lowered the flag at City Hall to half-staff to mourn the project's loss. Mayor Jack R. Kapp raised the flag for the second time yesterday. (Karin Anderson for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Giuliani Seeks Funds for New Buyout

BYLINE: By SAM ROBERTS 

 BODY:
Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani indicated yesterday that he was looking for money to pay for a broad new severance program to cut the city work force, possibly by turning again to the Municipal Assistance Corporation.
Mr. Giuliani said the idea emerged in a meeting earlier this week with Felix G. Rohatyn, the financier and former head of the assistance corporation. During the meeting, Mr. Rohatyn also pushed a much broader, multiyear scheme for the city to further pare its work force, shed some services and reduce taxes to stimulate the economy -- and in return to get an infusion of borrowed cash to help the city recuperate from the cuts. 
 "Essentially it's a way to finance," Mr. Giuliani said yesterday, "with guarantees that one, there would be a reduction in government; two, that there'd be a reduction in taxes so that you could give the business community a sense that the relocation that is going on will lead to a much better picture."
The Mayor's budget director, Abraham M. Lackman, is planning to meet today with officials of the Municipal Assistance Corporation, the state agency created during the 1970's fiscal crisis to borrow on the city's behalf.
The Mayor's comments came as his administration continued to look for ways to close a budget gap of up to $1 billion. Mr. Giuliani suggested that in addition to more cuts in city agencies, he was considering ways of "enhancing revenues," though he did not provide details.
The administration also is negotiating with municipal unions to cut the cost of health benefits by $200 million, a proposal included in the Mayor's original budget that the unions have yet to agree to.
"If we can get that, that would take some of the pressure off," the Mayor said.
Mr. Giuliani cautioned that Mr. Rohatyn's ideas were just being explored, and he indicated that most of what he discussed with Mr. Rohatyn involved drawing up a plan for the new budget in January. But the Mayor also said that some of Mr. Rohatyn's ideas might be useful in finding a way to raise money for a new severance package. The administration has already used $200 million in surplus funds controlled by M.A.C. for a round of severance offers that enticed more than 6,000 employees off the payroll.
The broad outlines of Mr. Rohatyn's proposal were broached two years ago during the Dinkins administration, but were abandoned when credit-rating agencies balked at seeing the city or the M.A.C. get $700 million or so deeper in debt with no guarantee that chronic budget deficits would be cured.
But in an interview, Mr. Rohatyn suggested that the proposal may be more palatable now. "They may have a more credible city government to carry it out," he said of the Giuliani administration.
"If a multiyear plan is considered and if it is rational and logical and has elements of certainty built in and guarantees structural balance, then the financing is something that can be worked out," Mr. Rohatyn said.
In an interview, Mr. Rohatyn repeatedly deferred to Eugene J. Keilin, who succeeded him as chairman of the Municipal Assistance Corporation.
"It's going to get a very serious hearing," Mr. Keilin said, should Mr. Rohatyn's proposal get to the M.A.C. board. "The Mayor, by the actions he's taken in the first nine months, has proven he's dedicated to cutting the budget."
But Richard P. Larkin, the managing director of Standard & Poor's, the credit-rating agency, expressed skepticism.
"My initial reaction to this kind of scheme is still decidedly negative," Mr. Larkin said. "I'm getting concerned that every time something major is suggested for city government restructuring, it always involves someone else's money or borrowing."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Big Push on Trade
 Democratic senators plan to vote on a law carrying out the new world trade pact in a session after Thanksgiving. Leaders of both parties said that they were sure the measure would be approved, but warned that opportunities for delay were so great that no one could be certain an impasse had been averted. [Page A1.]
As it shopped for Congressional support for the trade pact, the White House agreed to do favors for a range of industries. [D4.]
With prospects for a trade deal with Japan uncertain before today's deadline, top U.S. officials have argued that imposing sanctions on Tokyo need not destabilize financial markets. [D4.]
 

Companies
 The head of Sony Pictures Entertainment resigned after five years marked by film flops and internal turmoil, leaving questions about Sony's stake in the U.S. entertainment industry. [D1.]
 Chrysler outlined a go-it-alone strategy for international growth that it says will assure profits. [D1.]
 The Fox television network replaced its chief programmer with an executive recruited from CBS. [D1.]
 Viacom and Blockbuster Entertainment merged, though Blockbuster shareholders showed a high level of opposition. [D3.]
 USAir Group said it would defer quarterly dividend payments on its preferred stock. [D3.]
 Allstate Insurance raised its loss estimate from the Los Angeles earthquake by $350 million, or 37 percent, to $1.3 billion. [D3.]
 A top official of Towers Financial, the New York bill-collecting company, pleaded guilty to Federal fraud charges. [D3.]
 Bantam Doubleday Dell Publishing Group hired the former head of HarperCollins to run a new adult publishing division. [D17.]
 After saying it would abandon plans for a theme park near a Civil War battle field in Virginia, Walt Disney pledged to search for another site in the state. [A12.]
 
International
 President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia ended his American trip in Seattle, speaking to business leaders in a region where trade with the United States has moved well beyond talk. [D1.]
 The economic recovery will not significantly cut unemployment in industrial nations unless they become more open to market forces, the International Monetary Fund's chief said. [D15.]
 
The Economy
 Fresh signs of persistent economic vigor emerged as the Government published upbeat reports on housing and employment, while revising upward its estimate of total output in the spring. [D1.]
 
Markets
New data that the American economy was more robust than had been thought chilled the already depressed European stock and bond markets, while sending American markets lower. The Dow fell 23.55 points, to 3,854.63. [D1.]
 Prices of Government securities were pulled down by economic data that hinted at future inflation pressure. [D5.]
 Gasoline and other energy futures surged as the Energy Department warned of possible gas shortages in the Northeast. [D13.] The dollar mostly slid against other leading currencies. [D13.]
 
Today's Columns
 Hazardous-waste cleanups have proved less profitable than first thought, but water treatment offers environmental stocks a possible boon. Market Place. [D6.]
 Creative agencies at home in the global village are writing their own tickets. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D17.]

HEADLINE: Harry and Louise And a Guy Named Ben

BYLINE: By ROBIN TONER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MALIBU, Calif., Sept. 23

 BODY:
To some liberals, Harry and Louise symbolize everything that went wrong with the great health care struggle of 1994: a powerful advertising campaign, financed by the insurance industry, that played on people's fears and helped derail the process.
That makes the Malibu office of the agency that created Harry and Louise all the more incongruous. In the oceanfront aerie of Goddard-Claussen/First Tuesday, a campaign portrait of Robert F. Kennedy dominates the hallway, and the walls reflect past clients of a decidedly liberal bent, including the Rev. Jesse Jackson, former Senator Gary Hart and President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia. 
 "That doesn't mean I agree with every idea that every Democrat ever proposed," said Ben Goddard, just a trace defensively. "I think the President made a miscalculation on what the American people wanted and tried to do something that was too much too soon."
Mr. Goddard's shop is still in the thick of the health care battle, working to defeat Proposition 186, which would establish a Canadian-style system of taxpayer-financed health insurance in California. Two years ago Goddard-Claussen handled the opposition to another health-related California ballot initiative, which, among other things, would have required employers to contribute to the cost of their workers' insurance (the so-called employer mandate, which caused President Clinton so much trouble).
Bill Zimmerman, a consultant working to pass Proposition 186, said of Mr. Goddard, "Somewhere along the way, he lost his ideological bearings."
The 51-year-old Mr. Goddard, whose official biography notes that he began his communications career as a disk jockey and still considers himself a liberal, offers no apologies. The $15 million Harry and Louise campaign, created for the Health Insurance Association of America, "made a legitimate contribution to this debate," he said. "We dodged a very dangerous, overwhelming proposal that obviously most Americans did not support."
Mr. Goddard said Harry and Louise had their roots in a comment made by Bill Gradison, president of the insurance group, back in 1993: that the health care debate would be decided by couples sitting around their kitchen tables, figuring out how the various proposals would affect them.
That approach was one of several tested. Others included one using celebrities, and more traditional advertisements outlining problems in the health care system. But from the beginning, the couple at the kitchen table "resonated very powerfully with people in focus groups around the country," Mr. Goddard said.
It was not an accident that Louise was so dominant. Mr. Goddard said the intended audience for the commercials was people who not only voted but also closely followed issues and were likely to communicate with Congress.
Within that category, he added, educated women over 30 appeared to be the most vulnerable part of the coalition for the Clinton plan. They were women "who both had the desire to fix the system but also the intelligence to understand that it had to be fixed right or we could end up with unintended consequences that made things worse," he said. "Women like Louise."
Goddard-Claussen has offices in Malibu; Sacramento, Calif.; Boise, Idaho; Washington, and St. Petersburg, Russia. It describes itself as a full-service public relations, public affairs and campaign management firm and specializes in propositions and referendums; it produced television commercials on behalf of President Yeltsin's referendum on Russia's economic and political future in 1993.
These days Mr. Goddard is deep into the California struggle. After the national debate, he says, he finds that people have much more information, and much firmer beliefs, than one would otherwise expect. "It's unusual that you sit in focus groups and hear people use semi-technical terms like mandatory alliances, premium caps and pre-existing conditions," he said.
He worries that the debate in Washington has left another mark on the California electorate. About one in five, he says, were more willing to vote for Proposition 186 if Congress ended up in a deadlock this year -- to "send a message." In other words, he is coping with the fruits of his success.

HEADLINE: Whitman Says Tax Cuts May Require Four Years

BYLINE: By IVER PETERSON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TRENTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
Gov. Christine Todd Whitman, who has gained national prominence for her firm tax-cutting stand, today took a big step away from her often-repeated promise to cut state income taxes by nearly a third over her first three years in office, saying the cuts may take four years to complete.
The statement, made first in a radio call-in show and later repeated to reporters, quickly drew hoots of derision from New Jersey's Democrats. A good deal of Mrs. Whitman's appeal in the governor's race last year came from her dramatic pledge to roll back the state's income taxes within a strict timetable. Mrs. Whitman did not back off the goal of a 30 percent cut in income tax, only on its timing. 
 Although New Jersey's economy is recovering and state tax revenues are strong, a $2.3 billion shortfall was predicted last week by the state Treasury Department for the budget that begins next July. Mrs. Whitman has directed each of the state's departments to anticipate deep cuts in the 1995-96 budget.
The Governor's current budget, with an income tax cut of 10 percent scheduled to go into effect in January, was balanced by laying off several hundred state workers, by freezing state aid to most municipalities and public schools, by postponing the funding of some retirement benefits and by relying on $1.8 billion in one-shot nonrecurring savings.
The toughest budget cutting is yet to come. So far, Mrs. Whitman has reduced income taxes for 1994 by 5 percent, plus the 10 percent scheduled for January. The remaining 15 percent in cuts will have to come from an already severely shrunken revenue pool.
Unless the Governor and her staff neutralize the predictable partisan reaction, the admission that the centerpiece of the Whitman administration is coming unstuck could undermine Mrs. Whitman's image of invincibility and weaken her hand with New Jersey's public employee unions, which will bear much of the burden of the cuts.
"The depths of the state's problems must be sinking in, so the administration responds with a stall," said the Senate's minority leader, John A. Lynch, Democrat of New Brunswick. "This year, they borrowed against the future to balance the books and that's no plan. What's becoming more and more obvious is that the administration still doesn't have a clue about a fiscal plan."
Mrs. Whitman's tax pledge was widely dismissed as not doable by the Democrats when she first made it during her campaign last fall, but their attacks were deflected by her unshakeable commitment to the plan.
Appearing on the radio station 101.5 FM in Trenton, the Governor was asked, in light of the looming revenue shortfall for the next fiscal year, whether her vow to cut income taxes for most taxpayers by 30 percent over three years was still on track.
"Well, when I made the announcement, I said 30 percent in three years is what we outlined," Mrs. Whitman said, adding that half the goal, 15 percent, has already been enacted.
"The real question that comes," she continued, "and in all honesty we've got to look at it, will we make it in the three years or will it take four to reach the 30 percent?"
Afterward, her staff insisted that Mrs. Whitman has always left the door open to stretching out the cut, which would reduce the state's income tax by a total of $1.4 billion.
"From the moment she became Governor, she's said it might take her full first term," her spokesman, Carl Golden, told reporters this afternoon.
But the Democrats would give no quarter, and they promptly rolled out copies of the Governor's unambiguous pledge to make the cuts in three years.
The timing of today's admission that the Republican Governor's bold tax initiative may take a year longer than promised comes on top of the release yesterday of a very similar tax cut package from George L. Pataki, the Republican candidate for governor of New York.
Throughout his campaign, Mr. Pataki has cited Mrs. Whitman's unshakeable promise to cut taxes to quell the skeptics of his own tax cut promise in New York. Governor Whitman, who was elected last year on the strength of her tax cut promise, has made several campaign trips to New York in support of Mr. Pataki.
Mr. Pataki's plan calls for reducing income taxes by 25 percent over four years.
Mrs. Whitman, after all but conceding that she would need more time to make her cuts, said that the nearly $650 million scheduled to be cut this year and next has already produced an upturn in New Jersey's economy.
"I am committed to the 30 percent precisely because of the impact it has had on New Jersey thus far," she told the radio audience. "We are creating jobs at a record number. We are 50 percent better off than we were at the same period last year."
Joseph V. Doria Jr., the Assembly minority leader, said in a statement, "Governor Whitman's credibility gap is widening, and this won't help her with the other Republican politicians who she has been frequently traveling out of state to campaign for."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Gov. Christine Todd Whitman was making adjustments on plans to reduce state taxes yesterday. While adhering to her promise of a 30 percent cut, she said it might take four years, not three, to accomplish. She arrived for a luncheon appearance in Toms River, N.J. (Laura Pedrick for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: BIG PUSH ON TRADE: REASSURING THE MARKETS;
Tokyo Sanctions Likely; Officials Chat Up Dollar

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
With prospects of a trade agreement with Japan highly uncertain before Friday's midnight deadline, top American officials have been trying to persuade currency traders that if Washington imposes limited sanctions on Tokyo, it need not lead to a plunge in the dollar nor to instability in financial markets.
Such sanctions seemed increasingly likely in the wake of a meeting at the White House today between President Clinton and his senior trade and economic advisers. The President made clear that he was ready for a "get tough" policy, if the Japanese did not agree to American demands to open key sectors of their markets before the deadline of midnight Friday, aides said. 
 But fearful that any sanctions would lead traders to sell dollars and buy yen -- as they did when American-Japanese trade talks collapsed in February -- the Administration took the unusual step of sending several top economic officials to Wall Street to speak with big currency traders and hedge fund managers, who often set market trends.
Traders said several senior Treasury officials and representatives of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York had sought in the last few weeks to assure them that if the Administration did impose sanctions on Japan for not opening certain markets, those sanctions would be limited, focused measures. Any sanctions that are imposed would not threaten the overall economic relationship between the two countries, and therefore would not justify sudden, major currency movements, the traders say they were told.
The traders, who asked not to be identified, said they welcomed the contacts because in February they did not have a clear sense of where the Administration was heading. Likewise, they say, the Administration did not have a clear sense of where the big money managers and hedge funds that dominate the global financial markets were heading.
As a result, both the bond markets and the dollar plunged steeply after the trade talks broke down.
"Last February the Clinton people did not understand that they were going to get hit in the head if the Japan talks collapsed," one leading currency trader said, "and we didn't know that they didn't know how we were going to react. Now they have opened lines of communication."
The move is interesting because it underscores the degree to which the balance of power between markets and governments has increasingly tilted in favor of the markets.
In February, the Clinton Administration fell into the trap of being smarter than the market -- that is, the Administration knew what it was doing and was certain that any rational economic actor would understand what it was doing and therefore would not try to sell dollars in the belief that the Administration wanted a weaker currency to deal with Japan's $60 billion trade surplus.
But driven by their own logic, intuitions and investment positions, traders sold dollars against the yen, regardless of whether it seemed economically rational to Washington.
This current round of talks still seems headed for an ambiguous ending -- with some limited agreements in areas, like opening the Japanese insurance market, along with some limited sanctions because Japan did not open markets in autos or glass. Japan's Foreign Minister, Yohei Kono, and Trade Minister, Ryutaro Hashimoto, were scheduled to arrive in Washington on Friday for one last attempt to reach agreement.
Administration officials said they believed that they could get away with imposing targeted sanctions on Japan, without disrupting global financial markets and putting the dollar on the skids, provided they made clear that the overall economic and political relationship with Tokyo remained sound.
Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen seemed to be trying to prepare the financial markets for such a move during his news conference on Wednesday. "I think there's a redoubling of efforts on both sides in trying to work out some of the differences insofar as our trade concerns with Japan," he said. "If sanctions are imposed, I would expect them to be with great specificity and go to some of the more egregious areas. I would not expect some overall, broad-based sanctions."
Just in case the markets didn't get it, Mr. Bentsen added: "I want to emphasize the point -- I don't see broad-based sanctions."
Any sanctions would probably involve slapping punitive tariffs on certain Japanese exports to the United States under the Title VII and Super 301 provisions of the Trade Act. Mr. Bentsen's efforts to persuade the markets that such limited sanctions on Japan would not upset the overall economic relationship enough to justify a big currency swing seem to have gotten through.
"My view is that their analysis is right," said Richard Witten, top currency trader for Goldman, Sachs & Company. "The dollar-yen relationship has not moved more than one percentage point for sometime now, and that is almost unprecedented. The big betting all over the world is that the two big guys will find a way to deal with this situation in a face-saving way, not a world-ending way. The details of how they do it almost don't matter. I think most people are basically neutral on the dollar-yen rate. No one seems to have bet big money on a big move."
Anything short of a collapse of the trade talks should, if anything, give the dollar a slight lift, other traders say. Their reasoning is that Japanese market participants and financial institutions will be so relieved that a crisis has been averted that they will be ready to take off some of their hedges and start buying dollars and American debt securities again.

HEADLINE: U.S. and North Korea Announce Pause in Talks, but No Progress

BYLINE: By ALAN RIDING, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: GENEVA, Sept. 29

 BODY:
Announcing a brief suspension of its high-level talks with North Korea after a week of hard bargaining, the United States said tonight that the two sides had made no progress toward carrying out last month's agreement to settle their nuclear dispute.
But the chief American delegate, Robert L. Gallucci, refused to describe the negotiations as deadlocked. He said he would fly to Washington on Friday for consultations and return to Geneva next Wednesday to resume his meetings with North Korea's Deputy Foreign Minister, Kang Sok Ju. 
 "I cannot report that we have made substantive progress in terms of resolving any of the important and complicated issues that divide us," he said of the latest round of talks. "But I think there is interest in both sides in pursuing the discussions."
The somber mood of this week's talks nonetheless contrasted with the optimism raised by last month's framework agreement under which North Korea said it would freeze its nuclear program and implicitly renounced the possibility of building nuclear weapons.
In exchange, the United States said it would help provide North Korea with new light water nuclear reactors to replace existing and planned graphite reactors that yield high levels of weapons-grade plutonium. Washington also opened the door to diplomatic relations and new economic and trade links.
Since then, however, Pyongyang has raised new demands. Specifically, it has asked for $2 billion in compensation and has rejected an American plan under which South Korea would play a central role in financing and constructing the new light water reactors.
North Korea has also rejected renewed demands from the International Atomic Energy Agency that it open all its military installations to international inspection. The United States has said it is willing to be flexible about the timing of these so-called "special inspections," but that they would have to form part of any final settlement.
United States officials said the demand for compensation, the rejection of a South Korean role and the refusal to accept the inspections were preventing further moves to carry out last month's accord.

HEADLINE: BIG PUSH ON TRADE: LINING UP THE VOTES;
Senators Plan Return for Vote On Trade Pact, After Elections

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
Democratic senators made plans today to vote on a global trade agreement in a lame-duck session the week after Thanksgiving.
Leaders from both parties said they were sure that when the measure was voted on, it would be approved. But they cautioned that the rules of the Senate were arcane and the opportunities for delay so prevalent that no one could be certain an impasse had been averted. 
 Senator George J. Mitchell of Maine, the Democratic leader, and Senator Ernest F. Hollings, Democrat of South Carolina, who has promised to exercise his authority to block the trade pact for as long as he can, agreed today that a vote would be taken the week beginning Nov. 27, other senators said.
President Clinton has promised leaders of other countries that there would be a vote before the end of the year. But the agreement struck today means that vote will not come until after the Nov. 8 election unless Mr. Hollings relents.
The deal was not announced because, as a matter of courtesy, Mr. Mitchell does not make scheduling decisions public before they are cleared with Republican leaders. Most Republicans, however, favor the trade measure.
Congress is supposed to adjourn for the year next week or the week after. But Senator Hollings, the Democratic chairman of the commerce committee and a staunch opponent of the new General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade, announced Wednesday that he would take advantage of rules that allow his committee to spend 45 days on the pact, not counting weekends and holidays, before legislation putting it into effect can be brought to a vote.
With health legislation dead, the trade bill is President Clinton's highest legislative priority for the rest of the year.
The bill would permit the United States to participate in an accord that the country's trade negotiators concluded last December with more than 100 other nations. The pact, the culmination of years of negotiations, would reduce worldwide tariffs by an average of 40 percent, lower other barriers to trade like quotas on certain products, and provide patent protection for the American software, pharmaceutical and other industries. Most other countries are waiting for the United States to act before they formally approve the pact.
The Administration says the accord would produce half a million jobs in the United States in the next few years. It is supported by politicians and economists in the broad middle of the ideological spectrum. But it is opposed by some on the left like Ralph Nader and some on the right like Patrick J. Buchanan.
Today, in a ceremony at the Treasury Department where he signed a bill to allow interstate banking, the President repeated his call for Congress to return after the Nov. 8 Congressional elections to vote on the trade legislation if it could not be handled before the scheduled adjournment.
"We need to be able to continue to be the leader in opening up the global economy to efficiency and to competition and to expansion," he said.
The House intends to vote on the measure next week. The Speaker of the House, Thomas S. Foley, said today that he had no doubt that it would be approved.
Senator Mitchell said Wednesday that he was prepared to keep the Senate in pro forma sessions to eat up the 45 days and then call all senators back to Washington for a debate and a vote. The period expires Nov. 28, the Monday after Thanksgiving.
At a meeting this afternoon, Senator Hollings told Senator Mitchell that he still intended to block the bill for 45 days but that he would not insist that the Senate remain in session during that period. The two Senators agreed that a vote would be taken after a two-day debate the week after Thanksgiving. Those voting will be the senators now sitting, not the ones elected in November.
Under "fast track" authority granted the President on the GATT legislation, once it is brought up for a vote, it must be approved or rejected after a brief debate, with no amendments allowed.
There has not been a lame-duck session of Congress since 1982. Such sessions have often produced considerable mischief. It was unclear tonight whether this session could be restricted to the trade matter. But the opportunities for dealing with other issues could be limited, since the House will not return.
Earlier today, the Senate Finance Committee, the main committee with jurisdiction over trade policy, approved the legislation, 19 to 0.
"This has to be done this year," said Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, the New York Democrat who is chairman of the committee. "Too much is at stake. We can't take the chance of waiting."
The top Republican on the Finance Committee, Bob Packwood of Oregon, predicted in an interview today that Senator Hollings would relent and permit a vote next week. "He won't stick his finger in the eyes of the President and Mitchell," Mr. Packwood said.
But in an interview tonight on the "MacNeil/Lehrer NewsHour" on PBS, Mr. Hollings said, "I still am determined to use those 45 days."
In a narrow sense, Senator Hollings is concerned with protecting the textile industry in South Carolina from inexpensive imports. But the Senator, who was Governor of South Carolina from 1959 to 1963 and has been in the Senate ever since, has been opposed to free trade for as long as he has been in politics. He maintains that accords like the GATT agreement lead to the decline of manufacturing in the United States and to the loss of good jobs to overseas competitors.
Senator Packwood said that the White House had long been forewarned of Senator Hollings's opposition and that it could have easily constructed the legislation so that the commerce committee would not have had jurisdiction.
Other supporters of the trade measure from both parties agreed with that assessment. Several also said that they could not understand why the Administration waited until the last weeks of the session to send the bill to Congress, a delay that opened the way for Senator Hollings's obstruction.
One Senator said that a White House staff member told him as recently as last week that Mr. Hollings was so close to President Clinton that he could be persuaded to relent in his opposition to the trade bill.
A senior Senate staff assistant who has been working on the issue all year put it this way: "The breath-taking failure of the Clinton White House is its inability to recognize who means 'no' when they say 'no.' When Hollings says 'no,' he's a dead 'no.' "

HEADLINE: Gas Prices Up 5 Cents, With More Increases Seen

BYLINE: By JOHN T. McQUISTON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: JERICHO, L.I., Sept. 29

 BODY:
Gasoline prices have jumped an average of 5 cents a gallon at most filling stations on Long Island and throughout the New York-New Jersey region this week. They may increase again, perhaps by as much as 25 cents a gallon, in three months when gasoline that has been reformulated to meet new Federal air-quality standards will go on sale, retailers and consumer advocates said today.
Retailers in the region are required to switch by Saturday to oxygenated gasoline, which by Federal law must be sold from Oct. 1 through April 30 to help reduce fuel emissions during the cold winter months. 
 Motorists will be asked to pay still more on Jan. 1, when Federal regulations take matters a step further by requiring that all gasoline sold in the nation's heavily populated metropolitan areas be reformulated to help protect the earth's damaged ozone layer.
While some drivers said today that they did not mind the price increases if the changes would improve air quality, some New York State officials and representatives of gasoline retailers said they were concerned about rising prices.
"Such high, rapid increases could be a jolt to an already troubled economy," said Richard M. Kessel, executive director of the State Consumer Protection Board, who called on the Federal Government to closely monitor gasoline pricing over the next several months "to protect consumers against unfair practices by the oil industry."
"Prices could go through the ceiling this winter," Mr. Kessel said. "We could see regular gas going for a dollar-and-a-half a gallon, yet the supplies of oil and gasoline are plentiful."
Gasoline retailers in New Jersey and Connecticut are also worried about the impact of the price increases, which could be as much as 8 cents a gallon by Saturday, officials said.
The price for regular gasoline posted today at the Turnpike Texaco Gas Station here on the Jericho Turnpike was $1.25, reflecting a 5-cent-a-gallon increase for the oxygenated fuel.
The station served as the setting for a news conference called by Mr. Kessel, Assemblyman Thomas Di Napoli, chairman of the New York State Assembly Consumer Affairs and Protection Committee, and Mary Ann Ragona, executive director of the Long Island Gasoline Retailers Association.
Mr. Kessel said that oxygenated fuel had been on the market for the last two winters and that during each season prices rose by as much as 12 cents a gallon, despite industry projections that the increases would be only 3 to 5 cents a gallon.
"While we laud the goals of the 1990 Clean Air Act, we also must look out for the interests of consumers," Assemblyman DiNapoli said.
Motorists who stopped at the Texaco station said they were unaware that any changes had occurred. They did express concern, though, about whether the oil companies might be using the situation to make additional profits.
"I have no way of knowing whether I'm paying a fair price," said Ellen Lazarus, who with her 4-year-old daughter, Jillian, stopped to fill the tank of her Jeep Cherokee. "I guess paying more is worth it for clean air."
Andy Harris, the owner of the Texaco station and president of the Long Island Gasoline Retailers Association, said his 5-cent-a-gallon increase was based on the cost of his latest delivery. He said that the increase for the consumer would soon be more than 5 cents, because he will likely pay more for his next delivery.
Ms. Ragona said that while gasoline suppliers basically set the price, there was a free market, based on supply and demand, and that consumers could have an impact by "shopping around for the best deal."
There are no industry estimates of the cost of reformulating gasoline. But the American Petroleum Institute, an industry trade group based in Washington, disclosed earlier this week that an analysis of gasoline futures trading suggested an increase of 6 cents a gallon was coming in January as a result of the new Federal requirement.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Gasoline prices are rising in the New York region and may increase by as much as 25 cents a gallon in three months when gasoline that has been reformulated to meet new Federal air-quality standards goes on sale. HEADLINE: Campaign Trail;
For Cuomo, an Additional Opponent: An Eccentric Millionaire

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK, Special to The New York Times 

 BODY:
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo is about to learn that it is never wise to make enemies in politics, especially when they have deep pockets.
Abraham Hirschfeld, the eccentric real-estate developer who ran for lieutenant governor in 1986 only to be knocked off the ballot by Mr. Cuomo, is seeking revenge with what he says will be a $1 million advertising campaign against the Governor. 
 The campaign began yesterday with a radio ad on black-oriented stations in New York City featuring C. Vernon Mason, a lawyer. In the ad, Mr. Mason says Mr. Cuomo "dissed Rev. Al Sharpton and his supporters" and ordered a Crown Heights inquiry "that destroyed Mayor Dinkins." Mr. Mason implores: "Skip over Cuomo or cross over. Demand respect."
A similar print advertisement is scheduled to run in black-oriented newspapers next week. In addition, a political action committee (A Better Economy Political Action Committee, or ABE-PAC), formed by Mr. Hirschfeld, will begin a television advertising campaign on New York City stations in the next few weeks that "graphically makes the case that New York is no longer the Empire State," said Jay Severin, Mr. Hirschfeld's media consultant.
Mr. Severin, who typically works for Republicans, said there had been no coordination with the campaign of Senator George E. Pataki, Mr. Cuomo's Republican opponent.
But the Cuomo campaign was not convinced. "It's a sad attempt by the Pataki campaign to deceive intelligent New York voters," said Arda Nazerian, a Cuomo spokeswoman.
Mr. Hirschfeld said yesterday he was motivated largely by his anger at being removed from the Democratic primary ballot in 1986 by Mr. Cuomo's lawyers and at being opposed by the Governor in his efforts to buy The New York Post in 1993.
"Am I mad at Cuomo about '86?" he said. "Yes. Am I mad at Cuomo about The Post? Yes."
But he then called back to amend his statement. "I am not mad at the Governor," he said. "I am not mad at the Governor that he chased me out from The Post."
Mr. Hirschfeld, who said he has contributed $35,000 to Mr. Pataki's campaign, said he and members of his family gave $600,000 to ABE-PAC and that he expected to raise $400,000 more from other sources.
 
Focusing on Abortion
 Speaking of television ads, the Cuomo campaign began airing two new ones Wednesday night that criticize Mr. Pataki for changing his position on abortion rights.
In one of them, a woman asks, "How can you possibly trust a man like that?" The other ad states that Mr. Pataki has repeatedly been supported by anti-abortion groups and received a 100 percent rating from Pat Robertson's Christian Coalition.
Zenia Mucha, the Pataki spokeswoman, said, "George Pataki's pro-choice and Mario Cuomo knows it. But Mario Cuomo's desperate, so he's resorted to lies."
Mr. Pataki has said that he originally favored abortion rights, then changed his stand after the birth of his first child, but now supports a woman's right to choose. He has voted against Medicaid funding for abortions, but he says he could support it if abortions for poor women were accompanied by mandatory counseling about family planning. He also favors parental notification before a girl under 16 can have an abortion.
While the New York State Right-to-Life Committee endorsed Mr. Pataki in several of his legislative races, it refused to do so this year. The Christian Coalition gave him a 100 percent rating last year and an 83 percent rating this year.
 
Trying Nice Now
 A week after Herbert London, the Republican candidate for comptroller, lashed out not only against his Democratic challenger but the head of his own ticket, here he is as the nice Herb.
The London campaign has released two 30-second commercials of Mr. London, to the strains of inspirational music, hugging, grinning, shaking hands and generally being pleasant with Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani; Roy G. Innis, director of the Congress for Racial Equality; the bandleader Lionel Hampton, and other grass-roots supporters. The slogan is equally upbeat:
"Herb London for comptroller. No wonder the hard-working taxpayers of New York are smiling again."
 
On Debates, No Debate
 With none of the bickering and posturing that usually accompanies the scheduling of debates, the candidates for state attorney general have agreed on three forums. The first will take place in New York City, tentatively on Oct. 9. The others are scheduled on Long Island and Buffalo (the respecitive political bases for the Democrat, Karen S. Burstein, and the Republican, Dennis C. Vacco). Those dates have not been set.

HEADLINE: Wall Street Fears Spread To Bourses

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
The old adage that when America sneezes, the world catches a cold applied with special force in financial markets yesterday. New data that the American economy was more vigorous than thought chilled the already depressed European stock and bond markets. Like their counterparts in the United States, investors feared a new wave of global inflation, stoked by the powerful American economy.
Of course, there were local factors at work, too, in the overseas market declines. But it was the United States, with word that housing sales were unexpectedly robust and that second-quarter growth was even stronger than first estimated, that added the extra push downward in Britain, France, Italy, and, most importantly, Germany, Europe's largest economy. 
 Investors and traders feared that the American Federal Reserve would seize on the new economic data as evidence of resurgent inflation, raise United States interest rates and further sour global bond markets. United States and European stock traders, although there were exceptions, followed the lead of their bond markets.
Bond and stock prices fell further in Europe than they did in the United States. Share prices have been dropping slowly all month on many European bourses. American prices dropped sharply more recently.
"The reality of higher rates is just seeping into Europe," said Michael Metz, chief investment strategist at Oppenheimer & Company. Todd Clark, senior director of equity trading at Mabon Securities, seeking to explain the relatively gentle drop for United States share prices yesterday, said. "We already had a nasty hiccup last week,"
The Dow Jones industrial average fell 23.55 points, to 3,854.63, while the broader Standard & Poor's 500 fell 2.58 points, to 462.23. The smaller-company Nasdaq composite index fell 0.67 point, to 759.34.
In percentage terms, the Dow fell 0.61 percent and the S.& P. 500 fell 0.56 percent. By contrast, Europe's markets plummeted more than twice as far. Britain's Financial Times-Stock Exchange 100 Index fell 1.51 percent; France's CAC-40 Index dropped 1.51 percent, and Italy's Mibtel Index fell 1 percent.
Germany's DAX index fell 2.8 percent, much of it in after-hours trading when the American housing and gross domestic product data were released. But German stocks' decline was already well under way before then.
The Bundesbank announced yesterday that it would leave interest rates unchanged for at least two more weeks, leading to speculation that the central bank's next move would be to raise rates after a steady series of rate cuts. At the same time, Chancellor Helmut Kohl's lead in German opinion polls was dropping, calling into question the ruling party's ability to win the Oct. 16 national elections.
The Japanese financial markets, which are essentially quiet when Western markets are open, are likely to react more to the fate of United States-Japan trade talks that are scheduled to conclude today, analysts said.
There seemed to be optimism in Japan, based on statements by Lloyd Bentsen, Secretary of the Treasury, that broad United States trade sanctions against Japan could be avoided. But United States inflation worries could eventually hurt Japan, too.
Stocks were higher early today in Tokyo. At the midday recess in trading, the Nikkei index of 225 issues was up 45.21 points, or two-tenths of 1 percent, at 19,660.33. What first moved the American markets, at 8:30 A.M., Eastern time, was the gross-domestic product growth figure of 4.1 percent in the second quarter, up from 3.8 percent, in the August revision. About an hour and a half later, the Commerce Department reported that August sales of single-family homes rose 9.7 percent, instead of the 1.7 percent decline expected by Wall Street.
"But if I were a trader, I wouldn't buy or sell on the basis of this housing survey," said James R. Solloway, director of research at Argus Research, an investment advisory firm. He cited the relatively few companies polled for the survey, which leads to high volatility, despite the Government's seasonal adjustment of the data.
Declining stocks outnumbered advancing stocks by 1,262 to 874. Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was above average, with 305.4 million shares changing hands, about 6 percent above this year's average of 287 million shares.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index fell 1.22 points, to 255.15, but the American Stock Exchange market value index rose 0.27 point, to 456.47.
Of the 30 component stocks that make up the Dow industrials, 26 fell, led by the Aluminum Company of America, which fell 1 3/4, to 85 3/4. Of the 500 component stocks that make up the S.& P. 500, a total of 286 fell, led by AT&T, which fell 3/4, to 53 7/8.
