HEADLINE: Health Care Battle In California Turns On State Insurance

BYLINE: By ROBIN TONER 

 BODY:
The great health care battle of 1994 may have sputtered to a close in Washington. But if some advocates of a health care restructuring have their way, the struggle will simply shift to the states, in the tradition of many long-haul social crusades, bubbling up in places as improbable as an art-filled, affluent home high in the hills over Hollywood.
There, on a balmy night last week, an earnest group of actors, doctors and assorted other professionals gathered to discuss the pros and cons of managed care and health maintenance organizations versus a state-run, Canadian-style system of taxpayer-financed health insurance. The session had its Hollywood moments. "Say we get an acting job in New York," one woman asked. "How would we be covered?" 
 But the tone was exceptionally policy-minded and the purpose exceedingly political. Organizers say that about 50 such house parties are held each week to raise money and spread the word for Proposition 186, an initiative on the ballot this November that would make California the first state to create a Canadian-style, or "single payer," system.
Nothing would give the proposal's advocates greater pleasure than to have California do what the nation could not: to pass something far more sweeping than even a Clinton-style restructuring, to prove that despite the deepest convictions of the political establishment, the drive for a health care overhaul is alive and well far from the deadlocked Congress.
"Citizens are suffering a case of reformus interruptus," said Steve Hopcraft, a spokesman for Californians for Health Security, the coordinator for groups backing the initiative. "We spent two years telling them there really is a problem and we've got to change it, and now we're telling them we can't change it."
The effort to pass this initiative is a decidedly uphill fight. But win or lose, Proposition 186 is a case study of the rough new politics of health care as the battle moves to the states, just as the abortion struggle did in the 1980's.
In some ways, the fight in California has picked up right where Washington left off. The opponents of Proposition 186 have hired Goddard-Claussen/First Tuesday, the media consultant that created Harry and Louise for the Health Insurance Association of America, and they are using many of the same arguments that proved so effective in the national debate.
"I think we have a good chance of defeating it," said Allan Zaremberg of the California Chamber of Commerce. "The public doesn't want the government to run their health care system."
Advocates of Proposition 186 counter by declaring that here in the land of managed care and H.M.O.'s, the public is becoming tired of having its system run by profit-minded insurance companies.
In Washington, most of the major Democratic plans were a mix of market changes and government intervention, and thus came under fire from both the left and the right. Proposition 186 seeks no such ideological straddle. It would largely eliminate the role of private health insurance in California and replace it with a state-administered insurance system covering every resident.
The system would be financed by a $1-a-pack tax on cigarettes, an employer payroll tax ranging from 4.4 percent to 8.9 percent and new state income taxes equal to 2.5 percent of taxable income for individuals and an additional 2.5 percent for those with incomes over $250,000. (That is on top of California's current income tax, whose top rate is 11 percent.)
A state legislative analysis estimated that the proposal would raise $40 billion to $50 billion a year in new taxes.
Advocates emphasize that this would replace all premium payments and provide a generous, and secure, package of health benefits, including such expensive services as long-term care. They say that Californians would still have their full choice of doctors and that the doctors themselves would be freed from the second-guessing of managed care plans preoccupied with the bottom line.
Supporters of a single-payer system, which would be run by an elected health commissioner, also believe that with private insurers eliminated as middlemen, vast sums could be saved on administrative overhead.
Against this earnest organizing, the opposition has unleashed an argument that is simple, well honed from the national debate and already on the airwaves. "Believe me, we know health care reform is critical," a nurse says in one television spot. "But Proposition 186 isn't the reform we want. One eighty-six will force most of us to give up our private health coverage and push us into a government-run health care bureaucracy -- controlled by an elected politician, not a doctor or a nurse."
Critics also argue that the initiative would have a devastating impact on the state's economy, placing on small business a harrowing burden that would cost thousands of jobs. They say the benefits promised are far too generous to be covered by the financing in the initiative, thus raising the possibility of ever larger tax increases or even delays or denial of some care.
They also warn that out-of-staters would come flocking to California for its health coverage. (The initiative specifies a two-year residency requirement for long-term-care benefits but would make residency requirements for other care a matter for the State Legislature to decide.) And they say that no matter what the deficiencies of the current system, turning it over to the state government -- and to an elected official, to boot -- would be preposterous.
A survey conducted Sept. 13-18 by the Field Institute, a leading California pollster, found that voters' awareness of the single-payer initiative was growing -- but so was opposition. Fifty-eight percent said they had heard something about the proposition; 20 percent said they were inclined to vote yes, 28 percent said they were inclined to vote no, and 10 percent said they were undecided. After voters were read a description of the single-payer initiative, the opposition rose to 53 percent, with 30 percent supporting and 17 percent undecided.
Findings like those alarm many advocates of health care restructuring, who fear that Proposition 186 may be setting them up for a one-two punch, making 1994 the year that major health initiatives failed both nationally and in the nation's most populous state.
"I personally wish that it hadn't come so quickly after what I think was a very negative national debate," said John Rother, legislative director for the American Association of Retired Persons, whose state affiliate is supporting Proposition 186. "I think we need time to remake the case for health care reform."
A case can also be made that the initiative is simply out of synch with the leaner, more cynical politics of the 1990's, when many polls show people more interested in putting limits on elected officials than on expanding their duties. None of the major candidates for governor or senator have endorsed Proposition 186. According to the polls, the hot initiative on the California ballot this year is Proposition 187, which would deny most government services to illegal immigrants.
Its advocates acknowledge that Proposition 186 may be ahead of its time, but just barely. Managed care and managed competition, in which cost-conscious health plans regulate care and compete vigorously for patients, are offered as a panacea in much of the country and certainly have their true believers in Congress and the White House. But they are already a reality for many in California, and they are creating a backlash among doctors, driving some of them to take a fresh look at the single-payer concept as a means of giving them more independence.
"There's no other issue that's crossed specialty lines, age lines, demographic lines, so much as this anger at what they see as intrusion and the compromising of health care by these plans," said Danielle Walters of the California Medical Association. The association is not supporting Proposition 186 but is studying the general concept of a Canadian-style system as well as other alternatives, she said.
The proposition does have support from labor, health groups like the California Nurses Association and various other organizations. Mr. Hopcraft, of Californians for Health Security, said that the supporters hoped to raise $3 million to $4 million and would schedule their television advertising for the last few weeks before the election but that they expected the opposition to outspend them by 10 to 1. Richard Wiebe, a spokesman for the Taxpayers Committee Against the Government Takeover, the opponents' coordinator, asserts that its budget is only about $8 million and that spending will probably not reach even that.
The single-payer advocates, in the tradition of guerrilla movements, hope to prove that they were badly underestimated. The struggle is hard, however. With money for paid advertising short, getting news coverage is crucial, and organizers say it is difficult to compete in California with O. J. Simpson and Heidi Fleiss.
Still, Bill Zimmerman, a consultant and strategist for Proposition 186, was sanguine in an interview last week. "We're people with a long view," he said. "If we lose, the issue isn't going away, and the need isn't going away." He added with a smile, "And strange things can happen with these initiatives."
The health care wars, in other words, rage on.

HEADLINE: BIG PUSH ON TRADE;
Yeltsin Visits Seattle, a Good Market

BYLINE: By TIMOTHY EGAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: SEATTLE, Sept. 29

 BODY:
President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia stopped off in Seattle today at the end of his American visit, playing up the one part of the country where trade between the United States and Russia has moved well beyond the talk-and-dream phase.
Washington, the state most dependent on trade and home of the Boeing Company, a leading exporter, has the largest concentration of companies doing business with Russia of any state, according to its trade officials. 
 From apples to dairy products, potato chips to microchips, the Pacific Northwest has seen a sixfold increase, to nearly $200 million annually, in the amount of trade with Russia over the last five years.
And in Russian East Asia, American business leaders are laying the foundation for a huge new source of timber and fishing products. Products from the region are seen as replacements for the dwindling natural resources in the Pacific Northwest.
But numerous problems remain, and Mr. Yeltsin, in a passionate speech before 800 West Coast business leaders here today, tried to allay many of the fears. Repeating a message he told President Clinton earlier this week, the Russian President said: 'We don't need help. I'm not here with outstretched arms." What Russia needs, he said, is investment of all sorts.
The Russian President called for a free-trade zone between his country and the United States, similar to what the North American Free Trade Agreement has created among the United States, Canada and Mexico. Asian leaders made the same appeal at their summit meeting here with President Clinton last November.
"Why is it believed that we are still painted red?" asked Mr. Yeltsin, who rose to political prominence as the Communist Party boss of Moscow.
In a pitch for investment, Mr. Yeltsin told the American business people that Russia's inflation rate had dropped to 4 percent, from 25 percent, and that business fears of Russian organized crime were overblown.
"Yes, you were afraid of political instability, but today, let me assure you, we have achieved political stability," Mr. Yeltsin said.
But for some of the bigger American companies that sent representatives to hear Mr. Yeltsin, government chaos remains a real problem, as do currency flucutations and high tariffs. The Weyerhaeuser Company, the world's largest timber concern, which is based in Tacoma, has put its fledgling operation in Russia on hold after running into numerous problems.
"We planted a million and half trees there, and hoped to do some harvesting of their timber, but it's a real tough place to do business," said Frank Mendizabal, a Weyerhaeuser spokesman. "The question is: who owns the resource? Almost every time we go back there, we're dealing with somebody new."
But another timber concern, the Global Forestry Management Group of Portland, Ore., announced plans this week to develop extensive logging and exporting operations in Russian East Asia. The consortium plans to convert an old naval base in the port city of Sovetskaya Gavan to an export dock. Using what it says are environmentally sound logging methods, the consortium plans to cut as much as 100 million board feet of timber a year from a million acres of leased land in Russia. The timber would be sent to 10 mills on the West Coast of the United States.
The Pacific Northwest and Russian East Asia have a "special link," Mr. Yeltsin said today. "Now it is time to cooperate on some real dramatic business."
Mr. Yeltsin's visit recalled the historic links between Russia and the Northwest via the Pacific Ocean, links that are starting to pay off in trade. Seattle is the mainland American city closest to Russian territory, excluding those in Alaska. It takes less time to ship goods from Russian East Asia to Seattle -- about 10 days by ship -- than it does to Moscow, which can take three weeks by rail.
As a result, some Russian companies have started to use Seattle as a convenient supply center for their own ventures in Russian East Asia.
The relationship that this part of the country and Russia first established during the cold war is another reason business projects are now bearing fruit, officials say. Seattle played host to the Goodwill Games in 1990, a sort of mini-Olympicss between the United States and the Soviet Union. Some of the Seattle organizers of the games are now running companies selling everything from groceries to pharmaceuticals in Russia.
To help minimize the investment risks, the Overseas Private Investment Corporation, a Federal agency, announced plans this week to offer up to $550 million in support of American companies with ventures in Russia. Companies in the Northwest, including a fish-processing concern, a telecommunications venture and an equipment-leasing company, are receiving a large share of those subsidies.
Boeing executives envision a market for $60 billion of airplanes in Russia and other former Soviet republics over the next 20 years. Mr. Yeltsin toured a Boeing plant today, but he did not publicly address the company's complaints about the high tariffs that have so far kept the world's largest airplane manufacturer out of Russia.
Total two-way trade between this region and Russia is still relatively small, at less than $200 million. By comparision, Washington state had $6 billion of exports to Japan last year, and $2.2 billion to China.
But the growth potential is enormous, business leaders say. Mr. Yeltsin, in his cheerleading address, not only reminded them of the potential, but of how far the two regions had come.
"As I told the governor, at least 10 strategic missiles used to be trained on Washington state," Mr. Yeltsin said, to strained laughter. "We have taken those missiles off target."
Seattle was the only city outside New York and Washington on Mr. Yeltsin's tour, before he flew back to Russia tonight.

HEADLINE: Chrysler Chief's World View: Place to Sell, Not Build, Cars

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: SIENA, Italy, Sept. 29

 BODY:
In boom years for the automobile industry, the Chrysler Corporation has used its surplus cash to build more factories and invest in everything from aircraft companies to foreign competitors. When hard times hit, it found itself closing factories, selling businesses and losing money.
Today Chrysler, the most profitable of the Big Three auto makers, outlined a cautious, go-it-alone strategy for international growth that it says will assure profit even when the domestic market turns sour. 
 While Chrysler has been rumored as a potential partner for everyone from Volvo of Sweden to Fiat of Italy, Robert J. Eaton, the company's chairman and chief executive, rejected the idea of joint ventures. He talked with American and European journalists at a retreat here before the Paris Auto Show, discussing Chrysler's corporate strategy.
Mr. Eaton also said he had no plans to buy any auto maker outright. He would add factories abroad, he said, only in countries that demand it as a condition to sell vehicles within their borders.
"We think of ourselves as a regional company with an international viewpoint," Mr. Eaton said, citing BMW as a model. "We also think we're about the right size, so we don't plan on growing very much."
With North America as a development and manufacturing base, Chrysler hopes to increase its sales overseas by 20 percent a year, to about 300,000 by the turn of the century. Its primary vehicles for sales abroad will be Jeeps and mini-vans, Mr. Eaton said, modified to meet the regulations of foreign markets, not consumer tastes.
The Ford Motor Company and the General Motors Corporation are trying to more closely link their foreign and domestic operations, to cut vehicle-development costs and to expand into new markets, particularly in Asia. Chrysler is not so ambitious. "We expect to be only a niche player" in foreign markets, Mr. Eaton said. "We don't have any volume goal or penetration goal."
A major concern for investors -- and one reason Chrysler stock has slipped so far from its peak in January of $63.50 a share -- is that unlike Ford and G.M., Chrysler has negligible sales abroad. By contrast, when the auto cycle turned down in the United States, G.M. and Ford have benefited from recovery in Europe.
But Mr. Eaton argued that Chrysler has cut costs deeply enough to remain profitable even in a downturn. Its break-even point -- the number of vehicles it must sell to offset its costs and begin making money -- is down to 1.6 million units, compared with 1.9 million units in the late 1980's, the company says. In addition, James D. Donlon, Chrysler's controller, said the company had identified a further $8 billion, from absenteeism to warranty costs, it is trying to eliminate.
Chrysler, like other auto companies, thinks that auto-sales cycles on different continents are slipping into sync as economies become interdependent. As a result, Mr. Eaton said, Chrysler would gain little from building factories abroad but would incur costs.
To some extent, the company is making a virtue of necessity. Having ignored foreign markets for years, it lacks not only foreign manufacturing structure but also expertise to exploit such markets aggressively, Mr. Eaton acknowledged.
That has not stopped the company from trying in the past. In the 1960's Chrysler bought several small European auto makers like Simca, in a bid to enter European markets. During its financial crisis in 1978, it sold them to Peugeot.
Chrysler is now making mini-vans at an Austrian factory run through a joint venture with an engineering company, Steyr-Daimler-Puch. It has contracted with the same company to begin assembling Grand Cherokees there, starting on Tuesday.
The auto maker has been negotiating with China to assemble mini-vans there, and has been pursuing nine other possible manufacturing operations in South America, India and elsewhere. The investments required would range as high as $1 billion for the Chinese plant, but the company said none of the operations was critical.
"We're perfectly willing to walk away from every single one of them," Mr. Eaton said.
Chrysler, he added, intended to bring out some new types of vehicles, though he would not describe them. He did say that Chrysler would not make a more expensive luxury car than its current LHS model, because the luxury market was already too crowded.
Mr. Eaton said Chrysler was aiming for double-digit annual productivity increases from its main blue-collar work force, but was only getting 6 percent to 7 percent now. Chrysler expects 50 percent of those workers to retire by 2000.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Government Securities Prices Fall

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Government securities were pulled down yesterday by economic data that hinted strongly at future inflation pressures, raising the specter of an interest rate rise that could come sooner than the next Federal Open Market Committee meeting in November.
Despite the day's first dose of data -- the final revision to the second- quarter gross domestic product figures -- bond prices held up well. But the steadiness was short-lived as prices for bonds immediately fell under the weight of the August new- home sales, which were much stronger than expected. Those statistics were considered by some as confirmation that the economy is not weakening, and inflation pressures are a threat down the road. 
 The 30-year bond fell 9/32 , to a price of 964/32 , for a yield of 7.84 percent, up from 7.81 percent on Wednesday. Rates were higher as well for bills of shorter maturity, amid concern by investors as to where the rates will be at least three months from now. The three- and six-month bill rates both rose 6 basis points, to 4.55 percent and 5.21 percent, respectively. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
Some economists note that the bond market has repeatedly underestimated both the strength of the economy and how high bond yields could go. Behind speculation about the timing of any other rate rise by the Fed is concern by traders about just how many other tightenings it will take before inflation is offically ruled under control.
The gross domestic product figure was revised up slightly to 4.1 percent, from the preliminary estimate of 3.8 percent. A Reuters survey of economists showed expectations of 3.9 percent growth.
Marilyn Schaja, an economist with Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette, saw the revised figure as confirming that growth during the quarter was dominated by inventory accumulation.
Most of the upward revision, Ms. Schaja felt, was caused by farm inventory accumulation that was stronger than estimated.
New single-family home sales for August rose 9.7 percent, to 703,000 units, much higher than expected and higher than the 641,000 in July. The rise in August was the largest since September 1993, when it was 14.1 percent.
Analysts said the July data were revised down to 641,000 from 664,000 and that may have made the August increase look more impressive than it was. The South, a key region for growth, fell 4 percent.
Stuart G. Hoffman, chief economist for the PNC Bank Corporation in Pittsburgh, yesterday regarded new-home sales as the number with the greatest market impact, "for at least today."
Mr. Hoffman noted that the market did not like the home sales number since it rose despite higher mortgage rates this summer. And it was contrary to what home builders had been saying -- that traffic was down and that mortgage applications were down.
"The market was interpreting that the strong homes sales number could hasten the day the Fed will raise the funds rate," he said.
Ms. Schaja thought the home sales suggested that the five interest rate increases enacted by the Fed since the beginning of the year may not be enough to stabilize the housing sector.
While traders have been calculating how soon the Fed will move again, the home sales may cause the market to start thinking a rate rise could occur before the November meeting of the Federal Open Market Committee, the Fed's policy-making arm. "But not before the release of the employment report, inflation data and retail sales for September due out by mid-October," Mr. Hoffman said. He said he expected that the Fed would want to see and digest the most recent data before deciding.
In addition, the Fed will watch the dollar and commodity prices, including the price of gold on a daily basis, as companion elements to the monthly economic numbers.

HEADLINE: Growth Alone Won't Assure Jobs, I.M.F. Tells Rich Lands

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MADRID, Sept. 29

 BODY:
A sustained world economic recovery will not significantly lower high unemployment rates in the industrial nations unless their economies become more flexible and open to market forces, the top official of the International Monetary Fund said today.
Michel Camdessus, the fund's managing director, said at a news conference here that "inflation could still destroy the recovery" unless major countries devised an appropriate strategy for prolonging the current economic upswing. 
 Mr. Camdessus spoke as finance ministers from around the world converged in Madrid for an annual meeting that also marks the 50th anniversary of the Bretton Woods agreement that created the I.M.F. and the World Bank.
For the first time in this decade, the monetary fund has raised its economic growth forecast for the industrial nations, and declared the recent period of recession and slow growth over.
In its latest World Economic Outlook, compiled every six months, the fund predicts that the industrial economies will grow at an annual rate of 2.7 percent this year and in 1995, compared with its previous forecast of 2.4 percent and 2.6 percent for those years.
The world economy as a whole is now expected to grow 3.1 percent this year and 3.6 percent next year, returning to the average growth rate since 1973.
Mr. Camdessus said he would ask the finance ministers to approve a package of new measures that would include the creation of a further $50 billion worth of special drawing rights -- S.D.R.'s in international financial shorthand. The certificates, which supplement gold and hard-currency reserves, were first issued by the International Monetary Fund in 1970.
Almost half of that would be set aside for former Communist and developing countries that are short of hard-currency reserves.
British and American monetary officials said they would press for a more modest allocation of new S.D.R.'s, which would still favor countries short of cash. The United States, Britain, Germany and Japan maintain that there are adequate reserves worldwide, and fear that issuing new S.D.R.'s would stoke inflation.
They agree that something should be done to increase the monetary reserves of the former Communist countries, which have recently joined the I.M.F. These nations were not members when drawing rights were last issued in 1983.
Britain has begun a separate initiative here to ease the debt burdens of the very poorest countries by selling some of the $40 billion worth of gold now owned by the monetary fund. The money raised from the sale of the gold would be invested and the income used to finance easier repayment terms for those countries' foreign debts.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Copying Stocks and Bonds, Dollar Edges Downward

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar edged mostly lower against other leading currencies yesterday, weakened by declines in the American stock and bond markets.
The dollar's value also eroded because of persistent anxiety over whether the United States and Japan would reconcile their key trade differences by a weekend deadline. 
 Traders attributed much of the dollar's weakness to declines in stock and bond prices, which resulted from rising fear of inflation in an ebullient economy. Drops in the stock and bond markets signal weaker demand for dollars from foreign investors.
The dollar fell to 98.58 yen in late New York trading yesterday, from 98.96 yen on Wednesday. Currency dealers described trading as light ahead of tomorrow's expected announcement as to whether the United States and Japan have reached any meaningful agreement in their trade disputes.
"It's just a waiting game right now," said Earl I. Johnson, a vice president at Harris Trust and Savings Bank in Chicago. "Many people think it's going to go down to the midnight deadline." The Clinton Administration has threatened to impose sanctions against Japan unless the two sides reach an agreement by midnight tonight.
Traders were "a little less optimistic" about prospects for an agreement, Mr. Johnson said.
Many dealers believe that without a breakthrough on trade, the United States will push to make the dollar even weaker, which would hurt Japan's exporters by making their products more expensive in the American market.
The dollar was also weaker against the German mark yesterday, falling to 1.5485 marks from 1.5527 on Wednesday.
In other currency trading in New York yesterday, the dollar was quoted at 1.2855 Swiss francs, down from 1.2876 on Wednesday; at 5.2852 French francs, down from 5.2930, and 1,557 Italian lire, down from 1,561. The British pound rose to $1.5780 in New York, from $1.5740 on Wednesday.

LENGTH: 993 words

HEADLINE: HOCKEY;
Face-Off or Batter Up: 2 Sports With Similar Objectives in Mind

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
Hockey and baseball terminology don't mix. In baseball, to cite a couple of examples, players use a ball and bat and score runs; in hockey, they use a puck and a stick and score goals. But labor disputes have brought the lexicons of the two sports closer together. Negotiations have created terminology common to both. Try tax plans and disincentives, for example.
The National Hockey League is on the brink of joining major league baseball in its shutdown mode. Unless a last-minute agreement alters the league's plans, Commissioner Gary Bettman plans to lock out the players by postponing tomorrow's scheduled start of the 1994-95 season. Baseball players have been on strike for 50 days, matching the length of the 1981 strike, the longest work stoppage in professional sports history. 
 Like their baseball counterparts, the N.H.L. and its union are discussing tax proposals as a means of creating a revenue-sharing pool to be used to bolster the sagging finances of lower-revenue teams. Like their baseball counterparts, they can't agree on the type of tax plan or the rate of tax to be applied to team payrolls.
There is one difference. While the N.H.L. has placed its tax plan on the bargaining table and even has changed its proposal once or twice, baseball's club owners have talked about a payroll tax only in private conversations with union officials and player agents. They have not made a formal proposal.
They have refrained from taking that step because they then would lose the option of unilaterally implementing their coveted salary-cap proposal if they decide to declare an impasse in their negotiations. When an employer declares an impasse and can legally defend it, he can implement only his last proposal.
But the managements of both sports acknowledge that their proposals provide elements that would serve as disincentives for teams to give players the kind of escalating salaries they have created, though more recently in hockey than in baseball.
In the last formal bargaining session in the baseball dispute, three weeks ago today, Stan Kasten, president of the Atlanta Braves, said the owners received a clear message from the union. "We were told that any feature that resulted in disincentives to signing would not be acceptable," Kasten said.
In one of his first comments at a news conference in Toronto yesterday, Bob Goodenow, the hockey players' labor leader, said, "We're definitely opposed to a system that imposes limits on players' earnings."
But it's the idea of a limit on salaries that sends the terminology of the two sports into divergence once again, at least in the view of N.H.L. officials. Despite the union's public position to the contrary, hockey officials say their proposal is not a salary cap.
"A cap is a prohibition on spending and a prohibition on player acquisition and, in some cases, a prohibition on player retention," Jeffrey Pash, the N.H.L.'s senior vice president and general counsel, said in an interview yesterday. "This system is none of those. Our proposal to the players explicitly states that there is no limit on what a team can pay a player or what it can have for its payroll."
League officials also say that, privately, a union official has said the union is satisfied that the league's owners do not seek a cap. But at his news conference, Goodenow said, "We find their proposal to be a cap on salaries." Speaking by telephone from Toronto last night, and specifically addressing the view of league officials, he said, "We have not indicated that to them."
Baseball management's proposal calls for the players to receive 50 percent of the clubs' revenues, which would be translated into an average club payroll that could be exceeded only by 10 percent. It is a system similar to the one that exists in the National Basketball Association and the National Football League.
"We're doing something different from the other sports," Pash said. "Our latest proposal basically cuts in half the amount of tax."
The hockey owners initially proposed a system that would have created a two-tier tax. If a team exceeded the average payroll by any amount up to 10 percent, it would pay into the revenue-sharing pool an equal amount, $1 for $1. For any excess beyond 10 percent, the tax would rise from 100 percent to 200 percent, $2 for every $1.
The proposal now on the table still centers on an average payroll, which is called the deductible. For each $250,000 above the deductible, a club would be assessed a 5 percent tax, up to a maximum of 125 percent for the excessive payroll amount.
As an example, the Rangers had a 1993-94 payroll of $20,172,000, a shade under Detroit's league-leading $20.2 million. The deductible would have been $14 million, the average payroll. The Rangers would have exceeded that figure by $6,172,000, making their "marginal contribution rate" the full 125 percent.
Their so-called contribution would have amounted to $3,965,000, or 19.7 percent of their total payroll. League officials say theoretically no club would be assessed more than 20 percent, but they acknowledge a higher rate could emerge depending on a team's payroll and the deductible.
Jerry Hausman, an economist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology and a consultant to the union, told the players that the system, with its many tax brackets and its steeply graduated rates, would give teams the "incentive both to cut their taxes dramatically by slashing salaries and to release veteran players."
The result, Hausman said, would mean little money raised for low-revenue clubs. The tax plans, he added, are "not intended to raise a pool of money for small-market clubs but they will have essentially the same effect as a salary cap."
The union, meanwhile, has proposed taxing payrolls and home gate receipts of the 16 highest-revenue clubs. Both taxes would be 5.5 percent and, the union says, they would raise about $40 million for low-revenue clubs.

SECTION: Section C; Page 30; Column 1; Weekend Desk 

LENGTH: 2343 words

HEADLINE: CRITIC'S NOTEBOOK;
In Video Films, a More Intimate 'Reality'

BYLINE: By STEPHEN HOLDEN 

 BODY:
Although the experimental film world has produced thousands of quirky personal statements, it is probably safe to say that nothing quite like Obitani Yuri's video movie "The Hair Opera" has been seen before. The one-hour work, which is to have two showings next week as part of the Third New York Video Festival opening today at Lincoln Center, consists of this Japanese artist's filmed correspondence with a fellow artist, Yosufu Kosuzu, who incorporates the pubic hairs of men she has slept with into her work.
So love-smitten that he periodically breaks into soaring operatic arias, Mr. Yuri woos Ms. Kosuzu in a series of ardent 8-millimeter filmed declarations of his passion in which he confesses his obsession with her body hair. He even provides her with her own 8-millimeter camera so she can respond to his messages. After several replies from Ms. Kosuzu, who dismisses him as a pervert, he breaks into her apartment while she is asleep, wakes her up and eventually wins her affection along with permission to shave parts of her body for the camera. 
 Considering that it is a crude home movie, "The Hair Opera" is a surprisingly rich work. It blends comedy, romance, musical parody, sexual politics and esthetics into an endearing love story, which despite its kinkiness ends up seeming positively wholesome.
How much of "The Hair Opera" is autobiographical and how much is fiction is impossible to gauge. The movie's technological crudeness lends it a cinema verite authenticity that would be missing in a more sophisticated film, so the viewer is tempted to take it as a filmed event of real-life performance art. But what does that mean exactly?
 
Offbeat and Oddball
 Errol Morris's "Interrotron Stories," which is to be shown tomorrow and Sunday, raises similar questions. The program consists of three episodes of an "Unsolved Mysteries"-style television series. Made with a device called the interrotron, which allows a subject to look into the camera and into the interviewer's eyes simultaneously, it weaves dramatic re-creations and interviews into a deadpan spoof of reality-based television.
Is the California murder case described in the episode "Parrot May Have the Answer to a Killing" an actual event? Probably not. But the story of a parrot who witnessed a murder and keeps repeating an incriminating protestation by the victim has just enough credibility to make you wonder.
The festival, which continues at the Walter Reade Theater through Oct. 8, includes enough of this sort of oddball work that it qualifies as the avant-garde arm of the New York Film Festival, of which it is a stepchild. Work after work suggests that the video movie, no matter how closely related it may seem to mainstream films, is a much more personal medium that inspires a kind of trust in its content that conventional movies rarely do. Although it's tempting to assert that if movies are dreams, then video is reality, it is never that simple.
Peggy Ahwesh and Margie Strosser's video "Strange Weather," which focuses on the lives of four crack addicts -- three young women and a man -- who share an apartment in Miami, is another entry that has a strong feel of documentary reality. As the characters ramble on about their chemical and sexual adventures, and whine over the phone to their parents for more drug money, the video compiles a devastating picture of young lives adrift that recalls Andy Warhol's "Chelsea Girls." Contributing to the ominous atmosphere are actual television news reports about the approach of Hurricane Andrew.
But although "Strange Weather" looks and feels like a documentary home movie, it is really fiction. It is also the most substantial of three short videos being shown in the program "The Philosophical Toy: Pixelvision Triumphant," which is to be shown on Oct. 6 and 8. All three videos in the program use scenes shot with a plastic video camera for children that has only one-tenth of the number of dots as a regular television. The video's coarse, grainy look and lack of focus add to its aura of realism because everything that is shown looks and sounds unplanned and undoctored.
 
For High-Definition TV
 By no means are all of the works being shown as odd as "The Hair Opera," "Interrotron Stories" and "Strange Weather." On the other side of the technological coin, the festival is presenting a program, "Turbo Video: High-Definition Television" (Oct. 7 and 8), featuring four short videos made for high-definition television. The most spectacular is Kit Fitzgerald's "Painted Memories, Spider's Garden," a ravishingly beautiful visual tone poem, with music by Peter Gordon, which explores relationships between painting and nature with a rhapsodic sensuousness.
Also on the "Turbo Video" program: Mark Rappaport, who deconstructed scenes in Rock Hudson movies to uncover their gay subtext in his witty documentary "Rock Hudson's Home Movies," turns his attention to film noir in "Exterior Night," a much more formal investigation of Hollywood film iconography. The work's most technically striking moments are sequences in which contemporary actors shot in color appear to step out of scenes from old black-and-white detective films.
 
By Jean-Luc Godard
 In between these technological poles are everything from straightforward serious documentaries to weird fun and games. Amos Gitai's "In the Land of Oranges" is the first chapter of a four-hour documentary examining Israeli and Palestinian reactions to the recent peace agreements. Much of the work focuses on workers in a kibbutz that employs Palestinian laborers. The jumble of conflicting passions and economic problems does not offer an encouraging view of future Arab-Israeli relations.
Among the festival's most challenging works is Jean-Luc Godard's many-layered video collage "The Children Play Russian." This meditation on movie history and esthetics, and the crumbling of the Iron Curtain includes many witty images of the stampede of Western mass culture and commercialism into contemporary Russia. It devotes special attention to "Anna Karenina" and its metaphoric meanings both for the director and for Russian society.
As wildly dissimilar as many of its parts may be in style and intention, the festival makes a strong case for the video movie as an art form with a markedly different set of esthetics from mainstream movies. Where film confers a kind of monumentality on its subjects, the video movie tends to reduce whatever it shows to everyday size. That reduction is partly to do with television's traditional associations with living-room reality. For nearly 50 years, television has been a part of the family. But it also has to do with scale. Once a face is blown up into a larger-than-life image on celluloid, it loses its everyday qualities.
But why, even when blown up, do video images not seem especially mythic? Although their usually lower visual resolution is one reason, more important is the relationship between artist and technology. What we think of as movies are large-scale collaborations in which every shot is carefully planned, set up and filmed in a laborious process that entails shooting take after take.
The video movie is not a collective vision but a personal home movie shot with equipment that is portable and that can poke into places a stationary 35-millimeter movie camera cannot readily go. Judging from works shown in the festival, the do-it-yourself technology of video making is having the same impact on cinema that portable, easily programmed musical instruments have had on pop music. The easier it is to make, the more personal and spontaneous its tone.
But does more personal and more spontaneous guarantee greater realism or truthfulness? Superficially, yes. You don't need makeup and fancy lighting to shoot a documentary or home movie. But as this year's Video Festival makes abundantly clear, video movie "reality" is so easy to fake that it really is one more captivating illusion in an artistic hall of mirrors.
 
What Videos Are Playing and When to See Them
 The Third New York Video Festival sponsored by the New York Film Society of Lincoln Center is to run through Oct. 8 at the Walter Reade Theater, 165 West 65th Street, Manhattan. The festival features 17 programs of videos and three evenings of discussions; in many cases, the video makers are to be present at the screenings. Tickets to each program cost $7.50; $5 for members of the Film Society, and $4 for the elderly at weekday matinees. Unless noted, videos were made this year. Ticket availability: (212) 875-5610. SECTION: Section A; Page 30; Column 1; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 604 words

HEADLINE: The C.I.A. Club Needs a Cleanup

 BODY:
Surely Congress has had enough of evasion and self-protection from the Central Intelligence Agency. Yet there was the C.I.A.'s Director, James Woolsey, telling the House Intelligence Committee on Wednesday that he had let those responsible for one of the agency's worst security disasters off lightly because it was all the fault of some vague "systemic failure." Not one of those who allowed Aldrich Ames to hold a variety of sensitive posts while spying for Moscow was fired or demoted.
Mr. Woolsey's failure to clean house underscores the need for an independent commission to examine the C.I.A. and other intelligence agencies and to rethink their roles. The C.I.A. itself is clearly incapable of the job. 
 In his go-easy-on-the-culprits crackdown, Mr. Woolsey sent letters of reprimand to 11 officials touched by the Ames case. Six had already retired and one is about to. The four that remain may simply have their next promotions delayed. Such high-level officials as Ted Price, director of clandestine operations, and Burton Gerber, Deputy Inspector General, will keep their jobs.
So now the same clubby culture that was blamed for letting Mr. Ames flourish at the C.I.A. despite obvious signs of his deceit and unreliability has shown comparable leniency toward his negligent superiors. Senator Dennis DeConcini, chairman of the Select Committee on Intelligence, is right that it will take "dramatic reorganization to change the culture, the good old boys' club" that promoted Mr. Ames and gave him sensitive posts.
The best hope for a thorough review lies with a new commission being established by Congress. But with the White House hesitant and the C.I.A. downright hostile, it is disconcerting to learn that Senator John Warner, who first conceived of the commission as a way to blunt criticism of the C.I.A., remains confident it would not "slash and burn" the agency. Some slashing may be just what's needed, as Senator DeConcini's comments suggest.
Disbanding the agency is not realistically on the agenda. If the U.S. did not have a C.I.A. it would have to invent one -- to coordinate intelligence-gathering and analysis and perform more disinterested assessments than the armed services and the State Department.
However, the commission could usefully assess not only the agency's internal culture but also what changes are needed in its missions now that the cold war is over. That will require looking at the entire intelligence apparatus. The C.I.A.'s $3-billion budget is but a fraction of the $28 billion a year the U.S. spends on intelligence.
Rival agencies do almost everything the C.I.A. does. The State and Defense Departments collect and assess intelligence. The armed services run covert operations. The F.B.I. has expanded its role in counterintelligence. Other agencies operate spy satellites and process what they photograph and overhear. That makes a turf war likely. But if the commission merely redraws bureaucratic boundaries, it will have failed.
The commission needs to reorient U.S. intelligence for the next century by answering basic questions: Should the U.S. spend more on spies or rely primarily on open sources like press reports and broadcasts? Does it need all the spy satellites it now has? Should the State Department be given responsibility for political and economic assessments? Could these tasks be farmed out to think tanks and academic contractors? Does the U.S. need all the covert operatives it now has?
Above all, the commission should start with the premise that the C.I.A. and other intelligence agencies need correction, not coddling.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: CUOMO CRITICIZES PATAKI TAX PLAN

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO 

 BODY:
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo said yesterday that State Senator George E. Pataki's plan to slash income taxes would inevitably force the state to cut aid to New York City and other local governments, in turn causing local officials to raise property taxes "through the roof" to pay for the lost assistance.
Mr. Cuomo's comments seemed aimed partly at driving a wedge between Mr. Pataki, the Republican candidate for governor, and local political leaders, particularly New York City's Mayor, Rudolph W. Giuliani, a Republican who has withheld any endorsement of Mr. Pataki. 
 Yesterday, Mr. Giuliani echoed the Democratic Governor's concerns that deep tax cuts could force local governments to either raise local taxes or cut services. But he said he might support the tax cuts if Mr. Pataki could show him how the state would compensate for the lost revenue by reducing its many demands on local governments. Those demands include housing the homeless and paying some health care costs for the poor.
In imposing that condition, Mr. Giuliani pushed any endorsement of Mr. Pataki further into the future since the Mayor said Mr. Pataki had yet to provide that explanation.
In presenting his tax cut plan, which includes reducing the top tax rate by 25 percent in four years, Mr. Pataki said the cuts could be paid for through a combination of revenue from a growing economy and some belt-tightening by the state. No cuts in aid to local governments would be needed, he said.
But yesterday, Mr. Cuomo, joined by some economists and Wall Street financial analysts, greeted Mr. Pataki's assumptions with deep skepticism. They said that even if his projections for 6 percent annual revenue growth proved correct, steep annual increases in an array of government programs would make a painless cut in spending impossible. They said deep cuts in aid to local governments, which makes up about 70 percent of the $34.2 billion state budget, would be virtually unavoidable if the Pataki tax cuts occurred.
"This is a Republican by the name of George telling you to read my lips," Mr. Cuomo said at a campaign stop in Buffalo. "I mean, how many times do they have to do it to you?"
Mr. Cuomo has proposed a far smaller package of tax cuts worth $1.5 billion a year. He said he would cut income taxes and a state utilities tax but has yet to release the details.
Felix G. Rohatyn, a Cuomo ally and former chairman of the Municipal Assistance Corporation, said the Pataki plan "could have a truly devastating impact" on New York City's precarious financial condition, as well as on the state's.
Mr. Pataki said that even with his cuts, which would reduce state revenues by $5.6 billion a year by 1998, the state budget could be balanced by holding state spending to the rate of inflation, about 3 percent this year. Yesterday, his campaign also released a list of possible spending reductions, including consolidating agencies, not filling one-third of all vacant state jobs, reducing services for prisoners and reducing supply purchases, which were estimated would save $823 million a year.
"We will not cut local assistance, we will not impose new mandates on local governments that we don't pay for and we will not in any way cut state taxes by shifting the burden either to the mayor, the county executives, to local governments or to schools," Mr. Pataki said in Syracuse yesterday.
Several economists and Wall Street analysts said that making cuts in the state bureaucracy alone could not pay for Mr. Pataki's tax cuts because most of the state's spending is aid to local governments for schools, Medicaid and welfare. Those programs have traditionally risen at rates faster than 3 percent a year; Medicaid alone has been at double-digit rates for years.
"Unless some way is developed to not simply increase the local burden, what you're going to have is tax shifting from the state to localities," Peter D. Salins, a senior fellow at the Manhattan Institute, said on New York 1 News last night. "It will make the state look better, but it will make the localities look worse."
Michael Brooks, senior municipal credit analyst for Sanford C. Bernstein & Company, said Mr. Pataki's plan, if approved by the State Legislature, might jeopardize the state's ability to improve its bond rating, the lowest in the nation. "If you make a tax cut of this magnitude, you're going to adversely affect the credit of the state," he said.
The proposal also came under fire from a group representing mayors, which said income tax cuts in the 1980's led to cutbacks in state aid to local governments totaling $1.9 billion. It said those aid reductions had caused local governments to raise property taxes by an average of 10 percent each year for the last five years to cover their rising costs.
"One must not forget that the last cut in state income taxes was financed on the backs of local governments," said Edward C. Farrell, executive director of the New York State Conference of Mayors.
Under attack, the Pataki camp found itself on the defensive yesterday in explaining how its candidate could finance the proposed tax cut. When the plan was announced on Wednesday, Mr. Pataki's campaign aides produced a document that the $5.6 billion tax cut would be paid for out of $11 billion in future savings.
But yesterday, that cushion disappeared as the experts who worked on the plan conceded that their official projections would barely produce the $5.6 billion needed and might even fall short of that.
Democrats and outside fiscal analysts said there was little evidence supporting Mr. Pataki's proposal. Frank Mauro, director of the Fiscal Policy Institute, a labor-supported group that often opposes tax cuts, said that the assumptions underlying the plan were deeply flawed and that the plan would leave the state government at least $2 billion short if it were fully in effect in 1998.
Republican officials on Long Island, where support for Mr. Pataki runs strong, also raised concerns about his tax plan.
"I have to be cautious, as do my colleagues, that reductions in state taxes are not a charade, in the sense that they pass those tax burdens along to our local real-property-tax payers," said Thomas S. Gulotta, the Nassau County executive.
Herbert London, the Republican candidate for state comptroller, expressed similar concerns. But Mr. London, who had criticized Mr. Pataki for not releasing the plan earlier, praised his proposals yesterday as "significant breakthroughs."
In campaigning, Mr. Pataki has talked of not just cutting the personal income tax but also eliminating a business tax, known as the corporate surcharge, next year. But yesterday, in an apparent shift, his campaign said he could not promise to eliminate the surcharge in 1995. The tax is scheduled to be phased out by 1997.
"He has said he wants to eliminate it next year, but his priority is to do the personal income tax," said Zenia Mucha, Mr. Pataki's campaign spokeswoman.

CORRECTION-DATE: October 3, 1994, Monday

 CORRECTION:
A chart on Friday about State Senator George E. Pataki's proposal to cut New York's state income tax misstated one example of the tax prospects under existing law. After the last stage of a tax reduction that is to be completed by April 1, 1996, a taxpayer earning $13,000 would get a refund of $590, not $509.


GRAPHIC: Thomas J. Marine, a partner at Price Waterhouse who heads the firm's Multistate Tax Consulting Practice, compared what families at a number of income levels would pay under State Senator George E. Pataki's tax cut proposals with what they pay now. The result would be an across-the-board tax cut, he said, because it is not a complex plan.
Graph: "CLOSE-UP: What Pataki's Tax Cut Would Mean"
 Senator Pataki is "basically dropping the rates" and going from five or six brackets to two, he said, and giving tax credits of $200 per dependent. That amounts to $120 to $160 of tax savings, compared with the current $1,000 deduction per dependent, he said, so big families would realize a substantial savings under the Pataki plan.
 Credits reduce taxes dollar for dollar, while deductions reduce income and the actual dollar savings depend on the tax bracket. A person in the top New York bracket would save $78.75 with a $1,000 deduction.
 Table shows how Pataki's plan would affect the taxes of various gross income groups. (pg. B6)

PUB-DATE: September 30, 1994

SECTION: Section D; Page 1; Column 6; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1365 words

HEADLINE: Economy Is Strong, Data Show

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
Fresh signs of persistent economic vigor emerged today as the Government published upbeat reports on housing and employment, while raising for a second time its estimate of total output during the spring.
Another official report showed stepped-up inflation for imported goods, reflecting, in part, the decline of the dollar. 
 Taken together, the various figures fanned fears that overly rapid growth could revive inflation, and could lead the Federal Reserve to raise short-term interest rates again, perhaps after next week's monthly job report for September, to slow the economy down.
"It looks like the economy's snapping back," said Joshua N. Feinman, an economist at Bankers Trust. He added that the Fed's inaction on rates when it met to consider raising them two days ago could prove short-lived.
Although none of today's reports was particularly compelling on its own, the combined force -- all in the same direction -- was enough to push interest rates higher and thereby depress the stock market.
"The system, I think, is generating economic growth that is still well above the 2 1/2 percent that is supposed to be the trend" over the long term, said Richard Aspinwall, chief economist at Chase Manhattan.
First-time claims for unemployment benefits, which analysts had expected to rise a bit, fell 11,000 last week to 310,000, the lowest level of the year, the Labor Department reported.
More importantly, this fit a pattern of unmistakable improvement in recent months; the four-week claims average eased to 322,000 for the period ended on Saturday.
The buoyant labor market, analysts said, has helped blunt the impact that rising interest rates have had on housing. Conventional mortgages command about two percentage points more than they did at their lows last autumn.
Today, the Departemtns of Commerce andHousing and Urban Development reported that sales of new single-family homes climbed 9.7 percent in August, paced by a huge, although statistically suspect, jump in the Northeast.
This advance, the strongest since September 1993, was also better than expected and included gains in all four major regions but the South. Sales were revised downward for July, but that revision was nearly offset by an upward adjustment for June.
The increase in the Northeast was an eye-popping 82.1 percent, but this was from a much lower level than other regions, and analysts said it was undoubtedly a fluke.
Nationally, sales were 703,000 at a seasonally adjusted annual rate, with 102,000 in the Northeast (up from 56,000), 117,000 in the Midwest (up from 113,000), 217,000 in the West (up from 194,000) and 267,000 in the South (down from 278,000).
The report on gross domestic product showed output of goods and services expanded at an annual rate of 4.1 percent in the second quarter. A month ago the pace was thought to have been 3.8 percent; the initial estimate, made in late July, was 3.7 percent.
The biggest factor in the latest update was higher business inventories, much of it farmers' holdings of cattle. But while the second quarter is now seen to have been eight-tenths of a point stronger than the January-March period, when the growth rate was 3.3 percent, analysts note that much of the strength consisted of goods produced but not yet sold. This implies that there will be a significantly lower gain, perhaps only half as much, in the quarter that ends Friday, as inventories are worked off.
The fixed-weight price index for gross domestic purchases was revised back to the 3.2 percent inflation rate that was initially reported in July. Last month it had ticked up to 3.3 percent.
But evidence mounted today that inflation has picked up in recent months. The Labor Department found that the price of imported goods other than oil surged six-tenths of 1 percent for the second straight month, double the average advance during the period of March through June.
This partly reflected the dollar's decline in world currency markets, which means that more American currency is needed to buy a quantity of foreign goods or services than previously.
The American Business Conference, a group of chief executives at middle-sized companies, predicted today that the final three months of the year would be marked by "continued growth, although at a slower pace and accompanied by early warning signs of inflation."
And a survey of 3,000 executives by the Dun & Bradstreet Corporation found that prospects for higher sales, profits and hiring during the fourth quarter "decreased moderately" with economic growth "one notch slower by the end of the year."
Today's housing report showed that builders' inventories of new homes would last 5.7 months at the August sales pace, down from 6 months at the end of July.
The median new home price for last month's sales was $134,000, the departments found, up from $125,000 in July. This, analysts cautioned, undoubtedly reflected a change in the mix of homes changing hands, as well as price appreciation.
